########## J.13 Equal Employment Opportunity.pdf ##########

THE LAW
Equal Employment Opportunity is

Private Employers, State and Local Governments, Educational Institutions, Employment Agencies and Labor Organizations �
Applicants to and employees of most private employers, state and local governments, educational institutions,
employment agencies and labor organizations are protected under Federal law from discrimination on the following bases: �

RACE, COLOR, RELIGION, SEX, NATIONAL ORIGIN
Title VII of the Civil Rights Act of 1964, as amended, protects applicants and
employees from discrimination in hiring, promotion, discharge, pay, fringe benefits,
job training, classification, referral, and other aspects of employment, on the basis
of race, color, religion, sex (including pregnancy), or national origin. Religious
discrimination includes failing to reasonably accommodate an employee’s religious
practices where the accommodation does not impose undue hardship.

GENETICS
Title II of the Genetic Information Nondiscrimination Act of 2008 protects applicants
and employees from discrimination based on genetic information in hiring,
promotion, discharge, pay, fringe benefits, job training, classification, referral, and
other aspects of employment. GINA also restricts employers’ acquisition of genetic
information and strictly limits disclosure of genetic information. Genetic information
includes information about genetic tests of applicants, employees, or their family
members; the manifestation of diseases or disorders in family members (family
medical history); and requests for or receipt of genetic services by applicants,
employees, or their family members.

DISABILITY
Title I and Title V of the Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990, as amended, protect
qualified individuals from discrimination on the basis of disability in hiring, promotion,
discharge, pay, fringe benefits, job training, classification, referral, and other
RETALIATION
aspects of employment. Disability discrimination includes not making reasonable
All of these Federal laws prohibit covered entities from retaliating against a
accommodation to the known physical or mental limitations of an otherwise qualified person who files a charge of discrimination, participates in a discrimination
individual with a disability who is an applicant or employee, barring undue hardship. proceeding, or other wise opposes an unlawful employment practice.
AGE
The Age Discrimination in Employment Act of 1967, as amended, protects
applicants and employees 40 years of age or older from discrimination based on
age in hiring, promotion, discharge, pay, fringe benefits, job training, classification,
referral, and other aspects of employment.
SEX (WAGES)
In addition to sex discrimination prohibited by Title VII of the Civil Rights Act, as
amended, the Equal Pay Act of 1963, as amended, prohibits sex discrimination in
the payment of wages to women and men performing substantially equal work,
in jobs that require equal skill, effort, and responsibility, under similar working
conditions, in the same establishment.

WHAT TO DO IF YOU BELIEVE DISCRIMINATION HAS OCCURRED
There are strict time limits for filing charges of employment discrimination. To
preserve the ability of EEOC to act on your behalf and to protect your right to file a
private lawsuit, should you ultimately need to, you should contact EEOC promptly
when discrimination is suspected:
The U.S. Equal Employment Opportunity Commission (EEOC), 1-800-669-4000
(toll-free) or 1-800-669-6820 (toll-free TTY number for individuals with hearing
impairments). EEOC field office information is available at www.eeoc.gov or
in most telephone directories in the U.S. Government or Federal Government
section. Additional information about EEOC, including information about charge
filing, is available at www.eeoc.gov.

Employers Holding Federal Contracts or Subcontracts
Applicants to and employees of companies with a Federal government contract or subcontract
are protected under Federal law from discrimination on the following bases:
RACE, COLOR, RELIGION, SEX, NATIONAL ORIGIN
Executive Order 11246, as amended, prohibits job discrimination on the basis
of race, color, religion, sex or national origin, and requires affirmative action to
ensure equality of opportunity in all aspects of employment.
INDIVIDUALS WITH DISABILITIES
Section 503 of the Rehabilitation Act of 1973, as amended, protects qualified
individuals from discrimination on the basis of disability in hiring, promotion,
discharge, pay, fringe benefits, job training, classification, referral, and
other aspects of employment. Disability discrimination includes not making
reasonable accommodation to the known physical or mental limitations of an
otherwise qualified individual with a disability who is an applicant or employee,
barring undue hardship. Section 503 also requires that Federal contractors take
affirmative action to employ and advance in employment qualified individuals
with disabilities at all levels of employment, including the executive level.
DISABLED, RECENTLY SEPARATED, OTHER PROTECTED,
AND ARMED FORCES SERVICE MEDAL VETERANS
The Vietnam Era Veterans’ Readjustment Assistance Act of 1974, as amended, 38
U.S.C. 4212, prohibits job discrimination and requires affirmative action to employ
and advance in employment disabled veterans, recently separated veterans (within

three years of discharge or release from active duty), other protected veterans
(veterans who served during a war or in a campaign or expedition for which a
campaign badge has been authorized), and Armed Forces service medal veterans
(veterans who, while on active duty, participated in a U.S. military operation for
which an Armed Forces service medal was awarded).
RETALIATION
Retaliation is prohibited against a person who files a complaint of discrimination,
participates in an OFCCP proceeding, or otherwise opposes discrimination
under these Federal laws.
Any person who believes a contractor has violated its nondiscrimination or
affirmative action obligations under the authorities above should contact
immediately:
The Office of Federal Contract Compliance Programs (OFCCP), U.S.
Department of Labor, 200 Constitution Avenue, N.W., Washington, D.C.
20210, 1-800-397-6251 (toll-free) or (202) 693-1337 (TTY). OFCCP may also be
contacted by e-mail at OFCCP-Public@dol.gov, or by calling an OFCCP regional
or district office, listed in most telephone directories under U.S. Government,
Department of Labor.

Programs or Activities Receiving Federal Financial Assistance
RACE, COLOR, NATIONAL ORIGIN, SEX
In addition to the protections of Title VII of the Civil Rights Act of 1964, as
amended, Title VI of the Civil Rights Act of 1964, as amended, prohibits
discrimination on the basis of race, color or national origin in programs or
activities receiving Federal financial assistance. Employment discrimination
is covered by Title VI if the primary objective of the financial assistance is
provision of employment, or where employment discrimination causes or may
cause discrimination in providing services under such programs. Title IX of the
Education Amendments of 1972 prohibits employment discrimination on the
basis of sex in educational programs or activities which receive Federal financial
assistance.
EEOC 9/02 and OFCCP 8/08 Versions Useable With 11/09 Supplement

INDIVIDUALS WITH DISABILITIES
Section 504 of the Rehabilitation Act of 1973, as amended, prohibits employment
discrimination on the basis of disability in any program or activity which receives
Federal financial assistance. Discrimination is prohibited in all aspects of
employment against persons with disabilities who, with or without reasonable
accommodation, can perform the essential functions of the job.
If you believe you have been discriminated against in a program of any
institution which receives Federal financial assistance, you should immediately
contact the Federal agency providing such assistance.

EEOC-P/E-1 (Revised 11/09)



########## J.14 USCP Veh Del Proc Ref Gde (5-08).pdf ##########

United States Capitol Police
Vehicle Delivery Procedures Reference Guide
Effective May 1, 2008
Off-site Delivery Center (OSDC)
Hours of Operation
Monday thru Friday
0500 - 1400
**Closed Weekends & Holidays**

Delivery Access Points

* All vehicles will be inspected at OSDC
(4700 Shepherd Parkway) prior to
making any delivery on Capitol Grounds.

* Deliveries to the Capitol or Capitol
Visitor Center (CVC) will enter at NJ Ave
& C Street NW. Hours: M-F 0700-1500

* Delivery information must be received
by OSDC prior to arrival.

* Deliveries to the House Office Buildings
will enter at Delaware Avenue SW.
Hours: M-F 0500-1900

* Road restrictions are in effect for all
vehicles with a capacity over 1 and 3
ton.
* Vehicles will proceed directly to their
Delivery Access Point after inspection by

Section L: Instructions to Offeror
Section M: Basis for Award
The Architect of the Capitol (AOC); Supplies, Services, and Material Management Division (SSMMD) is
issuing Request for Proposal (RFP) # AOCSSB26R0023 for Conservation and Stabilization of the Plaster
and Decorative Paint at the President’s Room Ceiling, S-216, United States Capitol Building.
This RFP will potentially result in the award of one (1) contract to the responsible Offeror that is actively
registered in System for Award Management (SAM) and whose proposal provides the overall best value
to the AOC in accordance with the requirements set forth by this solicitation and the Statement of Work
(SOW).
This solicitation will use the procedures in the AOC Contracting Manual, Chapter IX Acquisition Process,
9.1 Negotiated Acquisitions, 9.1.2 Source Selection Process, (a) Tradeoff Process.
Offeror(s) is/are advised the AOC is not subject to the Federal Acquisition Regulation (FAR), but follows
the AOC Contracting Manual which can be found here: https://www.aoc.gov/sites/default/files/202301/AOC-Order-34-1_Contracting-Manual-2022.pdf
The North American Industry Classification System (NAICS) codes for this requirement is 541990 All
Other Professional, Scientific, and Technical Services. This acquisition does NOT establish a set-aside
and is open to all businesses, regardless of size, that are registered in SAM
The McNamara-O'Hara Service Contract Act (SCA) is not applicable to this procurement due to all
personnel are considered professional roles as per the EAP Exemptions: Individuals in bona fide
Executive, Administrative, or Professional roles.
The contract period of performance will have a 6-month base period from date of award and a 16month option period.
The List of Attachments is in Section J of the RFP.

Section L: Instructions to Offeror
L.1 General Conditions
The submission of a proposal to the Government shall constitute the Offeror’s acknowledgement,
agreement and compliance with the terms and conditions of the RFP. The Government will not be
obligated to pay any cost incurred by a contractor in the preparation and submission of a proposal. The
Offeror is cautioned to ensure that their proposal submissions are complete and reflective of the
evaluation criteria and requirements described herein. The Offeror is encouraged to provide their best
terms with their initial Offeror submission. The Offeror shall not assume that the Government will afford
an opportunity to clarify, discuss, or revise a proposal. The Government reserves the right to allow the
Offerors to address weaknesses and or deficiencies and revise their proposals if it determines that it is in
the best interest of the Government.
Proposals shall be valid for 120 days from the date of Offeror’s submission.
Any exceptions or conditional assumptions taken with respect to the requirements outlined in the RFP
or SOW shall be fully explained in the Offeror’s Introductory Letter. The Government cautions the
Offeror that taking exception to any term or condition may render their proposal non-responsive and
the Offeror will be ineligible for an award unless the RFP expressly authorizes such an exception with
regards to a specific term or condition.
L.2 SITE VISIT, QUESTIONS AND PROPOSAL SUBMISSIONS
All correspondence related to this solicitation, including questions and proposal submittals, shall be
directed only to the Contracting Officer, Troy Dorsey, via e-mail at troy.dorsey@aoc.gov and Contract
Specialist Lily Najera at lily.najera@aoc.gov. E-mail messages shall not exceed 20 MB. If necessary,
offerors shall compress files with a .zip extension.

Section M.1. Basis for Award:
The Government anticipates awarding to the Offeror whose proposal conforms to the solicitation and
represents the best value to the Government, as determined by the evaluation criteria described herein.
The Trade-Off approach will be used in evaluating the Offeror’s proposal and an award will be based on
a detailed evaluation of all factors outlined below.
Evaluations will be conducted in accordance with the procedures defined below. The Government will
first verify the on-time submission of all quotations. The Contracting Officer will verify the proposal
meets the Volume and Organization structure described in Section L.3. Volume I of the proposals that
meet the requirements of Section L.3 will be submitted to the Technical Evaluation Panel (TEP) for
evaluation. The TEP will evaluate each proposal against the evaluation criteria. Each non-price factor
will be assigned a rating in accordance with the Ratings Table in M.2. Once all Volume I proposals are
evaluated, the TEP will evaluate Volume II, Price in accordance with the evaluation criteria for this
factor.
The Government will reserve the right to make an award without discussions if there is a fully
acceptable proposal received in the initial submissions. If the Government decides to conduct
discussions and allow quotation revisions, the Government will allow revisions from Offerors who are
acceptable and above. As an alternative, the Government may elect to form a negotiation range. There
will be no common cutoff dates for resubmissions, and the Government may conduct multiple rounds of
exchanges with Offerors to resolve previously unresolved matters or issues that arise as a result of the
responses without re-engaging with Offerors who are not similarly suited. Once the Government
determines the contractor that is the best-suited (i.e., the apparent successful contractor), the
Government reserves the right to communicate with only that contractor to address any remaining
issues, if necessary, and finalize an award with that contractor. These issues may include technical or
price. The Government also reserves the right to make no award.
Evaluations will be based on a complete assessment of the proposal submission. Proposals will be
evaluated on the following technical factors, listed in descending order of importance, and then the
non-technical factor, Price. The technical factors when combined are considered significantly more
important than price. If two or more proposals are determined not to have any substantial technical
differences (i.e., are technically equivalent), price may become the determining factor and award
may be made to the lower priced proposal.
Non-Price Factors:
Factor 1: Corporate Experience
Factor 2: Technical Approach
Factor 3: Key Personnel
Factor 4: Past Performance
7

Supplies, Services, and Material Management Division
Ford House Office Building, Room H2-263
Washington, DC 20515
202.226.2557
www.aoc.gov

Price Factors:
Factor 5: Price
The evaluation criteria for each factor are as follows:
M.1.1 Factor 1- Corporate Experience:
The Government will evaluate the extent to which the offeror provided three contracts (of similar size,
magnitude, complexity, and scope to this requirement) that demonstrated the contractor’s ability to
satisfactorily provide the requested services and references increase the likelihood of successful
contract performance.
M.1.2 Factor 2- Technical Approach:
The Government will evaluate the extent to which the offeror’s technical approach for items L.3.4 Factor
2 a and b demonstrate an understanding of the scope of work in the SOW, demonstrates a sound,
practical and feasible approach for the scope of work and will satisfy the government’s requirements.
M.1.3 Factor 3- Key Personnel:
The Government will evaluate the extent to which the offeror demonstrates:
a. their ability to provide experienced and qualified staff as outlined in Section 15.0 Key Personnel
of the SOW.
b. the proposed key personnel possess the education, expertise, abilities, and experience
necessary to successfully manage and complete requirements of similar size, magnitude,
complexity, and scope.
M.1.4 Factor 4- Past Performance:
The Government will evaluate PPQs and the Government may also use information obtained from other
sources to evaluate past performance. Contractor Performance Assessment Reporting System (CPARS)
and similar databases. The Government reserves the right to use the additional information
received/retrieved in its evaluation of the offeror’s Past Performance.
The Government will evaluate:
a. The extent to which the Offeror demonstrates their ability to successfully deliver the requested
services of similar size, magnitude, complexity, and scope to this requirement; and

8

Supplies, Services, and Material Management Division
Ford House Office Building, Room H2-263
Washington, DC 20515
202.226.2557
www.aoc.gov

b. The extent to which the Offeror demonstrates their degree of customer satisfaction or evidence
of the same.
Offerors without a record of relevant past performance, or for whom information on past performance
is not available, will not be evaluated favorably or unfavorably, but will instead receive an overall rating
of “neutral.”
M.1.5 Factor 5- Price:
Price will not be rated adjectivally but will be evaluated based on a comprehensive review. The price
proposal will be evaluated with respect to accuracy and completeness based on information submitted
in the offeror’s written submission. This process will involve verification that figures are correctly
calculated, prices are presented in the requested format, and any applicable discounts are accurate.
Additionally, the Government may utilize one or more of the following methods to evaluate price:
• Comparison to other prices received;
• Comparison and/or analysis based on Independent Government Cost Estimate; or
• Comparison/analysis to historical cost or publicized pricing
The offeror’s price proposal will be evaluated for price reasonableness to prevent the Government from
paying too much for the services outlined in the SOW. Additionally, a price realism analysis may be
performed to determine whether a offeror’s price is unrealistically low. An unrealistically low price
proposal may question whether an offeror fully understands the size, magnitude, complexity, and scope
of service outlined in the SOW. An unrealistic price may serve as a rejection of the proposal.
M.2 Non-Price/Technical Adjectival Ratings:
The evaluation of proposals is critically important as this process assesses the Offerors’ proposed
solution and ability to perform. This process includes examining the proposal in detail against the
evaluation factors and criteria set forth in the solicitation and assigning a rating with a supporting
narrative. The technical evaluation process consists of two phases: individual technical evaluations and
the technical consensus. Following each panel member’s individual evaluation, the Chairperson of the
TEP will lead the panel in developing a consensus rating of the proposal based on the ratings in section
M.2. Offerors who rate below Acceptable under Factors 1, 2, and 3 will not be eligible for award and
their price (Factor 5) will not be evaluated.
During the evaluation, the TEP will evaluate proposals for Significant Strengths, Strengths, Weaknesses,
Significant Weaknesses, and Deficiencies. The definitions of these terms are as follows:
Significant Strengths: A strength that appreciably enhances the merit of a proposal or appreciably
increases the probability of successful contract performance.

9

Supplies, Services, and Material Management Division
Ford House Office Building, Room H2-263
Washington, DC 20515
202.226.2557
www.aoc.gov

Strengths: Any aspect of a proposal which exceeds a requirement and increases the probability of
successful performance of the contract.
Weaknesses: A flaw in the proposal that increases the risk of unsuccessful contract performance.
Significant Weaknesses: A weakness that appreciably increases the risk of unsuccessful contract
performance.
Non-Price RATING DEFINITIONS
The Government will evaluate non-price factors 1, 2 and 3 of Volume I using the following
adjectival ratings and definitions scale.
Outstanding

Good

Acceptable

Marginal

Unacceptable

The proposal provides a very clear, comprehensive and detailed
response which meets all requirements and includes significant
strengths with no deficiencies or significant weaknesses. The risk of
unsuccessful performance is very low as the proposal demonstrates a
clear understanding of the requirements and can be expected to result
in outstanding performance.
The proposal provides a sound response which meets all requirements
and includes strengths with no deficiencies and few weaknesses. The
risk of unsuccessful performance is low as the proposal demonstrates
an understanding of the requirements and can be expected to result in
satisfactory performance.
The proposal provides a response which is capable of meeting all
requirements but includes both strengths and weaknesses with no
deficiencies. Strengths are not outweighed by the weaknesses. The risk
of unsuccessful performance is moderate as the proposal demonstrates
a general understanding of the requirements and can be expected to
result in satisfactory performance.
The proposal provides a response which does not meet all
requirements and includes strengths, weaknesses, and/or deficiencies.
Strengths are outweighed by the weaknesses and/or deficiencies. The
risk of unsuccessful performance is high as the proposal does not
demonstrate an understanding of the requirements and can be
expected to result in unsatisfactory performance.
The proposal provides a response that does not meet all requirements
and includes significant weaknesses and/or deficiencies which far
outweigh any strengths. The risk of unsuccessful performance is very
high as the proposal does not demonstrate an understanding of the
requirements and will result in unsatisfactory performance.

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

I. SUMMARY
Between the fall of 2023 and the fall of 2024, Integrated Conservation Resources Inc. was tasked with bringing together
a team to evaluate the condition of the President’s Room plaster ceiling and decorative finishes (S-216). Four prior
reports written between 1994 and 2018 indicated that the ceiling did not have long-term stability based solely on
a relatively simple nondestructive assessment. The goal of this study was to create through documentation of the
room and its current condition and investigate the physical condition of the plaster previously hypothesized through
nondestructive techniques.
ICR’s 2023 campaign included archival research through the AOC Curator’s office; documentation using digital
photogrammetry and 3D scanning; finishes evaluation through onsite inspection, solvent testing for paint removal,
and offsite sample analysis; nondestructive evaluation, including visual inspection, crack mapping, Sounding, Scanning
Laser Doppler Vibrometry, and Surface Penetrating Radar; physical evaluation using Drilling Resistance Measurement
System, and offsite plaster analysis.
The finishes investigation found that the fresco portions of the ceiling are in good condition while the oil painted
architectural trompe’loeil borders displaying blind cleavage and flaking paint. Laboratory analysis confirmed a ra nge
of media on the ceiling including buon fresco, tempera, gilding, and overpainting in oil. It also confirmed the presence
of carbonate and oxalate salts on the surface of both the fresco and overpainted portions of the ceiling but was unable
to identify a source. Subflorescence was n ot observed i n a ny of the fi nishes sa mples. Ov erpaint re veals identified a
product that was successful at exposing original finishes. However, wholesale removal of the overpaint would be time
intensive, and the original surface underneath would need further conservation work.
Nondestructive evaluation of the ceiling using Scanning Laser Doppler Vibrometry and Sounding identified many areas
in the ceiling as high mobility and physical evaluation confirmed the condition of the plaster at discrete locations
in the ceiling through use of Drilling Resistance Measurement System (DRMS). DRMS includes drilling through the
plaster ceiling and up to the brick back-up in order to identify any voids, debonds, or deterioration. Out of 13 carefully
selected testing locations, 6 locations displayed detachment from the brick back-up. Of the 6 locations displaying
detachment, there was a complete correlation to areas theorized as detached based on nondestructive evaluation.
Where detachment has been identified between plaster and brick, the voids present were found to be between
2mm and 9mm wide. Due to the complete correlation found between detachment at the discreet plaster
drilling sites and medium and high mobility noted in the nondestructive evaluation, we believe that all areas
identified as mobile during the nondestructive evaluation can be presumed to be fully detached. This identifies
a significant portion of the plaster ceiling as detached from the brick -substrate.
Three samples of plaster were removed from the ceiling and analyzed for composition, proportion of materials, and
bond quality between layers. This is the first time plaster from Brumidi-decorated plaster inside the US Capitol building
has been chemically and petrographically analyzed. Analysis and evaluation of the plaster samples indicated that the
original plaster mix and application has several features that could make it more vulnerable to damage and deterioration
by outside factors such as vibration or moisture. These factors include the use of narrowly graded sand, a lack of hair
or fiber reinforcement, and the absence of plaster keying into the brick mortar joints.
Because the plaster in the President’s Room ceiling is functioning as an independent thin-shell structure, failure is
likely to be widespread and may occur suddenly rather than having incremental, small losses over time. Due
to these findings, our team recommends that a design for a monitoring plan and treatment campaign should
be started immediately to secure the long-term stability of the ceiling. The monitoring plan would include
vibration monitoring, environmental monitoring and additional investigation to determine any ongoing
conditions that are affecting the ceiling. This data would help create an appropriate treatment plan to ensure long
term stability of the ceiling. In the short term, access to the room should be limited, and shoring and support
of the plaster ceiling should be designed and implemented quickly to ensure both life safety and the protection of
the Brumidi decoration that are integral to the ceiling.

Page 3 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

II. INTRODUCTION
Background
This report has been prepared by Integrated Conservation Resources, Inc. (ICR) to document the findings of our
Phase 1-3 analysis of the President’s Room Ceiling (S-216), located in the Senate Wing of the U.S. Capitol Building in
Washington D.C.
While the U.S. Capitol was completed in 1830, the President’s Room is in a section of the building completed in the
1850s during an expansion of both the House and Senate wings. The President’s Room was decorated by the Italian
fresco painter Constantino Brumidi (1805-1880) between 1859 and 1860. Brumidi learned traditional fresco techniques
in Italy prior to moving to the United States in 1852. He was commissioned to decorate many interior spaces at the
Capitol primarily from 1855-1860 and occasionally worked in the Capitol into the 1860s. The President’s Room ceiling,
which is completed with fresco, tempera, and gilding, is one of the most highly decorative spaces executed by Brumidi
and is one of the most extravagant rooms in the Capitol.
The fresco portions of the President’s Room ceiling include four portraits of historical d1s (Benjamin Franklin, Christopher
Columbus, Amerigo Vespucci, and William Brewster), four depictions of allegorical figures (Liberty, Legislation, Religion,
and Executive power), and many surrounding putti. The original fresco elements are identified in Figure 1. The depictions
are surrounded by tempera trompe-l’oeil architectural elements and gilding.

Figure 1. Reflected ceiling plan (RCP) of the President’s Room indicating original fresco areas in yellow.

Page 4 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

The President’s Room has a slightly rectangular floor plan (23’ ½” x 21’ 8” with window bays extending an additional 2’
10”) while the ceiling is a smooth vault with a square plan. The transition from the rectangular space to the square
ceiling is subtle, with pilasters and recesses accounting for the difference in dimensions (Figure 2). The ceiling
construction is assumed to be a brick vault approximately 12” deep. The vault is covered in layers of plaster with the
surface layer finished in the traditional buon fresco technique. The original frescos have had minor alterations and
conservation over time, including consolidation and in-painting, but the remaining alterations are visually compatible
with the murals. The surrounding tempera trompe-l’oeil and gilded decoration have undergone major restorations since
the 1860’s. Alterations have included overpainting and regilding large portions of the decorative borders.

Figure 2. RCP of the President’s Room (left) and elevation looking north (right).

The space between the brick vault and the floor above may include some type of infill material. Original drawings from
the Capitol extension were reviewed during archival research, but these drawings displayed incomplete and often
conflicting information dated prior to the completion of the President’s Room. The diagram shown in Figure 3 was
created by ANA based on findings from Surface Penetrating Radar (SPR) in Phase 1 and review of original drawings
provided by AOC. Without as-built drawings or probes in the space above this President’s Room, this diagram remains
a theorized ceiling structure.

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.
and masonry vault at the President’s Room. One large section through the North Wing of the Senate

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

The President’s Room is on the second floor of the Senate Wing. The entrance is on the South side of the room, and
two windows are located on the North wall. Between the windows on the north wall is a fireplace that is no longer in
use. The room above the President’s Room is currently used as a press gallery. The first room of the press gallery on the
third floor has a larger, rectangular floor plan with three windows and two fireplaces on the north wall. The windows
and fireplace in the President’s Room align with the central and northwest window and fireplace in the press gallery.
The President’s Room is not only significant for its extraordinary decoration, but the space has been utilized by many
sitting U.S. Presidents. It was initially used as an office space for the President when they would visit Congress during
their term. The room would be used most heavily in the hours just before the end of the term for the President and
Congress as they rushed to pass legislation. This use for the room declined in the early 20th century as it became less
necessary for Presidents to do work in the Capitol building. In the second half of the 20th century, the President’s Room
became the location where incoming Presidents sign their first executive order after being sworn into office, a tradition
that continues today.
In 2017 a panel of experts met at the Capitol to review the condition of the President’s Room ceiling. This panel was
convened after multiple investigations drew attention to potential plaster conditions in the ceiling. There was also
concern that the condition of the ceiling may have been affected by an earthquake in 2011. The panel reviewed previous
reports on the ceiling, inspected the ceiling from lifts, and discussed the need for further investigation. While the panel
concluded that the ceiling was not at risk of failure in the short term, they recommended documentation and further
investigation to thoroughly characterize the condition of the ceiling. These recommendations played a major role in
defining the scope for this phase of work.
Project team
ICR gathered a team of experts to evaluate conditions in the President’s Room. The Team consultants are as follows:

The Team completed site visits to the Capitol in the fall of 2023 and spring of 2024 to conduct Phases 1-3 of the
scope of work. ICR was on-site with all members to facilitate work, leading badging and access. Informal discussions
with the Team and AOC were common on-site, prompting collaboration. Off site, ICR also conducted archival review,
coordinated the Team’s work, and supported the sharing of data and findings between team members. During Phase
3, ICR took the lead on further on-site investigation using physical evaluation. Included herein are the findings and
deliverables associated with the scope for Phases 1-3.
Scope
Prior investigations into the President’s Room have drawn attention to the condition of the ceiling and highlighted the
necessity for further investigation. The content of these reports and their recommendations were used to create the
scope of work. The project was divided into four phases as defined below:

Page 6 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Phase 1: Nondestructive Evaluation
This phase was completed between August and December of 2023. Phase 1 included a review of
archival materials provided by the Architect of the Capitol; documentation of the space using laser
scanning, photography, and digital photogrammetry; investigation of the material conditions of
finishes on the ceiling; and investigation of the material conditions of the plaster using Scanning Laser
Doppler Vibrometry, nondestructive techniques, and Sounding. The results of Phase 1 were submitted
in a draft report to AOC on December 22, 2023 and presented to AOC and the Panel on January 16-17,
2024.
Phase 2: Panel Meeting
The Phase 2 panel meeting took place from January 16-17, 2024. This two-day meeting included
presentations from team members on the work completed in Phase 1 and discussion of work to be
conducted in Phase 3. The original Phase 3 scope of work included the removal of plaster samples,
plaster analysis, and additional finishes testing. Following the panel meeting, ICR provided a proposal
for additional work including drilling resistance measurement testing, surface penetrating radar and
probes in room S-316, vibration monitoring, thermographic imaging, additional finishes testing, and
additional Scanning Laser Doppler Vibrometry. AOC decided to move forward with adding the drilling
resistance measurements (DRMS) and additional finishes testing to the original scope.
Phase 3: Physical Evaluation and Additional Testing
This Phase was completed between January and July of 2024. Phase 3 included additional archival
review, additional testing of finishes samples, overpaint reveals, drilling resistance measurement
testing (DRMS), small scale plaster probes, and petrographic and chemical analysis of plaster samples.
Phase 4: Panel Meeting
The Phase 4 panel meeting will take place in August in 2024 to discuss the findings from all phases of
the project and discuss recommendations for next steps.
Goals
The intent of the investigations captured in this report is to assess the current condition and material health of the
plaster ceiling and finishes, to determine if conditions of structural or physical concern exist, and to assess findings in
context of previously performed investigations.
This report and appendices document the tasks executed, condition observations, and recommendations for future
monitoring and interventions for the President’s Room ceiling. These findings fulfill the contracted Phase 1-4 scope of
work and are to be reviewed with the Project Team and Review Panel. This report was edited to consider comments
submitted by the Advisory Panel after the Phase 4 panel meeting.

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

III. GLOSSARY
Cold Joint/Giornata - A visible seam that forms between two adjacent sections of plaster where the plaster has not
formed a bond. Most frequently, this is due to a section of plaster installation being completed and allowed
to cure slightly before the adjacent section is installed. In most cases, a cold joint/giornata does not pose a
concern for the integrity or structure of the plaster.
Compromised Plaster – Condition of plaster in which there is some level of degradation within the plaster. In the case
of this report, cause of degradation is unknown.
Deflected Plaster – Downward facing plaster that has detached (see below) from its substrate and begins to be
weighed down by gravity, causing a void or space to form between the plaster and substrate. Once a section
of plaster begins to deflect, it can begin to detach plaster adjacent to perimeter of deflection.
Deformed Plaster - When used in this report, deformed refers to any bulges or other deformities in the topography of
the plaster surface that deviate from the expected topography of a smooth vault.
Detached Plaster – Condition of plaster in which the full depth of plaster has detached and lost bond from its 		
substrate, in this case, brick. Detachment describes the condition of the bond between the plaster and brick
while deflection refers to the systematic effect of large scale detachment, causing voids (see below).
Drilling Resistance Measurement System (DRMS) – a portable drilling machine system that measures the drilling
resistance of materials against the penetration depth of the drill bit. A constant rotation speed, penetration
rate, and drill bit diameter allows for continuous measurement based on constant parameters. This allows
the system to monitor increases or decreases in hardness throughout the depth of a material that may
signify changes in material quality and/or separation throughout the depth. This minimally invasive 		
technique is an important resource in measuring effect of degradation processes as well as the design of
potential conservation treatment options.
Mobility – Movement or vibration of plaster surface measured using various nondestructive evaluation techniques.
Nondestructive Evaluation (NDE) – Various investigative techniques that evaluate materials and their respective
properties without causing damage to the material itself. The goal of nondestructive evaluation is to assess
the quality, condition, and performance of a given material without damaging the substrate materials.
Petrography – An analysis that studies the composition of mineral-based materials (in the case of this report, plaster).
This analysis includes microscopic and chemical examination of a material that may provide quantitative
and qualitative information such as proportions of individual components, mineral identification, and quality
of compositional mix.
Plaster Sounding – A nondestructive investigative technique used to assess the condition of plaster material by
tapping the surface of the plaster with a light mallet. The sound as well as vibration of plaster during tapping
is assessed in reference to adjacent areas to compare quality of plaster within a larger area.
Hollow sound – A hollow or dull sound heard during Sounding may indicate either a void between plaster
		
coats or between the plaster or substrate, or deterioration within the plaster.
Scanning Laser Doppler Vibrometry (SLDV) – A nondestructive evaluation technique that induces movement in a
substrate using sound and measures displacement of vibration along the plaster surface. SLDV produces
high-resolution spatial maps of the vibration amplitudes and frequencies. This would allow any irregularities
within the plaster to be identified.
Anomaly – This term is used in this report to identify irregularities in the frequency readings which suggest
		
some difference in plaster vibration within a significant area. Based on SLDV data alone, it is not
		
clear what the cause or quality of the plaster condition at the location of the anomaly is.
Signal Processing – The analysis, manipulation, and interpretation of signals produced from electrical, acoustic, or
optical analyses that provide representation of physical quantities or data.

Page 8 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Surface Penetrating Radar (SPR) – A nondestructive evaluation technique used for subsurface evaluation.
		
Transmission of electromagnetic pulses into the plaster surface reflect signals that are collected and
analyzed 		
to create visualizations of subsurface features.
Void – This term is used in this report to a space identified between plaster and substrate following detachment and
deflection of the material.
Ultrasonic Pulse Echo (UPE) – A nondestructive evaluation technique by analyzing high-frequency ultrasonic waves
reflected off the plaster surface. This technique is used to map or visualize subsurface features.

Page 9 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

IV. ARCHIVAL RESEARCH
During Phase 1, archival materials were provided to the team, focusing on reports from the 1990s to present day. During
Phase 3, Additional archival materials were provided for review on site within the AOC Curator’s office. The materials
reviewed included additional photographs, correspondence, curators’ reports, and conditions and treatment reports
from other spaces decorated by Brumidi. A full list of relevant materials is included in Appendix G. During the Phase
2 Panel Meeting, the Team and panel discussed the extent of original drawings, including sections of the vault, and
modern mapping of systems such as plumbing and HVAC. From the documents provided by AOC, the Team cannot
confidently ascertain the as-built structure and does not feel that the systems mapping provided depicts the full extent
of systems concealed within the ceiling. Visual inspection of adjacent spaces identified piping that was not indicated on
the systems mapping provided, but could affect the President’s Room ceiling. Additionally, Surface Penetrating Radar
(SPR) identified a metal anomoly around the perimeter of the room that is not accounted for in the systems mapping.
The original drawings provided pre-date the completition of the President’s Room ceiling by up to five years, and show
a plaster configuration that was not completed. These drawings do not a provide a definitive as-built section of the
President’s Room ceiling.
Documents provided by the AOC included reports from several studies conducted in the President’s Room from 1995
to today. A large-scale conservation campaign was undertaken in 1994 that focused on the painted surfaces in the
President’s Room. This report, produced by Cunningham-Adams Conservation, is highly detailed and helped inform
the Team on the artistic methods used by Brumidi, allegorical references that likely informed the ceiling’s decoration,
previous interventions to the ceiling, and thorough documentation of the conservation work competed in 1995. This
report also raised concern about the plaster substrate of the ceiling, which had not been thoroughly studied at the time
of the Cunningham-Adams campaign.
Many of the plaster surfaces of the Capitol building were surveyed between 2003 and 2004, producing several reports
and publications that included information on the President’s Room. This work included both sounding and Scanning
Laser Doppler Vibrometry (SLDV). These reports also listed concern for the attachment of the plaster ceiling in the
President’s Room based on their findings.
After an earthquake in 2011, Cunningham-Adams Conservation returned to the President’s Room to evaluate the
progression of damage due to the earthquake. Cunningham-Adams Conservation submitted a brief report, which
indicated that the progression of cracks and detachment could not be determined, but Sounding in select areas
indicated further detachment compared to the 1995 findings.
In 2017, the Architect of the Capitol brought together a panel of experts to review the previous reports on the President’s
Room ceiling and conduct very limited on-site investigation. The panel’s conclusions were drawn from review of past
reports and investigation with limited access, indicating to the panel that the conditions on the ceiling did not appear
to be “active” or “growing.” While the report does indicate that there is likely detachment from the brick, the panel
thought that detachment may be more localized than indicated in previous studies. Some of these conclusions were
drawn from observations during the removal of plaster samples conducted in a discrete location on the wall near the
northeast window. There was no plaster analysis completed on the samples from the wall. The samples may not have
the same plaster makeup as the ceiling and have been exposed to different conditions, so the evaluation of these
samples may not be representative of the ceiling. The panel concluded that the ceiling was not at risk of imminent
failure but further investigation and treatment were necessary. They believed it does not have long term stability. The
panel’s findings heavily influenced the scope of the current project and highlighted the need for thorough investigation
of the ceiling’s conditions at this current moment, along with the creation of detailed documentation that will allow
future conservators to track changing conditions over time.
Additional sources reviewed for archival research included resources published online by the U.S. Capitol, C-Span, and
Library of Congress. These resources detailed important historic events that occurred in the President’s Room. The
Library of Congress digital collection also holds photographs of the room taken in the early 20th century.

Page 10 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

V. TIMELINE OF INTERVENTIONS AND PREVIOUS STUDIES
The President’s Room has been the focus of multiple studies and restoration campaigns since its completion in 1860.
The following timeline was produced to summarize the known work completed in the space. While the word “touchup”
has been used in archival documents to describe prior work to the ceiling, this phrase could have denoted significant
intervention to the finishes, including widespread over painting. The focus of many previous interventions was the
finishes of the fresco and surrounding trompel’oeil architectural framework, but reports from the last two decades have
shifted focus to the condition of the plaster substrate and its possible instability.

Figure 4. Photograph of the President’s Room prior to the 1920s. The photo was taken before the central octagon collapsed in 1921.
Image from the Library of Congress.

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

1860: President’s Room decoration is completed and signed by Brumidi
1862: Requests for Brumidi to complete repairs in the President’s Room
1868: Painting occurred in the President’s Room
1896: Chandelier was converted from gas to electric
1901: Cleaning proposed in the President’s Room
1915: Additional arms added to the chandelier
1915: Explosion in the Capitol, causing damage to the Senate Wing of the building. Touchups were
completed during this year
1918: The President’s Room was identified for “painting and touching up as needed”
1921: The plaster at the center octagon of the President’s Room ceiling fails and is replaced without
recreating the original fresco decoration (see Figure 4 for a photograph of the room before the
plaster failure). At some point after the plaster was repaired, a canvas painting was installed above
the chandelier
1920-1928: Water damage occurs in the northeast corner of the ceiling. The Brewster fresco and Putti
below are damaged
1931: Brewster fresco and associated Putti are restored by Comins. This restoration requred the
removal and replacement of the plaster at the Putti below Brewster. The entire room also had work to
be “restored to its original condition as near as possible.” A description of the work from the Report
from the Architect of the Capitol says, “it has been necessary to replace and reburnish the gold leaf
used in the work, until now the room presents a very bright and cheerful appearance on account of
the freshening of the colors and the gold leaf used in the decoration
1991: Treatment to the egg and dart portion of the architectural trompe-l’oeil around Brewster
1995: Full cleaning and conservation campaign completed by Cunningham-Adams Conservation,
including conservation treatment for the finishes, crack mapping, and sounding. The canvas was
removed from the central octagon, and was retained in the AOC curator’s collection
2003-2004: SLDV data collected in the room as part of a larger plaster survey conducted in the
Capitol.
2004: Further plaster survey conducted in various locations in the building (including sounding,
SLDV, and thermal imaging)
2011: Sounding completed by Cunningham-Adams to evaluate post-earthquake state
2017: Advisory panel to discuss the condition of the ceiling and the possible need for intervention

VI. LASER SCANNING AND DIGITAL PHOTOGRAMMETRY
Methodology
Aaslestad Preservation Consulting (APC) completed baseline documentation of the President’s Room prior to the
erection of scaffolding. This scope included 3D scanning, photography, and integrated photogrammetry. In August of
2023 APC conducted a site visit to complete laser scanning and photography as the basis for their documentation
package. The approximate density of the point cloud used was 420,000 points per cubic meter. This data was then
processed to create a high-density point cloud and a series of measured drawings and images. The point cloud was
further analyzed after the Phase 2 panel meeting based on findings from the Phase 1 nondestructive testing. APC was
provided with the Sounding map, SLDV map, and crack mapping from Phase 1 to investigate any deformations that
could correlate to plaster conditions. The form/shape of the ceiling was evaluated using topographic mapping and a
visual gradient corresponding to elevation. Color nodes in the gradient or irregular lines in the topographic map would
indicate areas of significant deformation (Figure 6).

Figure 5. View of the President’s Room ceiling looking upwards provided by APC.

Page 13 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Findings
APC has provided the Team with high quality images, measured drawings, and a point cloud that could be used by all
team members for site investigation and reporting findings. The point cloud captures the room’s existing conditions in
three dimensions. It can also be used by future investigators and will enable reliable comparison of conditions over
time. The 2D measured drawings with pointcloud projections and orthophotography can be seen in the documentation
packet included in Appendix A. The topographic map and elevation gradient (Figure 6) led APC to conclude that the
dome was very smooth. APC highlighted that any deformations in the ceiling could be original to the application of the
plaster. The surface of the dome did not indicate deformation correlated to areas of interest identified by sounding,
SLDV, or crack mapping. The laser scan confirms the presence of cracking but did not indicate effects of the cracking
on the overall form of the dome. Any offsets at cracks were smaller than the density of the laser scan.

Figure 6. Screen capture of the model with a color gradient and topographic overlay produced by APC during Phase 3. The color scale
goes from blue at the lowest elevation to red at the highest elevation. If there were large deformations in the ceiling, the color increase
would not appear in the uniform circles displayed here. The uniform circular topographic lines also indicate a typical dome shape without
large deformations.

Page 14 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

VII.

FINISHES CONDITION ASSESSMENT

Methodology
Gianfranco Pocobene Studios (GPS) was on site to complete a survey of the ceiling in September 2023. GPS conducted
visual observations of the painted surfaces under various lighting conditions (ambient, specular, raking, and ultraviolet
light) to identify conditions. Conditions were photographed and mapped on measured drawings. Aqueous and solvent
tests were conducted in discreet locations to identify the degree of soiling and locations of non-original materials
and overpaint. Multiple solvent mixtures were used to determine paint solubility for the possible removal of previous
restorations and overpaint.
While on site for Phase 1, the team extracted six samples, including both cross-section samples and scrapings. Analysis
of these samples would support the on-site work by characterizing pigments, substrates, and salts present on the
ceiling. The location of each sample was photo-documented and chosen with input from AOC. The samples were
then evaluated by Erin Mysak, PhD. Analytical techniques included cross sectional microscopy, scanning electron
microscopy (SEM), and the use of FT-IR, Raman, and SEM for medium/pigment identification. During the Phase 2 Panel
meeting, the findings from these samples were discussed and the Team determined the focus for further sampling in
Phase 3. Further finishes work focused on mapping and testing areas with conditions, including failing overpaint and
efflorescence, and determining the feasibility of removing overpaint in the architectural trompe-l’oeil framework.
GPS returned to site in March 2024 for Phase 3 to extract additional samples that were analyzed with similar methods
used in Phase 1. Many samples were taken from the architectural trompe-l’oeil framework and from areas exhibiting
efflorescence. While sample analysis in Phase 1 mainly focused on identified medium types, techniques, and layers
added over time, the Phase 3 sample analysis included the following goals:
•

Comparing efflorescence on fresco surfaces to efflorescence from the decorative borders.

•

Identifying any additional medium in the decorative borders.

•

Identifying the medium or consolidation in the decorative borders that are flaking.

•

Determining if the gold decorative border has been regilded.

During Phase 3, GPS also tested additional solvents for the removal of overpaint, and the most successful product was
used to create small reveals. These areas could provide information on the original color scheme of the architectural
trompe-l’oeil border. A sample map for the locations of analytical samples and reveals can be seen in Figure 7.
Findings
The President’s Room ceiling has multiple finish types. These include the original fresco panels, the trompe-l’oeil
framework originally completed in a water-soluble tempera (and later overpainted in oil), and gilded decoration. The
ceiling also has 32 ocher-colored medallions. These medallions proved to be water soluble during solubility testing, and
it is unclear if these elements are entirely original or if they have been touched up in the ceiling’s history. Each of these
media types display different conditions.
Fresco Conditions:
The fresco panels are in generally sound condition. Areas of retouching were identified in the frescos,
which adequately match the original surface in most locations (Figure 8). Efflorescence is present on
the fresco surfaces, but it only appears in isolated areas (Figure 9).
Analysis of cross section samples of the efflorescence indicates that salts are only present on the
surface of the ceiling. Subflorescence was not observed in the top layer of plaster in cross section
samples. The efflorescence was identified as sodium-based compounds including carbonates and/
or oxalates. The source and cause of efflorescence is unclear. The problem is not widespread and
does not appear to have damaged the fresco surfaces. All of the frescos exhibit a thin layer of soiling
that could be easily removed by lightly wiping with a cosmetic sponge. Analytical samples also
confirmed that Brumidi executed the frescoes in a traditional buon fresco technique. The notable
features of buon fresco technique used in the President’s Room include a thick base plaster layer with
calcium carbonate and quartz sands with a thinner finish layer with fine pigments creating the figural
decoration.

Page 15 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Figure 7. Map of all finishes sample locations and the location of the reveals conducted in 2024.

Figure 8. Photograph provided by GPS of ultraviolet induced fluorescence showing retouching in the niche background of an original
fresco element.
Page 16 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Figure 9. Photograph provided by GPS of efflorescence observed on the surface of an original fresco element.

Trompe-l’oeil Framework Conditions:
The trompe-l’oeil framework exhibited less stable conditions in comparison to the original frescoes.
These areas have a considerable amount of overpaint. The trompe-l’oeil exhibits areas of blind
cleavage, active flaking, and paint loss (see Figure 10). This appears to be an ongoing problem with
evidence of previous consolidation efforts being observed (Figure 11).
A sample taken from the restored framework also confirmed multiple layers of overpaint, but medium
analysis was complicated by the presence of consolidants from previous restorations necessitating
additional samples. Additional testing in the trompe-l’oeil architectural framework during Phase 3
did not provide further information on the binding medium of the original paint layer due to the
preponderance of the gypsum in the sample. Efflorescence from the trompe-l’oeil framework was
also analyzed and appeared similar in composition and crystallization location to efflorescence at the
original frescos.

Figure 10. Photograph taken by GPS of the trompe l’oeil architectural surrounding, showing current conditions with actively flaking
paint. This condition is primarily observed in areas with overpaint.
Page 17 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Figure 11. Photograph taken by GPS of the trompe l’oeil architectural surrounding, showing current conditions including uneven
consolidation of paint.

During the 1994-1995 conservation campaign by Cunningham-Adams Conservation, multiple reveals
were created to interpret the original colors of the trompe-l’oeil framework. Today, these reveals
appear to be a cooler tone than the overpaint, and they are less saturated in comparison. The Team
found that the previous reveals may have a chalkier appearance due to the aggressive methods used
for overpaint removal. A new reveal was conducted during Phase 3 using modern gel formulations,
which provided better results than the 1994 reveal. The colors of the original decorative surface may
have been warmer in tone than previously assumed and had more contrast (Figure 12).

Soy-Gel

2024
Reveal

1994
Reveal

Figure 12. Photograph of reveals from 1994 and 2024. Reveals conducted in 2024 tested a range of products (see Appendix B). Blue
Bear Soy Gel was the most successful removal technique.
Page 18 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

The original details and shadows of the decoration may have been distorted in the process of
overpainting. The overpaint showed little to no response to solubility tests in Phase 1 and reveals
conducted in Phase 3 confirmed the tenacity of the overpaint. Blue Bear Soy Gel Paint and Urethane
Stripper was the most successful of the formulations tested with an initial dwell time of 40-60 minutes
and additional shorter applications of 15-20 minutes.
Gilded Borders:
The gilded surfaces are in good condition, but they have been regilded multiple times since the 1860’s.
The layer visible today appears to have been applied with a drying oil mordant and glazed with a
water-soluble media to tone down the sheen and create and aged appearance. The regilding of the
President’s Room was mentioned in archival research. The analysis of a cross-section sample during
Phase 3 confirmed prior regilding campaigns and included four distinct layers of gold leaf.
Discussion
The finish of the original fresco decoration of the President’s Room ceiling is in stable condition, and only minimal attention
to the frescos is recommended at this time. Continued monitoring of the fresco conditions and surface efflorescence
is recommended. The more complex issues are observed in the trompe l’oeil architectural framework, which has been
heavily restored and now exhibits unstable finishes conditions such as blind cleavage, active flaking paint, and some
paint loss. Restoration efforts prior to the Cunningham-Adams campaign in 1994 failed to accurately replicate the
color and luminous quality of the original tempera paint. These areas raise both conservation and aesthetic questions
to be carefully considered. While a modern gel formulation was more successful for removing overpaint, there is a
still risk involved with its use. The strong bond between the original paint and the overpaint requires long dwell times
for overpaint removal, which can compromise the thin original paint layer. During the Phase 2 panel meeting, three
intervention options were discussed in a preliminary fashion. These included a minimal approach, a comprehensive
approach, and a restoration approach. The minimal approach would treat unstable paint conditions in the trompel’oeil architectural framework, remove efflorescence, and monitor change to the finishes over time. The comprehensive
approach would be to remove all non-original, restoration paint layers. Full scale paint removal would likely require
stabilization and in-painting after the overpaint is removed. A restoration approach would include conducting reveals
and analytical testing to determine the original color scheme in the overpainted areas for their replication. At this time,
a strategy of minimal intervention to stabilize the current overpaint and monitor conditions going forward may be the
most feasible. These intervention options raise both conservation and aesthetic questions to be carefully considered
before any conservation treatments are planned.

Page 19 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

VIII.

NONDESTRUCTIVE EVALUATION

A. Sounding
Methodology
During Phase 1, ANA conducted Sounding of the ceiling using small, spherical, wooden impactors. A sound library
was created by recording the Sounding. A grid system was determined and a recording of a sounding impact was
conducted at each grid location. The recordings for the sound library could then be processed to mathematically
differentiate the sounds observed while conducting sounding. The CUA Team also conducted signal processing on a
small subset of the sounding data.
Findings
Sounding techniques identified distinct differences in sounds in a variety of locations on the ceiling. The Team identified
and mapped three distinct sounds and their associated vibration in the substrate. The first of the sounds was identified
as a sharp sound without corresponding vibration, which often relates to an attached or sound substrate. This region
makes up ~12% of the ceiling. The other two sounds were defined as two hollow sounds of progressive severity. The
moderately hollow sound is ~83% of the ceiling, and the most hollow sound is ~5% of the ceiling. A mapping of these
sounds was created (see Figure 13).
These Sounding categories could be identified in signal processing from the sound library recordings using standard
signal analysis techniques. CUA was also provided with a small sample of the sounding data. The Team completed
additional signal analysis and processing to support ANA’s findings. Their commentary on the data processing can be
found in Appendix D.
Additional physical testing in Phase 3 focused on correlating Sounding findings to physical conditions in the plaster.

Figure 13. Sounding map produced by ANA. Blue areas indicate areas with a sharp sound that generally indicates attached plaster,
orange areas indicate a moderately hollow sound and red areas indicate the most hollow noise identified through Sounding.
Page 20 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

B. Scanning Laser Doppler Vibrometry (SLDV)
Methodology
During Phase 1, Catholic University of America (CUA) conducted data acquisition in August of 2023. The process was
conducted over eight days with data acquisition taking place at night to decrease disturbance to those working in
the building. The process of SLDV requires exciting the plaster using a repetitive acoustic signal. The microscopic
movement of the plaster is recorded using a laser. Areas of plaster that experience detachment, in theory, would exhibit
more movement than areas that are secure. An area is identified as an anomaly if movement is measured over multiple
frequencies. The process can identify anomalies in the plaster larger than 5 x 5 cm. The method is non-destructive and
does not require physical contact with the ceiling. 18 total scans were conducted to capture the entire ceiling. The data
was then processed using MATLAB and overlaid with images of the ceiling to accurately map anomalies.
The CUA Team conducted similar research from 2003-2004, collecting SLDV data in the President’s Room. This allowed
the Team to make observations about changes in the location of mobile regions over the twenty year period.
Findings
The data evaluation indicated that there are large anomalies present in the President’s Room ceiling. Based on the
anomalies identified by CUA, areas of low and high mobility within the ceiling were mapped. Areas of low mobility
include the center octagon, which was replaced in the 1920’s, the lower cherubs, and areas around the perimeter of the
vault. There is greater mobility observed on the east side of the ceiling than on the west side with the Legislation and
Vespucci frescoes showing smaller areas of mobility than other fresco areas (see Figure 14). By reviewing the SLDV
data alone, it is not possible to identify the cause or corresponding plaster condition within an area of lower or higher
mobility. However, correlation between the SLDV data and other physical evaluations carried out in Phase 3 are captured
in the Analysis section of this report.

Figure 14. 2023 Overall vibrational map of the ceiling with approximations of anomalies produced by CUA. Red areas represent
higher mobility zones while blue represent areas of lower mobility. The boundary between the red and blue areas (purple) represent
areas of lower amplitude readings than towards the center of the red area.
Page 21 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

The overlapping areas between scans were also evaluated to understand the variation from one scan to another. This
analysis indicated that the same areas of mobility were identified across multiple scans, but there can be variation in
the response between scans in the same region. While many of the areas of higher mobility span multiple elements of
the ceiling (original fresco and the trompe-l’oeil architectural border), others seem to correspond to specific giornata
(cold joints) (Figure 15).

Figure 15. Overall vibrational map of the ceiling with approximations of high mobility areas (shown in red) produced by CUA with giornata (cold
joints) overlaid in green. Red arrows indicate some but not all locations where giornata and SLDV mobility correlate.

CUA was also able to compare the 2023 SLDV results with data reported in 2004. This comparison showed that scans
often had a large amount of agreement on where anomalies exist. The change in magnitude of movement between
2004 and 2023 cannot be compared directly between the data sets due to changes in data collection, but the locations
where anomalies exist can be compared. Some of the differences observed in the two data sets are likely due to
increased degradation of the ceiling in the last 20 years. This is indicated by areas identified in both scans extending
over a larger area in 2023 than they appeared in 2004. The scans from the two different campaigns are plotted over
different frequency ranges, which could account for anomalies being visible in the 2004 scan that do not appear in the
2023 data (see Figure 38 in analysis for further comparison). Progess in SLDV technology has also allowed for
improvement in resolution, which can decrease some of the noise evident in the 2004 scan, particularly in the region
around the Vespucci fresco.
C. Surface Penetrating Radar (SPR)
Methodology
During Phase 1, ANA attempted surface penetrating radar to investigate the ceiling configuration in the President’s
Room. Surface penetrating radar (SPR) is a technique used to investigate subsurface features and anomalies. A pulse of
radar energy is transmitted into the substrate and the time between transmission and reception of the reflected pulse
is measured. SPR can detect subsurface differences in materials, void spaces, and the presence of metals in the cross
section of a substrate. ANA utilized a Proceq GP8800 unit with stepped frequencies from 400 to 6000 MHz.
Page 22 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Findings
Surface penetrating radar was not successful at identifying areas of detachment, but it was able to identify the cross
section of the vault including the plaster/clay brick masonry interfaces, the back of the masonry vault, and possibly the
overlying floor structure of the room above. A theorized cross section of the ceilings structure was produced by ANA
based on the SPR findings. Some of these measurements were confirmed by extracted plaster samples. SPR indicated
that the plaster ceiling was approximately 1-1.5”thick. The brick vault was approximately 12” in depth, and the floor
surface in the room above was approximately 4”(Figure 3). The SPR findings also identified a metal anomaly near the
spring line of the vault that extends around the room at a constant elevation.
D. Ultrasonic Pulse Echo (UPE)
Methodology
During Phase 1, ANA attempted Ultrasonic pulse echo (UPE) investigation as another NDE technique. UPE can also
be used to investigate subsurface features in the ceiling. UPE uses eight channels. Each channel emits a transmission
while the other seven channels receive data. The scans are combined to understand subsurface elements of the cross
section of the ceiling. The team did not expect to detect void or delamination using UPE but hoped to identify the
distance from the surface of the ceiling to the back vault. ANA used a Proceq PD8000 ultrasonic pulse echo array for
this investigation.
Findings
Ultrasonic pulse echo (UPE) was not able to detect the back of the vault, and it was unable to detect detachment of
the plaster. The Team suspects that there was too much scattering from material interfaces, which interfered with the
signal of the UPE.
E. VISUAL INSPECTION
Methodology
ICR conducted visual inspections of the ceiling in both Phase 1 and Phase 3. This included hands-on inspection of the
ceiling for visible plaster and finishes conditions, including but not limited to cracking, deflection, efflorescence, and
surface discoloration. It was noted that some locations of surface finish discoloration appeared in a pattern of wettingdrying. These locations often correspond with visible surface efflorescence and flaking paint. These visible conditions
suggest that moisture infiltration has occurred at these locations.
A detailed crack mapping was conducted in Phase 3. A transparent crack card was used to measure cracks, categorizing
them into four categories: cracks smaller than 0.01”, cracks ranging from 0.010-0.014”, cracks ranging from 0.016-0.018”,
and cracks larger than 0.018”. The crack card included measurements in 0.002” increments. Additionally, giornata also
referred to as “cold joints,” were mapped. The mapping produced was overlaid with NDE techniques from Phase 1 to
identify any correlation between cracking and plaster conditions. All crack mapping can be found in Appendix F.
Findings
The Team produced the following map (Figure 16) with areas of suspected moisture infiltration. These conditions are
most prevalent in the northwest corner of the room. It is important to note that without further investigation, the
Team cannot determine if moisture infiltration is ongoing. There have been previous moisture problems in the room
as noted in the archival research. Particularly in the region of the Brewster fresco (the northeast corner), which has
required the most remediation for water damage in the past. Figure 16 may not be a comprehensive mapping of
suspected moisture infiltration issues because it is only based on visible surface conditions, which may have changed
over time with multiple restoration and conservation campaigns. Further investigation through NDE would be required
to determine the determine if this issue is ongoing and if there is an identifiable source of moisture within floor and wall
system in the surrounding area.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

The majority of cracks in the President’s room are smaller than 0.016” and likely correspond to the shrinkage of plaster
when drying. These cracks can be identified as hairline, and it does not visually appear as though these cracks continue
past the surface of the plaster. Out of plane movement was not observed adjacent to the cracks, which was supported
by the laser scanning. GPS also noted that no finish and plaster loss was associated with crack locations. Additionally,
portions of some cracks are partially obscured by previous restoration campaigns, with infill and oil paint filling sections
of cracks. This indicates that some cracking predates the earlier restoration campaigns and have not re-cracked due to
ongoing movement since this previous refinishing. When overlaid with NDE techniques, no correlation between cracks
and Sounding or SLDV were observed. As mentioned above, there is a suspected correlation between Sounding and
giornata (cold joints). While crack mapping was completed in 1994, there were no recorded measurements of crack
width and no differentiation between crazing, joints, and true cracks. The Team does not feel that the two maps can be
reliably compared for the worsening of crack conditions.

Page 24 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

VI. PHYSICAL EVALUATION
A. Drilling Resistance Measurement System (DRMS)
Methodology
The Drilling Resistance Measurement System (DRMS) measures the required force to drill into a substrate while drilling
at a constant rotation rate and penetration speed. This technique can identify changes in hardness along the depth
of drilling that can indicate layer demarcations, material conditions, and voids between layers. The intention of using
DRMS in Phase 3 was to correlate findings from the Phase 1 NDE to physical conditions in the plaster, and note potential
patterns of loss or increase in hardness throughout the depth of plaster that cannot be attributed to the plaster
properties themselves. The team identified drill locations based on the Phase 1 Sounding and SLDV, grouping drill holes
into the categories of suspected low mobility, medium mobility, and high mobility. Additional holes were drilled into
areas that showed visual signs of moisture infiltration (Figure 16) where efflorescence and finishes discoloration was
seen in areas of perceived hollow sounding. Each test group included at least three drill holes. Holes were only drilled
into the trompe-l’oeil architectural border to avoid invasive testing at the original fresco panels.
