Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Drilling was conducted with a DRMS drill manufactured by SINT Technology. A specialized DRMS 5 mm diamond drill
bit was used with a rotation speed of 250 rotations per minute and a penetration rate of 30 mm/min. The team used
a videoscope to investigate visible features and conditions within each drill hole. Prior to taking data, a calibration
hole was required. The data from this hole cannot be used to compare to other data because drilling parameters were
altered prior to taking the rest of the data. Holes remaining from drilling were cleaned of dust and debris and were then
filled with neat plaster of paris. Plaster of paris was chosen because it would be softer than the surrounding plaster. It is
also a pure material, which avoids introducing additives that could affect the surrounding area of the ceiling.
Findings
Interpretation of DRMS measurements provided information about the plaster depth, plaster layers, plaster condition
and void space at discrete locations in the President’s Room ceiling. A log of all DRMS information is included in
Appendix H with a summary of the findings and patterns within the data discussed here. At drill locations, the team
could identify information about the following important features:
Plaster Depth:
At each drill location, the total depth from the decorated plaster surface to the brick vault was
measured. In the President’s Room, this depth varied greatly between drill locations. The deepest
plaster was identified in drill hole PR-MM-2 at 32 mm while the shallowest plaster was found at sample
location PR-HM-S1, which was 12 mm.
Finish Coat:
In the trompe-l’oeil architectural border, the DRMS indicated two coats of plaster, a thin finish coat
(0.5-1.2 mm) and a base coat. Each DRMS measurement included a spike in force at start of the
measurement, indicating that the finish layer of plaster was harder than the base coats.
Base Coat:
The base coat was identified in the DRMS data by a consistently low but varied force. The base coat
ranged in depth from 12 mm to 30 mm. At some locations, a slight drop in force occurred towards the
back of the plaster base coat, indicating a potentially weaker or softer area of plaster closer to the
brick.
Voids:
Notably, data of some drill holes identified a void between the base coat and the brick vault. These
were identified in the DRMS data by a drop to a constant, near zero force. Voids identified in the DRMS
data were verified using a videoscope. Some drill locations had no void while other locations had
voids ranging from 2.5 mm to 9 mm.
Compromised Plaster:
At some locations, DRMS drilling indicated a decrease in force toward the back of the plaster coat,
indicating some weakening of plaster.
While the team identified the drill locations based on the idea of low, medium, and high mobility theorized by the Phase
1 NDE, the DRMS data led to a new categorization of plaster identified by these physical characteristics. These include
attached plaster, detached plaster, and compromised plaster (for a map of sample locations, see Appendix I).
Attached Plaster:
This category was identified where sounding and SLDV indicated low mobility and the DRMS did
not indicate voids. The following graph of PR-LM-1 shows the typical markers of attached plaster
in the DRMS data (Figure 18). Attached plaster measurements were used as a control to compare
measurements at other locations where NDE identified mobility in the plaster.

vibration noted in areas of thinner plaster depth, and not related to depth of the voids of this plaster.

At areas with signs of water infiltration, DRMS often showed a decreased but non-zero force in the
base coat before reaching the brick interface (Figure 22). From the videoscope images, these regions
were confirmed to not include a void space, but rather a “haloed” effect where the drill disturbed a
larger diameter of plaster before reaching the brick vault (Figure 23). It is possible that a softer or
weaker plaster could be readily disturbed by the higher speed movement of the drill bit, causing this
haloed effect. These regions also corresponded to locations where sounding and SLDV did not align.
Sounding identified high mobility areas where as SLDV indicated low mobility.

B. Plaster Sample Analysis
Methodology
Three plaster samples were removed from the President’s Room ceiling in April of 2024. Sample locations chosen
based on the NDE data from Phase 1, including sounding and SLDV data. Samples were only taken from trompe-l’oeil
architectural border to avoid invasive testing at the original fresco panels. Samples were approximately 4” x 4” and
spanned the full depth of the plaster. One sample was removed from each level of mobility as identified during the NDE;
samples correspond to low mobility (sample PR-LM-1), medium mobility (sample PR-MM-1), and high mobility (sample
PR-HM-1) regions. A DRMS hole was drilled adjacent to the sample location prior to sample removal in order to identify
correlatory data between the two testing techniques. The low mobility sample was removed using a router bit at the
perimeter. The team then decided to proceed with using only hand tools to remove the medium and high mobility
samples. Sample locations were infilled with neat plaster of paris, applied in lifts. Plaster of paris was chosen due to the
similarity between this material and the original plaster in material properites. Additionally, plaster of paris will allow for
ease of removal if sample areas need to be studied or repaired differently as part of a larger repair campaign.
The samples were sent to Highbridge Materials Consulting, Inc. (HMC) for laboratory analysis. Laboratory analysis
included full compositional analysis of the low mobility sample and qualitative petrography for all three samples
extracted. Petrography is a particular type of microscopy intended that can be used on plasters and mortars with
chemical analysis to identify components of a plaster mix and estimate the original mix proportions. Petrography
can be used to identify binders, sand mineralogies, and other additives within a plaster mix. Chemical analysis can be
further used to identify the composition of a plaster and the proportion of each component. Acid digestion is used to
separate the aggregate from the plaster sample so that the aggregate can be sieved to measure the gradation of the
aggregate. Microscopy analysis additionally provides qualitative information regarding application and bond between
plaster coats.

Figure 24. Map of sample locations.

Page 30 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Findings
Sample Removal:
During removal of each sample, its relative attachment was determined based on the ease at which it released from the
ceiling. Sample PR-LM-S1 detached from the brick substrate once the edges were released, leaving a thin (<1 mm) layer
of plaster on the brick substrate (Figure 25). This sample area spanned a region with two perpendicular mortar joints.
A small amount of mortar remained attached to the plaster upon removal, creating an uneven surface on the backside
of the sample; the plaster did not appear to be keyed into the brick joint, rather the hard mortar was displaced from the
joint, creating the uneven surface in Figure 25.

Figure 25. Before image of the low mobility plaster sample location (left) and the after image of the sample location and the removed
plaster sample (right).

In sample PR-MM-S1, the region was identified as medium mobility by NDE. The sample readily came away from the
substrate when the edges were released. A thin (<1 mm) layer of plaster remained on the brick substrate. A ~6 mm deep
void was observed at the perimeter of the sample location between the brick and the base coat. Insertion of a thin tool
into the void confirmed that the void continued at least a few inches in each direction at the perimeter of the removed
sample (Figure 26). When light pressure was applied at the plaster directly adjacent to the sample locations, movement
was observed within the general area, suggesting deflection from the ceiling.

Figure 26. Before image of the medium mobility plaster sample location (left) and the after image of the sample location and the
removed plaster sample (right).

Page 31 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Figure 27. Image of the void space observed after the removal of the medium mobility plaster sample.

Sample PR-HM-S1 was removed from a region where both SLDV and sounding indicated high mobility. The sample
varied in depth but was much thinner than the other samples removed for the ceiling. The thinnest portion was ~12 mm.
A thin (<1 mm) layer of plaster remained on the brick substrate. The sample readily came away from the substrate when
the edges were released. A void was observed around the perimeter of the sample location. This void was smaller than
the void seen in the medium mobility sample, but still indicates a similar form of detachment between the base coat
and the brick substrate (Figure 29).

Figure 28. Before image of the high mobility plaster sample location (left) and the after image of the sample location and the removed
plaster sample (right).

Page 32 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Figure 29. High mobility plaster sample after removal (left) and the image of the void space observed after the removal of the high
mobility plaster sample (right).

Laboratory Analysis:
Petrography and chemical analysis indicated that the plaster from all three samples was similar in both quality and mix.
All samples included a scratch coat, brown coat, and finish coat. The scratch coat and brown coat, which are referred to
as the base coat layers, have quartz-rich natural sand that is sharply textured. The base coats have a lime putty to sand
ratio of approximately 1 : 3.7 (scratch coat) and 1:3.6 (brown coat). The gradation of the base coat sand is somewhat
narrow. The finish coat has a very fine quartz aggregate, which is also sharp-textured. The finish coat has a lime putty
to sand ratio of approximately 1 : 1.6. All plaster layers include a nonhydraulic lime binder, using an intermediate lime.
Petrography indicated that the workmanship for mixing and applying the plaster was skilled with all coats of plaster
well compacted upon installation. The sand was relatively well washed, the lime was thoroughly slaked, the lime and
sand were well blended, and the layers were well compacted.

Figure 30. PLL photomicrophraphs of the all plaster samples provided by HMC. Arrow indicates the demarcation between the finish coat
(FC) and the base coat (BC).
Page 33 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

While the samples display typical materials for 19th century lime plasters, there are several findings from the petrography
and chemical analysis that could indicate an inferior plaster mix. The base coats did not include hair reinforcement,
which was historically used to prevent fine cracking. The chemical analysis indicated that the lime used for the binder
was of a moderate quality. This is an inferior binder to a fat lime or dolomitic lime, which would have provided additional
plasticity or strength respectively. The sand contents of the base coats were higher than expected for an intermediate
lime and the sand was narrowly graded. A gradation that includes more fine particles (passing the No. 50 sieve) would
provide improved cohesive strength.
The petrography of the three samples did not indicate any fundamental differences between the three samples that
would correlate to the differences observed through NDE, DRMS, and sample removal. It is important to remember that
aspects such as poor initial suction, movement of the underlying structure, poor or nonexistent keying, and long-term
embrittlement can all affect the condition of plaster but cannot be identified using petrography and chemical analysis.

Page 34 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

III. ANALYSIS
The central goal for Phase 3 was to understand the physical condition of the plaster in the President’s Room and
correlate this to the nondestructive evaluation that was completed in Phase 1. This could allow the Team to extrapolate
the conditions observed through DRMS to the whole ceiling, using the previous NDE mapping. Multiple NDE methods
were attempted in the President’s room to evaluate the ceiling structure and plaster condition, including Sounding,
Scanning Laser Doppler Vibrometry, Surface Penetrating Rader, and Ultrasonic Pulse Echo. Sounding was a viable
method for identifying locations with underlying plaster conditions, but in this study, Sounding could not reliably
differentiate between areas of plaster that were detached and those that were compromised as observed through
DRMS. The sounding map produced in 2023 showed many similarities in hollow sounding regions to the map created
by Cunningham-Adams in 1994, which shows that the results can be replicable over time.
Scanning Laser Doppler Vibrometry (SLDV) proved to be the most successful NDE technique for identifying detached
plaster. There was complete correlation between locations where SLDV indicated low and high mobility to those that
had attachment and detachment within the DRMS samples. SLDV mapping from 2004 and 2023 produced similar
maps, but the Team was unable to compare the magnitude of movement between the two campaigns. Differences
between equipment, data collection, and plotting also make it difficult to compare locations of mobility between
the two campaigns.
Some of the NDE methods tested provided data that was not useful in identifying construction or conditions at
the plaster ceiling. Ultrasonic Pulse Echo (UPE) was not successful at identifying the back of the vault and therefore
could not be used to determine discontinuities in the plaster assembly. Surface Penetrating Radar (SPR) was
successful at determining the relative depth of the plaster and the depth of the brick vault but was unable to be
used to identify any voids or detachment. SPR was also able to identify anomalies in the construction that may
relate to conduit or additional metal elements in the ceiling. SPR was only conducted from the bottom of the vault,
but the Team believes conducting SPR in room S-316 (Press Room) would help to identify chases or conduit that
may be present above the President’s Room.

Figure 36. 2023 Sounding map produced by ANA (left) indicating attachment in blue and detached or compromised plaster in orange
and red based on findings from DRMS and sample removal. 2023 SLDV mapping produced by CUA (right) indicating detachment in red
and attachment in blue based on findings in DRMS and sample removal.

A summary table of the condition at each DRMS hole and plaster sample location is provided in the following table.
Of the fourteen DRMS samples, two did not produce reliable data. This included Sample 8, which experienced
a hardware failure during drilling and Sample 14 where the DRMS plot had a number of outliers, making the data
difficult to interpret. The failure at Sample 14 was believed to be based on the drill bit catching on a large piece of
aggregate rather than interferance due to a plaster condition or prior repair. Plots for every DRMS sample can be
found in Appendix H.
Through DRMS analysis, the Team has concluded that regions where the SLDV indicated mobility correlate
to detachment of the plaster. While the sample size tested by DRMS is statistically small, all DRMS holes where
SLDV indicated mobility also corresponded to an identifiable void. These included samples Samples 4, 5, 6, 7, 9, and 11
and the medium and high mobility plaster samples (Figure 32 & 33). At all locations where SLDV indicated low
mobility, DRMS and plaster removal indicated attached material. The data showed a complete correspondence
between the SLDV data and detachment.
DRMS was also conducted at locations where the SLDV indicated low mobility but Sounding indicated higher
mobility. At these locations, no detachment was observed using DRMS. Instead, these samples showed a weakening
of plaster just before reaching the brick vault, which could indicate compromised plaster at the interface between
plaster and brick. This was observed in Samples 10, 12, and 13 (Figure 34 & 35). Most of the locations where this
was observed also showed signs of water infiltration on the surface of the plaster (Figure 16). It is possible that
moisture infiltration entering the plaster from the brick affected or washed out the lime of the plaster, causing this
section of the plaster depth to weaken in strength. Additionally, it is also possible that soluble salts carried into the
plaster from moisture infiltrating through the brick caused the weakening of the plaster closer to the brick, and then
traveled to the surface to form visible efflorescence. At this point in time, no previous or ongoing cause of moisture
infiltration was identified. Due to conditions noted above, it is likely that the cause is not due to surface condensation
but likely a source entering the plaster via the brick vault.
Differences between Sounding and SLDV maps could also be caused by the wide range of plaster depth that
was observed through DRMS. The plaster depth would affect both the sound observed when Sounding and the
frequency at which the plaster moves during SLDV.
If the detached plaster is assumed to be the condition at all locations where SLDV indicated mobility, then a
significant portion of the ceiling is detached from the brick substrate. At all locations where DRMS indicated
detachment, the detachment occurred between the brick and base coat of plaster. It is important to recognize
that DRMS was only conducted at the trompe-l’oeil architectural borders. While some SLDV anomalies correspond
to giornata (cold joints), there was no perimeter cracking around “islands” of suspected detachment. This
suggests that though substantial
INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Page 38 of 383

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

portions of the ceiling are detached, the plaster shell is holding itself in compression. Detachment of individual giornata
could not be investigated because DRMS was not conducted within original fresco areas. No conclusions can be made
about possible delamination between fresco layers within the original fresco giornata. Before any intervention could
take place, a probe or DRMS would need to be conducted at the frescos to determine where detachment has occurred
in the cross-section of the plaster layers.
The President’s Room has been investigated a number of times in the last thirty years. Sounding was conducted in
1994, which can be compared to sounding form 2023.
The two iterations of sounding display many similarities. Large portions of the ceiling that produced a hollow sound
registered similarly thirty years ago. This could indicate some level of stability in the conditions, but there here has
been an extension of some high mobility regions over time. There was an earthquake in 2011, which could have made
the ceiling conditions worse (Figure 37).

Figure 37. 2023 Sounding map produced by ANA (left) indicating attachment in blue and detached or compromised plaster in the
orange and red space. 1994 Sounding map produced by Cunningham-Adams (right) indicating hollow sounding areas in red and attached
areas in green.

Similarly, SLDV was conducted in 2004, which can be compared to the data from Phase 1. The two SLDV campaigns
used the same fundamental technology, but the 2023 campaign could achieve scans with higher point density, larger
scan regions, and higher resolution. The 2004 data was plotted over a larger frequency range, producing a noiser map
and making it impossible to compare the change in magnitude of mobility between each campaign. Comparing the
location of anomalies is more feasible, and this exercise indicated that many of the same areas of the ceiling display
hightened mobility, and there may be worsening conditions in select locations. The major similarities of mapping
overtime for both Sounding and SLDV reinforces the replicable quality of both NDE techniques for monitoring plaster
condition (Figure 38).
Plaster analysis from all three samples was consistent, indicating that the plaster mix was likely the same across the
whole ceiling but this has not been confirmed since samples were only removed from the trompe l’oeil architectural
border. It is important to note again that samples were only removed from the trompe-l’oeil architectural border. Any
difference in the plaster finish at the original frescos was not investigated. The petrography and chemical analysis
concluded that the plaster in the President’s Room did not show any differences based on the mobility detected in NDE,
but that the mix has deficiencies. These include the lack of reinforcement (using fibers or hair), an intermediate lime,
and slightly narrow grading of sand. These deficiencies identify the plaster as more vulnerable to outside damaging
factors than what would typically be expected.

Page 39 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Figure 38. 2023 SLDV mapping produced by CUA (left) indicating detachment in red and attachment in blue. 2003 SLDV mapping
produced by CUA (right) indicating detachment in red and green and attached plaster in blue. Boxed areas highlight regions where SLDV
indicates worsening conditions since 2003.

There is little correlation between the plaster conditions observed and the condition of the finishes in the President’s
Room. While cracking is observed on the surface of the frescos and surrounding trompe-l’oeil, there is little paint
loss associated with the cracks. The fresco surfaces are in relatively good condition, but he trompe-l’oeil architectural
borders are in worse condition, displaying blind cleavage, active flaking, and paint loss. Based on the reveals conducted
in 2024, the overpaint would be extremely difficult to remove. Any future intervention to the plaster substrate will need
to consider the impact on the surfaces of the finishes.

Page 40 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

IV. RECOMMENDATIONS
The investigations of the Team have confirmed that there are significant issues of concern at the plaster ceiling. Based on
the findings outlined above, ICR believes there is large-scale detachment of the plaster ceiling from the brick substrate,
and this is an urgent-level concern. However, the cause of issues relating to detachment and compromised plaster have
not been identified. It is essential to understand if there are ongoing conditions actively affecting the plaster ceiling
(as well as the brick vault above) prior to providing remedial recommendations to ensure that all outside factors are
addressed and that all causes of decay have been repaired. In light of this, ICR would like to reiterate the need for
certain investigations previously discussed with the AOC team that would allow for confirmation of potential factors
affecting the ceiling. While these investigations are being performed, intermediate actions are also recommended.
Vibration and environmental monitoring are pivotal for these investigations, and we recommend installation of vibration
and environemental monitoring systems to the underside of the plaster ceiliing immmedately. Given the amount of
detached plaster we believe to be present, after two complete months of monitoring the team recommends prioritizing
safety and preservation of the ceiling by installing a shoring system to support the ceiling in place until repairs can be
implemented. This ensures both lowest safety risk to users of the room, as well as lowest risk of ongoing or continued
damage to the ceiling. Allowing existing unsupported detachment at the plaster ceiling may result in an increase in the
extent of areas of concern and potentially even loss of ceiling sections. A shoring system can be designed to support
the plaster ceiling and not damage the plaster finishes. It may be possible to provide a system that can be installed atop
a scaffold dancefloor, allowing for partial use of the room below this protected scaffold. Additionally, this would allow
for necessary time to perform investigations needed to design a long-term repair approach. In the interest of safety and
protection of the ceiling we recommend that the vibration and environmental monitoring be in place for two months
before the shoring is installed. This will allow the team to obtain at least the minimum of unobstructed data.
After the two initial months of monitoring, the team recommends increasing the extent of the initial monitoring program
to the brick vault space above the ceiling. This would also include performing Ground Penetrating Radar (GPR) at the
floor of the Press Room above the Presidents Room as well as vibrational and environmental monitoring in the brick
vault above the ceiling. This GPR will provide information on the layout of conduit and any voids in the brick vault, which
will inform specific locations for vibration and environmental monitors. Vibration monitoring will be performed at both
the plaster and the brick vault. Environmental monitoring will be performed via moisture sensors at select locations on
the plaster ceiling, in the brick vault, as well as temperature and relative humidity sensors through the space. Specifics
of monitoring system, including design of monitors, locations, and install approaches to be designed in a separate
campaign.
Upon completion of the monitoring program, an engineering analysis of the vault and plaster system using finite
element analysis (FEA) should be performed. The model would be used to understand the structural response of the
vault and plaster assemblage to varying environmental conditions and to understand how the partially bonded ceiling
is functioning structurally with the masonry vault. This model would serve to map potential locations of detachment
and deflection within the ceiling. This analysis would confirm and expand on the currently extrapolated information
regarding detachment locations, which would allow for a more precise approach to future stabilization and conservation.
Figure 39 below provides a recommended sequence and approximate duration for the investigative program as well
as design of the shoring system.

Page 41 of 383

INTEGRATED CONSERVATION RESOURCES, INC.

Phase 1-4 Report: President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

Figure 39. Recommended sequence for next phase of work.

If further investigative methods confirm that factors causing the plaster conditions are not ongoing, ICR can begin
to design potential treatment repairs and mock-ups to re-attach and stabilize the detached plaster to the brick vault
above.
Additionally, ICR is concerned that no physical evaluation data has been collected at the frescoes and cannot confirm
that correlations drawn between NDE and physical evaluations can be applied to the fresco areas. It is noted that
the giornata (cold joints) of the frescoes often line up with borders of potentially detached plaster, signifying that
some relationship is occurring between the frescoes and detachment pattern. Additionally, this would provide crucial
information for potential plaster repairs at the fresco locations.
The recommendations noted above outline a holistic approach to understanding the functionality of the plaster ceiling
vault in the President’s Room in sufficient detail for both the design of restoration repairs and the maintenance and use
of adjacent spaces to ensure longevity of this historic space.
If given direction to proceed, ICR will develop and submit a scope of work to design and install the above scope of work.

Jenn,
So I want to report back to you with regard to what I’ve found as I take a closer look at the
pointcloud/3D scan of the ceiling and its mid 19th century ceiling fresco by Constantino Brumidi.
For the most part my area of interest to report back to you is concerned with the existing
conditions of the architectural form of the ceiling. There is no way to compare it’s existing
condition to how it appeared soon after its original construction, but the current set of data that
we are collecting will provide a solid benchmark for similar investigations in future. But what more
can these existing conditions tell us in light of other findings that have been documented these past
few months?
ICR provided me with three sets of documentation to help direct my inquiry. These are the
mapping of visible cracks in the fresco, sounding, and vibrometry. Below are snapshots of each:

Page 1
Page 81 of 383

Aaslestad Preservation Consulting LLC PO Box 176 Staunton, VA 24402 aaslestad.com

Figure 2 Sounding

Page 82 of 383

Aaslestad Preservation Consulting LLC PO Box 176 Staunton, VA 24402 aaslestad.com

Figure 3 Vibrometry
What can the 3D scans tell us of the nature of existing conditions vis a vis these findings? Is there a
correspondence between local material degradation in aggregate and the overall form of the ceiling?
To understand the precise form of the ceiling I decided to look at it in terms of its form/shape
without the RGB pixel data related to the painting’s imagery - which is very effective in fooling the
eye as to the flatness or smoothness of the surface.
So I set up a view of the pointcloud using a gradient where the colors correspond to elevation.
Below is a look at my initial findings in plan and elevation. (The elevation drawing shows the color
change in elevation where blue is the lowest blending across the spectrum until we arrive at red, the
highest.
This shows a very smooth and well formed dome:

At this point I realized that what might best demonstrate the character of this well formed dome
would be a topographic map type description of the shape documented by the pointcloud. And so
my final analytical image of the point cloud is rather high resolution topographic map where each

Full size copies of these images are available in the dropbox folder along with other material that I
produced along the way, searching for any sign of deformation. But, my analysis just kept
confirming that the domed ceiling seems to be very smooth and well formed without sign of
correspondence to systemic structural displacement beneath the surface of the fresco.
We might want to look further together if you think I might be missing something? I think that the
cracking itself is beyond the resolution of the laser scan. Better said: the laser scan confirms the
presence and severity of cracking on the painted surface but does not register any effect of those
cracks on the overall form. I could look farther here with higher resolution scans from the
photogrammetry. But I don't anticipate learning much more in that way… let's talk after you have
had a chance to review this document and the materials shared in dropbox.

GIANFRANCO POCOBENE STUDIO
July 31, 2024
Painting Condition Assessment Report
President’s Room Ceiling Murals
United States Capitol
Introduction
This report describes the findings of the on-site examination of Constantino Brumidi’s President’s Room
ceiling murals carried out on September 11-13, 2023 (Phase 1), and March 27-29, 2024 (Phase 3), at the
United States Capitol and focuses on the painted decorations that adorn the ceiling. The work was
performed by Gianfranco Pocobene and Corrine Long at the request of Integrated Conservation
Resources. The observations made during these examinations describe the painting techniques, past
restorations, and the various condition issues that presently afflict portions of the ceiling decorations.
Recommendations to address observed condition issues are also presented for consideration.
Visual examination of the decorative paint surfaces was undertaken to determine and document the
artist’s materials and technique (including the use of fresco, oil, tempera, glazing, gilding etc.) and the
locations and boundaries of each type of paint finish employed throughout the room. The condition of the
painted surfaces was examined to identify areas of paint instability such as powdering, inter-layer
delamination, cracking and flaking of the paint layers. The paint surfaces were examined using specular
light, raking light, ultraviolet light (366 nm wavelength) and a low magnification loop. The artist’s
technique and paint conditions were photo-documented with digital images using a Sony Alpha a7R III
Mirrorless Camera and Samsung S22 cell phone. Images that document the condition of the murals along
with mapping of various condition issues are included in the report.
Aqueous and solvent tests were performed on the selected areas of the painted surfaces to determine the
degree of soot and grime build up, the presence and location of previous restorations/overpaint and any
non-original materials such as consolidation and varnish resins. The tests were carried out in a discreet
manner using rolled cotton swabs on bamboo sticks. Aqueous cleaning solutions such as diammonium
citrate at pH 7.0 were tested to determine the efficacy of surface cleaning. A range of solvent/mixtures of
odorless mineral spirits, isopropyl alcohol, ethanol, xylenes, and acetone were employed to assess paint
solubility and potential for the removal of restorations and overpaint. At the suggestion of Barbara
Ramsay, Christina Jackson was contracted to conduct overpaint removal tests during the second site visit
(See Jackson Art Care, LLC., Brief Study of Decorative Paint Finishes Report, March 28, 2024).
With the approval of the Architect of the US Capitol, six (6) paint samples were extracted for paint
analysis using surgical scalpels during the first site visit (Phase 1 - September 11-13, 2023). Sample
locations were photo-documented and secured in sample vials. The paint samples (cross-sections and
scrapings) were delivered to Erin Mysak, PhD who conducted analysis including cross sectional
microscopy, scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and medium/pigment identification using FT-IR,
Raman, and SEM. During the second site visit (Phase 2 - March 27-29, 2024), and with the approval of
the AOC and review committee, nine (9) more samples were taken by Erin Mysak for further analysis and
clarification of the earlier findings.
Cunningham-Adams Conservation Treatment – 1994-95
The Cunningham-Adams Conservation Treatment Report (Part I & II) dated April 30, 1995, is a
significant and useful resource that details the findings made during the examination and treatment of
ceiling murals from August through December 1994. The reader is urged to review the CunninghamAdams report for the specific information about the state of the murals in the early 1990s, and other topics
1
Page 90 of 383

Gianfranco Pocobene Studio, Paint Assessment Report
President’s Room Ceiling, US Capitol

including descriptions of the subjects and historical sources that Brumidi painted, execution technique,
previous treatment history and the conservation treatment.
The most pertinent discoveries made during the Cunningham-Adams conservation treatment can be
summarized as follows:
1. In Brumidi’s original execution, the rectangular, medallion-shaped and triangular panels
containing the figurative allegorical scenes, various portraits and putti were rendered in a fresco
technique which is executed by painting pigments mixed in water directly onto sections of damp
plaster composed of slaked lime and sand.
2. The trompe-l’oeil architectural framework surrounding the fresco panels was originally executed
in what is described as “tempera” with gold leaf accent bands.
3. The fresco paintings were found to be largely intact and generally stable, but up to 10% of the
surfaces exhibited blind cleavage and flaking. These surfaces had also been retouched during the
earlier restorations.
4. The trompe-l’oeil architectural framework was extensively repainted with casein and oil during
two of the restoration campaigns carried out after 1900. This dramatically altered the relationship
between the figurative and the architectural elements of the murals, most notably the marked shift
of the trompe-l’oeil architectural framework from a cool palette to a warmer one. The restoration
overpaint has higher contrast than the original which had a softer more luminous appearance.
5. The trompe-l’oeil architectural framework decorations were found to be in poor condition with
about 75% of the surfaces suffering from active flaking and blind cleavage.
6. In 1921, the complete detachment of the plaster in the center octagonal medallion resulted in the
destruction of the fresco above the chandelier.
7. In 1931, the putti fresco below the Brewster panel at the northeast corner was damaged by water
infiltration and thus entirely replastered and repainted by Comins using the fresco technique.
The conservation treatment undertaken by Cunningham-Adams sought to address the instability of the
painted surfaces, and the cleaning and inpainting of losses predominantly in the fresco panels.
Areas of blind cleavage and flaking in the fresco portions were infused with Acryloid B-72 (ethyl
methacrylate methyl acrylate copolymer) to re-establish contact between the paint layers and underlying
plaster reattach large lifting paint flakes. In the trompe-l’oeil architectural framework decorations, BEVA
D-8 (ethylene vinyl acetate dispersion) was used to consolidate and reattach the flaking paint.
Surface grime was lightly dusted from the murals and all stable paint surfaces were cleaned with plain
water followed by a “mild solution of trisodium phosphate and rinsing with water. Dimethylformamide
and acetone solvents were used to remove oil restorations on the fresco surfaces”.
Exposure windows to reveal the original tempera paint in the trompe-l’oeil architectural framework were
made with methylene chloride free commercial paint stripper then rinsed with lacquer thinner. Losses
were toned and in-painted with watercolors.
2
Page 91 of 383

Gianfranco Pocobene Studio, Paint Assessment Report
President’s Room Ceiling, US Capitol

Technique and Condition of the Mural Paintings
Examination of the ceiling murals revealed that the original fresco murals, which include the rectangular,
medallions and triangular panels decorated with figurative allegorical scenes, portraits and putti are
generally in sound condition with only minor areas of instability. Scattered retouching was observed on
several of the fresco murals with much of it still reasonably matching the original pigments.
By contrast, the trompe-l’oeil architectural framework surrounding the fresco panels is in a far less stable
condition. These surfaces exhibit extensive and scattered areas of blind cleavage and active flaking,
although thus far, relatively little paint loss has occurred as a result of the instability.

Original and intact Brumidi fresco panels highlighted in yellow. Unmarked areas represent repainted/nonoriginal trompe-l’oeil architectural framework decorations executed after 1900.

3
Page 92 of 383

Gianfranco Pocobene Studio, Paint Assessment Report
President’s Room Ceiling, US Capitol

Christopher Columbus Fresco (approximately 60” x 37”) - Southeast Corner
The fresco, which is composed of four giornate (a day’s
worth of painting on plaster section before it dries), is in a
very good state of preservation. Incision marks made into
the wet plaster to define contours during execution are
readily evident, especially in the sitter’s mantle and the
instruments and column. The pigments were variably
applied to the plaster. Much of it is of moderate thickness
although throughout the sitter’s face, hands, mantle, and
highlights of the globe and other instruments the paint
exhibits high impasto. By contrast, thin translucent washes
of reddish-brown pigment in the form of sketchy hatching
were employed to create the shadows and forms of the face
and hands.
A few vertical cracks in the plaster, running predominantly
through the middle of the image from top to bottom, are
visible in the fresco but no active paint loss has occurred
along the cracks. The painted surface has good cohesion
and is well adhered to the plaster substrate with only very
minor losses present. Scattered retouching of losses, which
adequately match the original, is evident on the surface,
especially in the brown background.
A thin layer of surface grime/dust is present on the fresco surface, but this does not darken the mural’s
appearance to any appreciable degree. Light wiping with cosmetic sponges is effective

Analytical Research
Sample PR 04 Location
The Cunningham Adams Report (page 18)
states that “Brumidi frequently creates high
impasto, both by loading his brush with a
dense compound of pigments and water (the
“paint”), and by adding condensed slaked lime
to his pigment and water mixture”. A scraping
from an area of pink impasto was taken for
analysis to determine the technique used by
the artist to create this effect (See Sample PR
04, Analytical Report for findings).

Benjamín Franklin Fresco (approximately 58¾” x 33½”) – Southwest Corner
The fresco is composed of four giornate, the contours of
which are readily visible. Overall, it is in a good state of
preservation with no active powdering or flaking of the
paint film. The pigments are generally of moderate
thickness but there is impasto and notable brushwork in
the face, hands, and clothing of the figure.
There are two large losses in the brown background above
the sitter’s head which were previously restored, possibly
in oils. The curved upper portion of the brown niche also
appears to have been evened out with a thin layer of oil.
Some vertical cracks in the plaster, running predominantly
through the middle of the image from top to bottom, are
visible in the fresco with only very minor, sporadic losses
along the cracks. The painted surface has good cohesion
and is well adhered to the plaster substrate. There are
numerous scattered areas of retouched losses in the brown
background.

William Brewster Fresco (approximately 58” x 33½”) - Northeast Corner
Four giornate make up the fresco image, the contours of
which are readily visible on close inspection. There is a ½”
wide hole at the top middle edge of the curved ledge that
appears to be original and may be a compass hole used to
mark the curved faux framework above the fresco. Overall,
the paint layers are in a good state of preservation with no
apparent powdering or flaking of the paint film. The
pigments are generally of moderate thickness but as with the
frescoes, there is impasto and notable brushwork in the face,
hands, bible, the figure’s clothing.
The curved upper portion of the brown niche and the ledge at
the bottom of the image appear to have been overpainted
with pigments in a drying oil medium. A few vertical cracks
in the plaster, running through the middle of the image from
top to bottom, are visible in the fresco, most prominently in
the sitter’s face. No significant paint losses are present along
the cracks. The painted surface has good cohesion and is
well adhered to the plaster substrate. The brown background
has a mottled appearance, possibly due to execution issues
and the scattered retouches that are present.

Amerigo Vespucci Fresco (approximately 58” x 33¾”) - Northwest Corner
The fresco, which is composed of four giornate, is in a fair
to good state of preservation. Incision marks made into the
wet plaster to define contours during execution are visible
in the lower portion of the figure, but not as readily
evident as in some of the other frescoes. The pigments
were applied in a sketchy manner to the plaster and much
of it is of moderate thickness, although throughout the
features of the sitter and instruments, higher impasto is
present.
Several vertical cracks in the plaster, running
predominantly through the middle of the image from top to
bottom, are visible in the fresco, especially at the bottom.
No active paint loss is present along the cracks. Just above
the sitter’s forehead there is a 1” wide triangular shaped
area of lifting paint that appears to be an old fill/restoration
that has come loose. The painted surface has suffered from
poor cohesion in the past as scattered retouching of losses
is evident on the surface, especially in the brown
background and the curved ledge at the bottom of the
image. Many of the old restorations were removed and
damages were reintegrated by Cunningham Adams during
the 1994 conservation treatment. In the lower section of the mural there is uneven application of a varnish
or adhesive that gives the surface an uneven appearance in specular light. Minimal surface dust/grime is
present, but it does not darken the surface.

Specular light detail showing uneven filling of losses and scattered restorations and minor areas of lifting
of repaint.

Executive Authority Fresco Medallion (46½” diameter) – Center East Side
The fresco is made up of five giorante, although the
giornata that includes the head and sky above it was
cut out and reworked by Brumidi as evidenced by
slight color mismatch with the rest of the image. A
fine, random network of cracks in the plaster are
visible in the sky and through the sitter’s knees.
Cunningham Adams indicated that prior to the 1994
treatment, the painted surface exhibited variable
conditions ranging from good cohesion and adhesion
to poor in other areas. Overall, the painted surface is
presently stable and there are no obvious signs of
active flaking.
The paint layers range from thicker impasto and
notable brushwork in the highlights of the figures and
sky to thin translucent washes and hatching in the
shadows and modelling of the faces for example.
There is very little surface grime or dust on the paint
surface.

Detail showing the giornata reworked by Brumidi that includes the face and sky above it.

Liberty Fresco Medallion (46½” diameter) - Center South Side
The fresco is composed of four giornate the joins of
which are readily evident. Overall, the paint surface is
in excellent condition. A fine, random network of
cracks in the plaster are visible predominately
throughout the center of the image. Slight darkening
of the paint has occurred along the cracks but this is
not aesthetically distracting. Cunningham Adams
reported that the plaster support is compromised by
weakness and poor attachment, although no losses or
flaking have occurred.
Incision marks in the plaster to delineate the form are
present but mostly covered by moderate to thick paint
application. The paint layers are applied in various
methods ranging from thin, translucent hatch marks in
the modeling of the figures, to moderate layers and
finally thicker impasto layers in the highlights of the
faces, drapery, and white stars along the edge of the
blue sky. Note the dramatic use of ultramarine blue in the shield held by the putto on the right. There is
14
Page 103 of 383

Gianfranco Pocobene Studio, Paint Assessment Report
President’s Room Ceiling, US Capitol

very minimal grime on the paint surface and some loose dust hangs from the textured plaster, but this is
easily removed by dry cleaning using cosmetic sponges.

Detail of Liberty showing network of plaster cracks, thin translucent brushwork in the hair and thicker
impasto paint in the highlights of the face and drapery.

Legislation Fresco Medallion (47” diameter) – Center West Side
Brumidi executed the fresco in four giorante. A
network or random cracks run through the
composition, with a slightly wider horizontal crack on
the left and one on the right. Overall, the painted
surface is stable and well preserved. Incision marks
made into the wet plaster to lay out the composition
are evident throughout. Puntini (charcoal transfer
marks) are also clearly visible along the top of the
heads of the puttos on the right. This indicates that
Brumidi employed cartoons through which the
composition was transferred onto the wet plaster
through pricked holes in the drawing with spolvero
(charcoal).
As with the other frescoes, the paint layers are applied
in various methods ranging from thin, translucent
hatch marks in the modelling of the figures, to
moderate layers and finally thicker brush layers in the
highlights of the faces, drapery. The impasto is particularly high on the clouds. There are two obvious
restored losses in the fresco, one on the right side of the chest (2” x 2”) and one in the knee (1½” x 3”).
Under ultraviolet light, scattered retouches are revealed along cracks and minor losses but in normal light
match the surrounding paint. At the left side especially, the retouches cover abrasions to the paint.
Some efflorescence is present along the right edge and above the figure’s head to the left which suggests
that soluble components of the plaster substrate are being released through the paint surface. Minimal
surface grime is present but easily removed with dry cleaning methods.

Detail showing plaster cracks and range of paint thickness from thin layer in brown bands in the dress to
impasto in the faces and clouds.

Page 105 of 383

Gianfranco Pocobene Studio, Paint Assessment Report
President’s Room Ceiling, US Capitol

Puntini or spolvero transfer marks made through pricked holes in the cartoon are evident along the top of
the putto heads.

Ultraviolet induced fluorescence image showing scattered retouching of abrasion at left edge.

Religion Fresco Medallion (46½” diameter) – Center North Side
Originally, the fresco may have been composed of
four giornate, but with the reworking of the section
that contained the head and sky above it, Brumidi
appears to have created two giornate in that area. The
reworked giornate are somewhat mismatched from the
rest of the painted surface. Cracks in the plaster are
random across the surface and most noticeable in the
lower portion.
The painted surface of the fresco is in excellent
condition and one of the best preserved of the entire
mural cycle. Minor areas of retouching are present
along some of the cracks and in the sky, but these are
only noticeable under ultraviolet light. A light layer of
surface grime is present on the fresco although this
does not darken the image to any appreciable degree.
The only condition issue of note is the presence of
some efflorescence in the sky, just above
the left and right of the female figure’s
head. The cause of this condition issue,
which was also discovered in an area of
the restored decorative framework
decoration (see page 39), is unknown
but it is possible that moisture is moving
through the plaster. Here, the white
effloresce layer does not appear to have
damaged the fresco surface to any
appreciable degree. In any event this
problem is not a widespread problem in
the ceiling and was only observed in a
few isolated areas.
It is recommended that the efflorescence
be removed from the mural and
decorative paint surfaces by means of

and removal of overpaint, a dilute layer of Acryloid B72 was applied to consolidate the surfaces. Some

Overall, the triangular shaped fresco panels are in good condition with no apparent signs of instability.

are no obvious signs of paint loss. The fresco with three putti below the Brewster panel at the northwest
corner is not by Brumidi and not part of the original decoration. Significant damage from water
infiltration in the 1920s in that corner of the ceiling necessitated a complete repainting of the panel
although it is not an accurate restoration of what Brumidi painted. The present image was entirely
repainted in fresco in 1931 by Comins. In addition to not being an accurate restoration of the original
composition, the painting technique is not of the quality employed by Brumidi.

Trompe l’oeil Architectural Framework
The trompe l’oeil architectural framework that embellishes the original Brumidi rectangular, medallions
and triangular fresco panels containing the figurative allegorical scenes, various portraits and putti is not
original. Overpaint removal windows made by Cunningham Adams in 1994 (see map below) revealed
that Brumidi’s original decorations were executed in a water-soluble tempera on a plaster substrate
smoother than the fresco portions of the ceiling. After 1900, two extensive restoration campaigns were
carried out to repair deterioration problems in the trompe l’oeil architectural framework. The first
restoration was apparently executed in casein and the second in oils. These surfaces are completely
overpainted, especially the flat, non-ornate paint areas. Some areas, such as the egg and dart bands and
bands of floral decorations have more localized overpaint applied directly over the original with an
intermediate priming layer. Because of this, minimal amounts of the original design appear to show
through in places but are not easily differentiated, especially from a distance.
Overall, the restoration overpaint colors are saturated creating higher contrast between the darks and
lights in the tromp l’oeil. The tone is also warmer in color than the original tempera surfaces which are a
cool gray tone rather than the present beige/cream. This creates a visual dissonance between the nonoriginal decorations and the fresco surfaces which have a light luminous quality. Solubility tests carried
out on the oil overpaint indicate that it is highly polymerized and solvents such as ethanol and acetone had
little to no effect on the surface. Slight dissolution was possible with benzyl alcohol, but this was slow
and only marred the upper layer of oil paint. Solvent gels containing acetone and alcohol were somewhat
more effective but very slow. Note that Cunningham Adams employed Zip Strip Pant and Varnish
Remover, a commercial paint stripper to make the reveals, which is considered aggressive because of the
likelihood of damage to the underlying original paint. Abrasions marks and scratches visible in the
exposures she made may also have been caused by sanding of the surface prior to the wholesale
restoration campaigns.
The gilt portions of the ceiling decorations appear to have been applied with a drying oil mordant. The oil
gilding surface also appears to have a glaze on the surface that is water soluble ostensibly to tone the
sheen down and give it an aged appearance. Removal of the matte surface reveals shiny bright gold. The
gold has been completely re-gilt and it is highly unlikely that the original gold leaf can be uncovered
without significantly damaging its thin surface.
While many portions of the trompe l’oeil architectural decorations are intact and stable, in many passages
there are severe condition problems. This includes localized but severe areas of blind cleavage, active
flaking and some paint loss. This has been an ongoing problem as is evidenced by numerous lacunae in
the underlying paint layers that show through the present repainted decoration. Poor preparation of the
surfaces prior to repainting would be one cause of this. Another possibility is that the moisture moving
through the walls cannot migrate through the overpaint layers and is causing the oil and casein paint
layers to blister. Over many of the trompe l’oeil decorations, especially the triangular shaped faux
stonework along the outer edges of the ceiling, shiny, uneven layers of what is assumed to be a
consolidation resin is visible in specular light. These materials are slightly yellow and sticky and despite
previous stabilization efforts, flaking is active in these areas. Note that previous treatments employed
BEVA D-8 (ethylene vinyl acetate dispersion) and Rhoplex to stabilize flaking paint an multiple thin
applications of Acryloid B-72 were applied to the frescos to even out their surface. In a few passages,
namely in the Religion fresco and trompe l’oeil architectural decorations below the Executive Authority
fresco, a thin white film of efflorescence is present, but it is not extensive problem by any means.
Unlike the rest of the overpainted trompe l’oeil architectural decorations, the small sienna and ochrecolored medallions containing various symbols and landscapes that punctuate the floral bands around the
frescoes are water soluble. Analysis of a sample taken from one of the medallions has not confirmed if the
medallions are original or not and further investigation is warranted (see Sample PR 03, Analytical
24
Page 113 of 383

Gianfranco Pocobene Studio, Paint Assessment Report
President’s Room Ceiling, US Capitol

Report). Most of the medallions are in good condition but a few of them exhibit minor, pinpoint flaking
and paint loss, some of which was in-painting in the past.

Summary of Analytical Findings
The analytical work performed by Erin Mysak (see Analysis of Brumidi Ceiling Murals, President’s
Room, Capitol Building Report for comprehensive findings) confirms that Brumidi executed the fresco
portions of the ceiling in a traditional, buon fresco technique, namely a thick arriccio plaster layer
composed of calcium carbonate with quartz inclusions (sand) and a thinner intonaco layer that exhibits
even spreading of fine pigments into the top layer of plaster.
A scraping of an original decorative band to the left of the Executive Authority fresco uncovered
through overpaint removal to the left of the Executive Authority fresco, was analyzed to determine the
composition of the original binding medium but this was unsuccessful due to the preponderance of
gypsum in the sample that overwhelmed the signal. It is also likely that the amount of protein binder
present in the remaining paint film is below the detection limits of the analytical instruments. It is also
likely that after 165 years, the binder has deteriorated significantly making it less easy to detect.
The samples taken from the heavily restored framework decorations confirm the presence of non-original
paint layers, namely drying oil bound paints which are now polymerized and not easily solubilized. The
thickness of the overpaint layers built up over the original paint layers indicate that in some areas more
than one campaign of restoration is present. Medium analysis of the restorations is also complicated by
the presence of the consolidation adhesives that were applied to the decorative paint surfaces during the
previous restorations, including polyvinyl acetate (PVA), Acryloid B-72 and BEVA D8 resins.
Analysis of the efflorescence scrapings from two locations (the Religion fresco and trompe l’oeil
architectural decorations below the Executive Authority fresco) confirms the thin white accretions that
have formed on the paint surfaces are calcium (Ca) and sodium (Na) based compounds (carbonates and/or
oxalates). A cross-section taken in an area of efflorescence in the background of the Religion fresco
indicates that the efflorescence grains are present only on the paint surface and not within the structure of
the fresco itself. The cause of the efflorescence is not clear at this time and may simply be a reaction of air
46
Page 135 of 383

Gianfranco Pocobene Studio, Paint Assessment Report
President’s Room Ceiling, US Capitol

born moisture with the paint components. Most reassuring is the fact that the efflorescence does not
appear the be caused by water infiltration or leaks from above migrating through the ceiling structure.
A cross-section taken from an area of gold leaf in the decorative band to the right of the bottom right
corner of the Benjamin Franklin fresco reveals the presence of four layers of gold leaf and confirms, as
suspected, that these portions of the trompe l’oeil architectural decorations have also undergone extensive
restoration and regilded in the past.
Summary of Overpaint Removal Tests in the Trompe l’oeil Architectural Framework
In 1994-95. Cunningham-Adams made several exposures over portions of the trompe l’oeil architectural
decorations (see images and the exposures map above) using a methylene chloride free commercial paint
stripper to reveal the original distemper surfaces. For the most part, the exposures were performed on the
flat, stone bands that frame the murals. Overpaint removal tests were done during the Phase 1 site work
using a range of solvents and modern solvent gels. While the solvent gels used (combinations of acetone,
ethanol and xylenes) did cut through upper layers of overpaint, the initial tests indicated that the
restoration overpaint layers (confirmed through analysis to be pigments in a drying oil binder) were
highly polymerized and strongly resistant to the solvent/solvent gels tested.
During the Phase 2 committee meeting it was agreed that compete removal of the tenacious restoration
overpaint covering the trompe l’oeil architectural decorations would be extremely difficult if not next to
impossible. Nonetheless, Barbara Ramsay suggested that another round of tests be made with more
delicate solvent methods and recommended that we contract Christina Jackson, who has worked on paint
finishes at the US Capitol, to assist with the tests. This would enable us to compare our tests to the
Cunningham-Adams exposures and provide a better sense of the complexity and possibilities such an
undertaking would entail should any further testing/restoration be envisioned in the future.
On March 28, 2024, Jackson carried out a solvent gel test on a portion of a light gray, faux molding band
to the left of the Executive Authority fresco. The site was chosen because of the apparent stability of the
oil restoration layers and the underlying original distemper paint. Two smaller tests on the faux frame
above the Brewster mural were also made, but these are partial because time did not allow for completion
of paint exposure. Below the chosen test area, a small overpaint removal window made by CunninghamAdams in 1994 would allow for a visual comparison of the two test areas.
Various solvent and thickening gel materials were tested including a commercial paint stripper - Blue
Bear Soy Gel Paint and Urethane Stripper (N-methyl 2 pyrrolidone, dibasic ester-LVP, soy methyl ester,
cellulose ether powder, surfactant, and fumed silica). For more details about the testing procedures, see
Jackson Art Care, LLC., Conservation Report: Brief Study of Decorative Paint Finishes, March 28,
2024). The results of the investigation indicated that the Blue Bear Soy Gel was the most effective and
controllable of the materials tested although an application time of 40 - 60 minutes was needed to soften
and remove the overpaint although second and third applications of another 15-20 minutes each were
needed for complete removal of the overpaint (see image below). The use of this product, however, was
not without its risks as the strong bond between the original paint surface and the overpaint restoration
layers necessitated that the gel remain on the surface for prolonged times, ultimately resulting in softening
and solubilizing of the thin original paint decoration.
A comparison of the 1994 Cunningham-Adams test window with the present overpaint removal indicates
that although slow and potentially tricky, modern gel formulations offer the ability to remove the
overpaint layers in a somewhat safer manner. Of note is the considerable difference in the appearance of
the two exposures. As can be seen in the following image, the 1994 exposure is lighter tone and more
chalky appearance, most likely due to the aggressive characteristics of the Zip Strip. Application of a slow
47
Page 136 of 383

Gianfranco Pocobene Studio, Paint Assessment Report
President’s Room Ceiling, US Capitol

drying solvent (Odorless Mineral Spirits) to re-saturate the chalky surface indicated that it may be darker
and richer in tone than it now appears. By contrast, the exposure made by Jackson suggests that the
original decorative surface in this area is not as cool in tone as previously thought and that it is warmer
and more contrasty. While the overpaint removal test made by Jackson is notably different from the 1994
exposure, a more comprehensive round of testing with a wider range of solvent gel formulations is
advisable to confirm the present result. We should also remain mindful of the fact that oil bound paint
layers darken in the absence of light and thus the exposure may not actually accurately represent original
appearance of the decorative paint layers.

48
Page 137 of 383

Gianfranco Pocobene Studio, Paint Assessment Report
President’s Room Ceiling, US Capitol

Jackson 2024 exposure window at top with Cunningham-Adams 1994 exposure window at the bottom of
the image. Note the more intense warm color of the top exposure.

Saturating a small area of a Cunningham-Adams paint exposure with odorless mineral spirits suggests
that the surface was made chalky by the Zip Strip (through leaching of paint components) and/or has
become chalkier over time.

Observations and Recommendations
As has been noted throughout this report, the original Brumidi fresco portions of the decorations that
include the rectangular, medallions and triangular panels decorated with figurative allegorical scenes,
portraits and putti are in very stable condition and require minimal attention at this time. No instability
was found on the original fresco surfaces and the only condition that might be addressed is the formation
of thin layers of efflorescence on the Religion fresco and the restored trompe l’oeil architectural
decorations below the Authority fresco. The analysis conducted by Erin Mysak suggests that this is a
surface phenomenon and not an inherent problem within the paint/plaster structure or because of water
infiltration. At any rate, imaging and mapping of the problem areas performed in this study provides a
base line for monitoring the any changes to the surface efflorescence.
The more complicated issue in the ceiling decoration is the trompe l’oeil architectural framework, much
of it heavily restored after 1900 and which now exhibits scattered areas of instability including blind
cleavage, active flaking paint and some paint loss. As noted by Cunningham Adams and illustrated by the
overpaint removal windows performed in 1994, these restorations may not accurately replicate the color
and luminous quality of the original tempera paint employed by the artist. While the Cunningham-Adams
exposures suggest that the subsequent restorations are too warm in tone and highly reflective (shinny)
because of the medium rich oil paint that was used in the last restoration campaign, the Jackson 2024
49
Page 138 of 383

Gianfranco Pocobene Studio, Paint Assessment Report
President’s Room Ceiling, US Capitol

exposure appears to indicate that the original surface may be richer and warmer in tone. More
comprehensive exposures of other decorative elements should be considered in the future to accurately
determine the visual characteristics of the trompe l’oeil architectural decorations.
During the Phase 2 Advisory Committee meeting the following possible treatment approaches were
suggested to address the conservation/restoration of the trompe l’oeil decorations in the President’s Room
ceiling and they are worth repeating:
1. A minimal approach to treating the murals would aim to stabilize the areas of flaking paint, readhere loose paint and inpaint losses. This would strictly be a conservation project that leaves the
restored trompe l’oeil architectural framework decorations intact, especially if the Architects of
the Capitol and other government entities consider the present appearance of the mural
decorations to be aesthetically acceptable.
2. A more comprehensive approach would strive to remove all non-original, restoration paint layers
from the ceiling with the aim of uncovering the original trompe l’oeil architectural framework
decorations that were executed by Brumidi in tempera paint. This would take considerable time at
significant cost and with uncertain outcomes. Can an overpaint removal system be devised that
will safely reveal the original tempera paint layers that are extant below the overpaint? How
much of the original is present to be recovered and how much reintegration of losses and damage
would be needed to restore the framework decorations to their original appearance? In short, this
approach is probably unrealistic.
3. A third possibility is to leave the old trompe l’oeil architectural framework restoration in place
and have the decorations replicated by a qualified decorative paint company. Even if it is possible
to achieve a skillful restoration, the instability presently afflicting these surfaces may well recur
in the future because of the thick, multiple layers of overpaint that are present. In fact, the
addition of yet another layer of overpaint may well exacerbate the flaking paint problem.
The second round of on-site examination, testing and analysis suggests that at the present time the first
approach (minimal conservation intervention) is the most prudent. Given the size of the ceiling and the
low lighting levels emitted by the chandelier, most if not all the flaking and minor paint losses are not
readily visible to the casual visitor entering the room. Overall, the murals and decorative paint elements
hold together quite well visually and while paint stabilization and conservation is recommended, at
present, the ceiling is not in urgent need of a wholesale restoration. Regular inspections of the paint
surfaces are recommended in the future to monitor conditions and ensure that any of the relatively minor
problems observed in this study do not worsen.

Gianfranco Pocobene and Corrine Long

July 31, 2024

Analysis of Brumidi Murals
President’s Room, Capitol Building
Request submitted by Gianfranco Pocobene
Principal and Chief Conservator
Gianfranco Pocobene Studio
193 Maple Street, Malden MA 02148
Material: Historic murals, President’s Room, Capitol Building,
Washington DC
Manufacturer: Constantino Brumidi
Date: 1859-60
Analysis and report prepared by: Erin R. Mysak, Ph.D.
Report date: July 31st, 2024

Summary
Samples from the Brumidi Murals in the President’s Room were analyzed as part of current
conservation treatment of the murals. The goal of the scientific study is (i) to determine the composition
of the original layers (both the fresco and decorative borders), (ii) to determine the composition of
potential overpaint in some areas, (iii) to understand the layer stratigraphy of the original fresco and later
overpaint in select areas, (iv) to determine the composition of the efflorescence on the frescos and
decorative borders, and (v) to assess whether efflorescence is forming under or only on top of the surface.
The fifteen samples included seven cross-sections and eight scrapings. Analysis suggests that the
original material in the frescos consists of a thick layer of support (arriccio) composed of calcite with
quartz and carbon black inclusions, and one or two layers of fresco (intonaco) composed of calcite and
pigments including ultramarine, carbon black, and yellow and red iron oxides. The even dispersion of
pigment particles through the entire top layer indicates use of the buon fresco method. Small amounts of
gypsum and silicates are detected in the original fresco layers.
The decorative borders around the frescos were also analyzed, and the original layers contain the
same pigments as the frescos: ultramarine, carbon black, and yellow and red iron oxides. They are believed
to have been executed in paint such as tempera, however their medium identification is challenging due
to the overpaint that has been used to treat them previously. Gypsum is the main primary component of
the original layer of the decorative border; however, the decorative borders have been significantly
overpainted in past treatments. The overpaint contains pigments such as lead white, chrome yellow and
likely zinc white. Some of these pigments and likely the binding media have absorbed into the somewhat
porous original surface layer, likely having been carried into the original surface at the time of their
application. The preparatory layer for the decorative borders is calcite mixed with quartz inclusions.
Analysis reveals that the overpaint on the decorative borders consists of oil in most locations,
however, in one flaking area of the decorative border, polyvinyl acetate (PVA) resin was detected. Visual
observation suggests that oil was used in most places and that only smaller areas were painted or
consolidated with PVA. The overpaint color of the decorative border is a lighter and brighter yellow than
from its original color. In addition to repainting the decorative borders, it is likely that the golden borders
have been regilded. Analysis of the golden border shows that there are four layers of gold foil are present,
onto which decorative elements such as the green leaves were applied.

Page 140 of 383

Efflorescence from the surface of the frescos and decorative borders was analyzed. Analysis
indicates that the efflorescence is only present on top of the surface, meaning that there is no
subfluorescence, and crystals are not forming inside the paint or fresco layers. Analysis of the
efflorescence shows that the efflorescence on various frescos and multiple decorative borders all have the
same composition. In each location, the efflorescence composition is a mixture of calcium- and sodiumbased carbonates and/or oxalates.

Summary of sampling and experimental details
Seven cross-sections and eight scrapings were analyzed with various techniques to identify
pigments, binding medium, and paint layer stratigraphy. In a cross-section, the top paint layer through the
bottom support preparation layer were sampled in one paint chip, which allowed us to examine and
understand the paint layer stratigraphy of the area sampled. The cross-sections were analyzed first with
several analytical techniques to understand the layering scheme including: (1) optical microscopy (visible
and ultraviolet (UV) illumination), (2) scanning electron microscopy back-scattered electron (SEM BSE)
imaging, and (3) scanning electron microscopy elemental detection spectroscopy (SEM EDS). These
techniques helped differentiate the number of layers and the elemental composition of each layer. Next,
Raman spectroscopy was performed on these cross-sections for molecular identification of pigments and
some binding media in each layer (for example, calcium carbonate detected with SEM can be more
precisely identified as calcite with Raman spectroscopy).
Loose samples, or scrapings, were analyzed for colorant and medium identification using Raman
spectroscopy, Fourier Transform Infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) and SEM BSE and SEM EDS analysis.
The experimental details used in all of this analysis are described at the end of this report. The sample
locations are shown in Figure 1 below.

Page 141 of 383

Figure 1. The locations of the samples removed from the President’s Room.

Results and discussion
Sample 1, cross-section of original blue fresco
In this first cross-section, an area of the original fresco was examined. This part of the fresco was
protected from later cleaning and retouching campaigns. The sample, shown in Figure 2 below, was a
fragment found inside of the chandelier and did not appear to have overpaint. During sampling of the
cross-section from the fragment, the images in Figure 3 were taken through the stereomicroscope of the
top and bottom of the fragment and the granular characteristic of the material is visible. The cross-section
made from this fresco fragment included a large amount of the support, or lower layer of fresco. Multiple
images for this cross-section were collected at the lowest magnification of the optical microscope (total
of 50x magnification) and were stitched together during post processing in Adobe Photoshop, which is
shown in Figure 4. A corresponding SEM BSE image is shown next to the visible light image in Figure
4. The pigment-containing fresco layers are challenging to resolve at this low magnification, so additional
images with higher magnification are also displayed below in Figure 4.

As seen in the images Figure 4a, 4b, 5a and 5b, the bottom of the sample (the thick layer 1)

of a white matrix. The inclusions were identified as quartz (clear granules), quartz with iron ochre
inclusions (yellow granules) and carbon-based black (black granules). The white matrix in layer 1 contains
calcite, or calcium carbonate. The bright blue layer visible in Figure 5a and 5b is composed of ultramarine
in a matrix of calcite, consistent with fresco. A small amount of barium sulfates was detected in this layer,
which show up as bright spots in layer 2 of the SEM BSE image below (Figure 6a). The thinly applied top
white layer 3 contains scattered blue particles (see Figure 5b above) that are ultramarine in a matrix of
calcite and gypsum. Gypsum is preferentially present on the surface of the top layer. The SEM elemental
map shown in Figure 6b and 6c below shows that calcium is distributed in all three layers and correlates
with sulfur in the top of layer 3. The sulfur present in the blue layer 2 is correlated with the ultramarine
pigment particles.

Sample 3, brown paint scraping
This sample removed from the medallion pictured below in Figure 9 contains a mixture of yellow
iron ochre, chrome yellow (lead chromate), a zinc-containing pigment likely zinc white, and gypsum.
Because this area of the Mural is part of the decorative border, it was not originally a fresco surface.
Pigments such as zinc white and chrome yellow that would not have been used in fresco could have been
used here originally with a different binding medium. The SEM BSE image below in Figure 10 shows
two parts of the sample; the darker grains towards the top of the image contains mostly gypsum and the
brighter grain towards the bottom of the image contain more chrome yellow pigment.

Figure 9. Sample 3, a brown paint scraping was removed from the medallion.

Sample 4, pink impasto
This sample was removed from an area of impasto for binding medium identification (see Figure
11). Conservators questioned whether additional material such as slaked lime was added to the original
top layer of the fresco.

Figure 11. Sample 4, a scraping of the pink impasto was removed from the fresco.

Analysis indicates that calcite is the main component in this sample, and there may be a small
amount of gypsum. Red iron ochre is the colorant. No additional binding medium such as protein (which
would suggest tempera) or oil was detected. The scraping analyzed on carbon-tape with SEM EDS is
shown in Figure 12 below, and no organic-rich area was observed. The scraping looked similar to other
areas of fresco analyzed here.
