Sample 5, cross-section of the decorative border
Cross-section 5 was removed from a crack in the decorative border (see Figure 13) and was
believed to contain some original layers and possible overpaint. Analysis reveals that layer 1 contains
calcite with quartz inclusions consistent with original fresco support in cross-section 1. Support layer 1
can be seen in photomicrographs and SEM images below (Figure 14a, 14b and 15a and 15b).

Figure 13. Cross-section 5 was removed from a damaged area of the decorative border.

Colorant in Layer 2 is yellow iron ochre, and these pigment particles can be seen in the visible
light images, Figure 16a and 16b. The matrix for layer 2 is primarily gypsum, which is not typical of the
fresco material calcite. It is not clear if gypsum was an original component, or if a transformation took
place in-situ from calcite to gypsum. Layer 3 contains primarily lead white with a small amount of barium
sulfate, and is not part of the fresco material, suggesting these layers are likely overpaint. Lead white
appears bright in the SEM BSE images, and the calcium-containing components appear darker. The SEM

Page 148 of 383

BSE image in Figure 15b shows that lead white paint layer 3 has absorbed partially into layer 2
preferentially at the top. The lead white paint has decreased the porosity of the original fresco layer 2.

a

Figure 19. Cross-section 6 imaged at 500x magnification with (a) visible and (b) UV illumination. There may be a thin
organic layer between layers 3 and 4, visible in (b).

Layer 2 is composed of primarily gypsum, which is not the typical fresco material calcite. A small
amount of iron is present, likely in the form of yellow iron ochre. In addition, ultramarine is detected with
Raman spectroscopy, suggesting that this layer may have been original. Layer 3 is mostly composed of
silicates, with some calcium-based particles and yellow iron ochres. Layers 4 and 5 contain chrome yellow
pigment, which would not have been a pigment used in the original frescoes, but could have been part of
the paint layers used in the top layers of decorative border painting. Alternatively, these layers could be
from later restoration. There is a small amount of barium sulfate in these layers. Layers 4 and 5 are both
thinly applied and somewhat difficult to distinguish in the visible light images in Figures 18 and 19,
however two distinct layers can be seen in the SEM BSE image, Figure 20 below. In addition, EDS
analysis indicates that layer 5 has more chromium-based pigment than layer 4. Although layer 6 is not
visible in these images, there is a thin and incomplete barium sulfate layer detected in some areas of this
cross-section. This layer may have been partially removed during a previous cleaning.

Sample PR07, efflorescence on fresco
The efflorescence was brushed from the surface of the fresco. As shown in Figure 21, areas from
both the proper right (PR) and proper left (PL) sides of the Religion fresco were sampled and analyzed.

Page 151 of 383

Crystals from the right and left sides of Religion’s head were analyzed separately, and results showed that
the samples contain the same mixture of efflorescence grains.

Figure 21. Efflorescence on the proper right (PR) and proper left (PL) sides of the Religion fresco medallion were sampled.

Microscopic investigation of the scrapings reveals that the efflorescence is a mixture of angular
and round crystal grains. This finding can be seen in Figure 22 with the scanning electron microscope
backscattered electron (SEM BSE) image. Follow-up SEM energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) analysis
of these crystals indicates that the angular grains are calcium-based, while the rounded grains are sodiumbased.

Ca-based

Na-based
Figure 22. Scanning electron microscope (SEM) back-scattered electron (BSE) image showing PR07, which contains both
angular and rounded particle shapes.

The efflorescence is composed of sodium (Na) and calcium (Ca) carbonates and/or oxalates, as
indicated by Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR). The samples PR07 proper left (PL) and
proper right (PR) from both sides of the Religion fresco medallion are consistent with one another and
with the efflorescence sampled and analyzed underneath the Executive Authority fresco medallion (sample
PR02) reported in an earlier analysis. The cause of the efflorescence is not determined, and although a
possible source of Ca is clear (from the calcite preparation layers), a possible source for Na is not apparent
at this time.

Page 152 of 383

Sample PR08, cross-section of efflorescence on fresco
One important question about the efflorescence is whether it forms below or on the surface of the
fresco. Salts forming below the surface can have additional structural implications, such as possible
detachment of surface layers due to physical stress. For this reason, a cross-section (PR08) on the Religion
fresco medallion was sampled at locations shown in Figure 23, which is the same area as PR07 where
surface efflorescence is present.

Figure 23. The sample site for PR08 after sampling. This cross-section was removed from the efflorescence on the proper
right side of the figure in the Religion fresco.

Cross-section analysis shown in Figure 24 for PR08 indicates that the sample has three layers.
Measurements include the optical microscope image in Figure 24a and the scanning electron microscope
back-scattered electron (SEM BSE) image in Figure 24b. Analysis of these images concludes that Layer
1 contains calcite, Layer 2 contains calcite, gypsum and yellow iron oxides, or ochres as colorant, and
Layer 3 is the efflorescence layer, although it is challenging to see at the magnification settings. The
efflorescence layer (Layer 3) is the whitish thin layer on top of the off-white fresco Layer 2. Mounting the
salts in cross-section required dry polishing, which unintentionally left a scratched surface that is visible
Figure 5a. A magnified view in Figure 25 shows the efflorescence layer is about 10 μm thick.

During the cross-section preparation, the loosely bound efflorescence grains on the surface fell out
of the top layer, which were then partially replaced by polishing materials (aluminum oxide) as the crosssections were prepared. Because of this challenge in preparing and then examining the loosely bound
material on the surface, about 6 small fragments from this same area were examined with alternative
analytical methods that are amendable to characterizing unmounted cross-sections (i.e. without mounting
in polyester resin). Two examples of loose fragments are shown in Figure 26. The grains were placed on
double-sided carbon tape in various orientations and examined in the SEM. The first example is surface
side up and is shown in the SEM BSE image (Figure 26). Results show that the large grains on the surface
of the fresco are Ca- and Na-based compounds. These are the same materials seen in Layer 3 of the crosssections presented in Figures 24 and 25.

Sample PR09, cross-section of efflorescence on the decorative border
Sample PR09 taken from the locations shown in Figure 28 was analyzed as a cross-section to
determine if the efflorescence in the decorative border contains any subfluorescence (accumulation of
salts just below the surface). It was also important to determine if the efflorescence visible on the surface
has the same composition as the efflorescence on the fresco.

Figure 28. A cross-section Sample PR09 was removed from the efflorescence on the border of the Executive fresco.

Like sample PR08 (efflorescence on the fresco), the surface efflorescence shown in Figure 29 for
PR09 was challenging to visualize and study in the embedded cross-section. The efflorescent salts may
have been lost during the mounting process because they solubilize during the resin embedding process
or because they were lost during dry polishing of the cross-section. Information on PR09 was gained by
analyzing loosely mounted fragments and avoiding the embedding process. Fragments were analyzed on
carbon sticky tape with a) the surface side facing up and b) a fragment on its side. Figure 29 shows a
Page 155 of 383

fragment on carbon tape with its surface facing up and some efflorescent salts on the surface of the
fragment and others scattered to the side of the fragment on the sticky tape. The efflorescent salts are
labeled in Figure 29 below.

Figure 29. A sample of PR09 with the surface side facing up is analyzed on carbon tape in the SEM. Efflorescence and
border paint material (gypsum and barium sulfate) are indicated in the BSE image.

FTIR combined with SEM analysis determines that the efflorescence on sample PR09 is composed
of the same Ca- and Na-based carbonates and/or oxalates detected in sample PR08 and sample PR02.
Together with the previous results, this finding indicates that the same efflorescence is affecting the fresco
and decorative border surfaces of the President’s Room.
A loose fragment of PR09 (not embedded in resin) mounted on its side was examined with SEM
and is shown in Figure 30 below. This image illustrates that the efflorescence is limited to the surface of
the decorative border, and that the Ca- and Na-based carbonates and/or oxalates are not present as
subfluorescence (underneath the surface). Elemental mapping (Figure 31) shows that sulfur (S) is only
present in the paint layer 2 and not in the efflorescence layer 3 (the surface of the sample), that Ca is
present in layer 2 and in layer 3. Together, these results show that Ca is present only as a sulfate (gypsum)
in the paint layer and as a carbonate or oxalate in the surface efflorescence. The Na map shows that the
Na-based crystals are present only on the surface.

Prior to sampling, some overpaint was removed with gel in a selected area, called a window. This
window reveals the original decorative border paint. The color of the overpaint is visibly lighter than the
original color. The conservator who cleaned the window remarked that the overpaint was somewhat
challenging to remove. A small scraping PR10 was removed from the overpaint in the area adjacent to the
window, see Figure 33 below for medium identification of the overpaint.

Figure 33. A scraping sample PR10 overpaint was removed from the border near the Executive fresco.

Overpaint in this area contains an oil binder (based on FTIR analysis) and lead white with small
amounts of iron ochres and traces of barium sulfate (based on SEM and FTIR analysis).
Sample PR11, scraping of original decorative border
Sample PR11 is a scraping of the decorative border’s original paint. This sample was taken in the
recently cleaned window, where the overpaint was removed by a conservator, see Figure 34. The color of
the original paint is visibly darker than the overpaint surrounding the cleaned window.

Page 158 of 383

Figure 34. Sample PR11 is a scraping of the original decorative border. It was taken in the recently cleaned window pictured
here, near the Executive fresco.

The composition of the original binder is very challenging to determine due to the large amount
of gypsum present. Protein bands are not detected, indicating that a protein-based binder is not, if at all,
present in a measurable quantity (amounts smaller than 10-20% are below the detection limit). A small
carbonyl band and aldehyde stretches are detected with FTIR, suggesting a minor amount of oil is present.
This oil may be original or be residue from the overpaint layer that was mostly removed from the original
surface, but that may have absorbed into the porous original layer during application or cleaning. SEM
analysis of a small fragment shows that after gel-cleaning, a small amount of overpaint layer 3 remains on
the original surface layer 2 (see Figure 35 below). The pigments used in the overpaint are easily visible as
bright in the SEM BSE because they are bright in the BSE image.

Figure 35. A fragment of sample PR11 on its side shows that the original surface (layer 2) is composed of gypsum with a
small amount of lead white oil paint remaining on the top (layer 3).

Sample PR12, cross-section of overpainted decorative border
Sample PR12 is a cross-section taken near PR10 and PR11; scrapings of the decorative border’s
overpaint and original paint, respectively. PR12 was removed in a pre-existing loss along a crack and this
sample location is shown in Figure 36. Sample PR12 was removed to obtain information about the layering
sequence next to the recently gel-cleaned window, near samples PR10 and PR11. This cross-section was
taken in an area that was believed to have original yellow paint underneath.

As seen in Figure 37, cross-section PR12 is composed of three layers; layer 1 is the support layer
and contains calcite with quartz inclusions, layer 2 is the original top layer and contains gypsum, yellow
ochre and carbon black and layer 3 is the overpaint containing lead white, yellow ochres and traces of
barium sulfate. These top two yellow layers can be seen more clearly in the magnified optical image
below, Figure 38. The yellow color of the original layer 2 is darker and more transparent, likely owing to
the carbon black and gypsum material than the overpaint layer 3, which contains primarily lead white.
The composition of each layer and layer sequence is consistent with sample PR05, which was taken from
the decorative border in a different part of the ceiling.

Sample PR13, scraping of flaking paint in decorative border
Sample PR13 is a scraping taken in an area where the decorative border overpaint appears flaky,
and cracking and tenting are occurring. The sample location for PR13 is shown in Figure 39 and is in a
problematic area adjacent to a crack. The majority of the decorative border does not appear to have this
type of flaking, tenting and cracking, so the overpaint medium in this area is of particular interest. During
sampling, it was noted that the surface paint was stretchy, very elastic-like and hard to separate from itself.

Figure 39. Sample PR13 is a scraping of the overpainted decorative border in a location that is flaking.

The medium of this sample PR13 is a polyvinyl acetate (PVA) resin AYAF. It is a low molecular
weight thermoplastic resin that is characterized by remaining flexible and relatively soft. It is unclear why
PVA was used in this location for the decorative border, however the different medium compared to the
oil medium of the overpaint seen in the other location (sample PR10) may explain the different current
visual appearance.
Sample PR14, scraping of intact paint in decorative border
Sample PR14 is a scraping taken from a different part of the room where the decorative border
overpaint appears intact and is taken for comparison to sample PR13. The sample location for PR14 is
shown in Figure 40. During sampling, it was noted that the overpaint was harder and not stretchy and soft
like PR 13.

Page 161 of 383

Figure 40. Sample PR14 is a scraping taken in the overpainted decorative border.

Sample PR14 contains an oil binder. This result aligns with the more brittle and hard quality of the
paint during sampling. Given that two of the three overpaint samples taken from the decorative border are
oil paint, it is possible that a significant portion of the overpainted borders are oil and suggests that the
removal of the overpaint would be labor and time intensive.
Sample PR15, cross-section from the gold and green border
Sample PR15 is a cross-section taken in the golden border with the goal of addressing several
questions about the border. First, was the green painted over a layer of gold? Secondly, how many total
layers are present? Finally, were there additional layers of gold present under the top gold layer?

Figure 22. Sample PR15 is a cross-section of the green paint and the gold border.

Figure 42. Cross-section PR15 is shown on the a) as an optical microscope image and b) as a slightly different part of the
cross-section in SEM BSE image. It contains twelve layers including four metal layers.

The location for sample PR15 is shown in Figure 41. Cross-section PR15 is composed of twelve
layers, as shown in Figure 42. Four of these layers are gold foil (layers 4, 6, 8 and 11 in Figure 42). Layer
1 is the support and is composed of calcite. Layers 2, 3, 5 and 7 are mostly colorless. SEM results suggest
that layer 2 is organic, layer 3 is organic with some barium sulfate, lead white and chrome-containing
components, layer 5 is organic with some calcium and silicon containing components, and layer 7 is
organic with some lead white and calcium, chromium and barium containing components. Layer 9 is a
greenish color composed of mostly lead white, possibly with ultramarine and chrome yellow. Layer 10 is
organic with some aluminum silicates and small amounts of yellow ochres and possibly chrome yellow.
The top yellowish green layer is composed of chrome yellow and lead white with a small amount of
barium sulfates. The top greenish layer appears to be applied on top of a layer of gold foil.
The elemental map shown below in Figure 43a clearly indicates four gold (Au) foil layers. About
4 layers are composed of primarily organic components and are likely partially transparent. Pb is present
in two of the surface layers 12 and 9, and Ca is present in the ground layer 1 (Figure 43 b and c).

Conclusions
Fifteen samples were taken for the purpose of scientific analysis of the Brumidi Murals in the
President’s Room and were examined with multiple analytical techniques. Results suggest that the original
material in the Murals consists of a thick layer of support (arriccio) composed of calcite with quartz and
carbon black inclusions and one or more layers of fresco (intonaco) composed of calcite and pigments
such as ultramarine, carbon black, and yellow and red iron oxides. The even dispersion of pigment

Page 163 of 383

particles through the entire fresco layer suggests use of the buon fresco method. Small amounts of gypsum
and silicates are detected in the original fresco layers.
The decorative borders around the frescos were largely overpainted and consolidation materials
were applied to damaged or degraded areas. These painted surfaces (surfaces that are not fresco) contain
pigments such as lead white, chrome yellow and likely zinc white and also contain extenders such as
barium sulfate. These layers of paint are likely later restoration, and the result suggests that the overpaint
is likely oil. Some of these pigments and binding medium have absorbed into the somewhat porous
original surface layer, likely having been carried into the surface at the time of their application. In
addition, polyvinyl acetate (PVA) was detected in the top layers of paint from some locations. Oil was
used as the overpaint in most places and only smaller areas were painted or consolidated with PVA.
Binding medium identification of the original paint of the decorative borders is complicated by the
presence of the PVA and strongly absorbing signal of the calcite and gypsum. It is possible that additional
binding media such as oil and/or tempera is present in the original layers, but further sampling and analysis
would be required. The color of the decorative border is currently lighter yellow than its original color. In
the golden border area, there are four separate layers of gold foil, suggesting that the gold borders may
have been regilded at some point.
Analysis of the efflorescence on the frescos and decorative borders show that all areas contain
efflorescent salt crystals of the same composition. They are composed of a mixture of calcium- and
sodium-based carbonates and/or oxalates. The visible efflorescence on the frescos and the decorative
borders is only present on top of the surface and no subfluorescence is observed (meaning that crystals
are not forming inside the paint or fresco layers).

Experimental details
Cross-sections
Paint chips were sampled with a scalpel blade and mounted in a clear hardening plastic resin called
Bioplastic. After the resin was fully cured, the resin blocks were ground to reveal the layers of each crosssection. Samples were dry polished with successively finer polishing paper, up to 12000 grit.
Fourier Transform Infrared (FTIR) spectromicroscopy
Samples were removed with a scalpel from the wallpaper fragments and pressed into a thin layer on a
microdiamond cell. A Nicolet iS10 FTIR spectrophotometer with a Nicolet Continuum microscope using
Omnic 32 (version 6.0) software was used to analyze samples in transmission mode with radiation over a
range of 4000-650 cm-1 at a resolution of 4 cm-1. Each spectrum is an average of 100 scans. The sample
spectra were matched with FTIR reference databases. In some cases, microexctractions were performed
on the diamond window with water and then chloroform.
Scanning electron microscopy (SEM)
Samples were either analyzed as loose scrapings pressed onto diamond windows, as loose powder
scrapings mounted on carbon tape, or as cross sections mounted in resin. The samples were analyzed on
a JEOL JSM 6460LV scanning electron microscope with INCA x-sight energy-dispersive X-Ray
fluorescence (EDS) and INCA platform software. The electron beam energy was 20kV with an SEM
chamber pressure of 35 Pascals. Images were collected using JEOL backscattered electron imaging in
shadow mode. The resolution of the INCA detector is 133 eV at 5.9 keV.

Page 164 of 383

Visible and UV reflectance optical microscopy
Normal and UV reflected light images of the cross sections were captured in order to improve
understanding of the layering scheme and to observe the presence or absence of autofluorescence. A
Halogen lamp (100 W, no filter) was used for normal light images and a mercury gas discharge lamp
(100W, excitation filter=340-380nm, suppression filter=425 nm) was used for UV images.
Raman spectromicroscopy
Raman spectroscopy was performed on loose samples and on mounted cross-sections. The instrument is
a Thermo Scientific DXR3 Raman microscope using a 785 nm laser and 10x or 50x objectives and power
setting of around 0.2 mW.

CONSERVATION REPORT: BRIEF STUDY OF DECORATIVE PAINTED FINISHES
Purpose/Goals of Study
This brief (one day) investigation was performed to build on the investigation into the historic
decorative painted finishes on the ceiling of S-216 performed by Christiana Cunningham-Adams in 19941995. This study was performed to explore the appearance of the original paint layers and to determine
if any alternative methods could be found for overpaint removal 30 years after the initial study.
Examination and Written/Photographic Documentation
Examination and written/photographic documentation were performed (as needed) before, during, and
after solubility testing/making exposure windows of the original painted finishes. The painted finishes
were examined under normal/ambient light and ultraviolet-excited visible light.
Existing Exposures of the Original Paint Layers
The existing exposures of the original decorative painted finishes on the ceiling in S-216 were done in
1994-95 by Christiana Cunningham-Adams using Zip Strip Paint and Varnish Remover (with a 30 minute
application time), which was found to remove most effectively the overpaint from the original
decorative painted finishes at that time. During this 2024 brief study, a few of the 1994-95 exposures in
the northeast corner of the ceiling were examined. Some blanching (appearing whitish/hazy) of the
painted surface was observed in these exposures, which could be temporarily remedied by wetting out
the original paint layers with odorless mineral spirits to add saturation and allow for comparison to a
newly made exposure/area of testing during this study. (This lack of saturation could be remedied long
term with a thin application of varnish – to provide more saturation of the original colors and not add
gloss to this originally matte paint.) Some sanding of the original paint layers appearing as series of fine
scratches, was also noted in these exposures and is likely a contributing factor to to the strong adhesion
between the original paint layers and overpaint layers observed in this study.
Solubility Testing and a New Exposure
Before solubility testing/making an exposure, the overpainted surface was examined to select a good
area, which would be in mostly good condition with intact original paint layers – i.e. free of large fills or
paint instabilities. Many areas of active lifting paint were noted; however, it is hard to tell without
further investigation if this just affects the overpaint or both the overpaint and the original paint layers.
So, as to not add another variable to testing, these unstable areas were not investigated at the present

JACKSON ART CARE LLC | 2089 Alder Ln, Dumfries,
22026
PageVA
166 of
383 | 202-854-1488 | christy@jacksonartcare.com

JACKSON ART CARE LLC

JAC No. 24-001

page 2

time. Ultimately, this concluded in choosing an area of trompe l’oeil molding to the east of a concavesided square putti panel in the northeast quadrant of the ceiling.
Testing was performed during this brief study using various gels (see photo documentation for more
information), which were made using distilled water and Carbopal 934, Pemulen TR-2, or Xanthan gum
as thickening agents. Various solvents (xylene, acetone, ethanol, and benzyl alcohol) and a chelating
agent (EDTA) were added as needed to the gels for testing. A commercial product called Blue Bear Soy
Gel Paint and Urethane Stripper (N-methyl 2 pyrrolidone, dibasic ester-LVP, soy methyl ester, cellulose
ether powder, surfactant, and fumed silica) was tested. Mechanical removal using a scalpel was also
tested.
Testing concluded that the gels containing benzyl alcohol were generally the most effective at overpaint
removal. Those with the lowest water content were found to have the least effect on the original paint
layers which are somewhat water sensitive. As a result of this known water sensitivity, all gels, including
the Blue Bear Soy Gel were cleared form the painted surface during testing with isopropanol on cotton
swabs. Testing using mechanical removal (scalpel), proved very challenging as the overpaint and original
paint layers are strongly adhered together. This strong adhesion appears to be due to the matte,
absorptive original paint layer and the general sanding of the original surface before overpaint
application.
The Blue Bear Soy Gel (40 minutes to 1 hour application time) was found to be most effective at
removing the overpaint from the original decorative painted finishes. In some areas (mostly in trompe l’
oeil shadow areas), to achieve complete removal, second and third applications were needed due to
remove thicker overpaint application. The Soy Gel was only allowed to remain on the surface for 15-20
minutes in subsequent applications as some undercutting of the original finishes (solubilizing of the
layer beneath the original paint layers) was noted if this product was left on the surface too long or if
more than three applications were used. This product was also used to make a small exposure of the
original paint layers in the test area as it was the most effective of what was tested. In comparison to
the overpaint, the original paint layers revealed in the exposure were found to be warmer/more vibrant
in color and the trompe l’oeil design to have higher contrast with dark painted shadows. This small paint
exposure was varnished with 10% Paraloid B-72 (an ethyl-methacrylate copolymer) in xylenes and
inpainted with Gamblin Conservation Colors (pigments in Laropal A81-based binder) to improve
readability.
Promising overpaint removal results were also found using a 1:9 benzyl alcohol/xylene gel (2g Carbopol
934, 15ml Ethomeen C-12, 90 ml xylene, 10ml benzyl alcohol, distilled water added dropwise) which
worked somewhat faster than the Soy Gel, however, it may be slightly too strong and some slight
sensitivity of the delicate original paint layers was noted. Perhaps, this formulation gel with a lower
amount of Benzyl alcohol would be more effective. Due to the time constraints of this brief study and a
lack of solvent extraction onsite, this was not able to be explored during this brief study.
Conclusions and Recommendations for Future Study
Since the overpaint removal from the decorative painted elements on the ceiling of the President’s
Room (S-216) is time consuming/tedious (due to long application times for overpaint removal agents
and the significant water sensitivity of the original paint layers) and some condition concerns affecting
the original paint layers were observed, full scale overpaint removal is not recommended at the present
time. Condition concerns noted during this study were: fine sanding scratches, filled losses, and many
areas of possible active lifting original paint (overpaint may only be affected, further investigation is

JACKSON ART CARE LLC | 2089 Alder Ln, Dumfries,
VA
22026
Page
167 of
383 | 202-854-1488 | christy@jacksonartcare.com

JACKSON ART CARE LLC

JAC No. 24-001

page 3

needed).
To move forward with this project in the future, it is recommended that a comprehensive study be
performed of the original decorative painted finishes on the ceiling in the President’s room to
determine all of the original paint colors and the original trompe l’oeil design scheme (the highlights
and shadows of the overpainting may or may not be accurate). This will involve the creation of many
more paint exposure windows and taking many paint samples to gather this information. After the
comprehensive study, it is recommended that the original painted finishes be replicated. Any exposures
of the original paint layers in good condition are recommended to remain exposed post-paint
replication as representative areas of the original painted finishes.

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations
ANA Job No. 22-193
September 12, 2024

Page 179 of 383

1.
INTRODUCTION
From October 24 through 26, 2023, engineers Dave Woodham and Gary Ogden of Atkinson-Noland and
Associates Inc., (ANA) were onsite at the US Capitol to conduct nondestructive testing and visual
assessment of the ceiling in The President’s Room (Senate Room S-216). Jennifer Schork and Michelle
Leach of ICR facilitated site access to the Capitol and assisted ANA during the investigations. While on
site, ANA and ICR met briefly with Joseph Vignola, of The Catholic University of America, and Amelia V.
Ware, of the U.S. Naval Research Laboratory, to discuss preliminary findings and similarities between the
recently completed Scanning Laser Doppler Vibrometry investigations (SLDV) and ANA’s initial results.
Prior to ANA’s site visit, scaffolding had been erected in the room which facilitated hands-on access to
the ceiling.
Testing performed by ANA, during this site visit, included surface penetrating radar (SPR), active infrared
thermography, ultrasonic pulse echo (UPE), and mechanical sounding. Digital recordings of the
mechanical sounding were made for further processing.
On April 23 and 24, 2024, Jennifer Schork, Assya Plavskina, Michelle Leach and Ryan Ramkhelawan of
ICR were on site at the US Capitol to conduct Drilling Resistance Measurement System (DRMS) tests in
the plaster ceiling of The President’s Room (Senate Room S-216). Atkinson-Noland & Associates
engineer Dave Woodham was also on site to assist with the DRMS testing and interpretation of the
results.
A SINT Technology DRMS cordless drill was used to record the drilling resistance of the plaster ceiling
from the face of the plaster to the plaster/brick interface. Observations of the interior of the drilled hole
were made using a small diameter wand on a Fluke diagnostic video borescope. During the two-day site
visit, three small plaster samples were obtained from the ceiling for petrographic analysis.
Plaster samples and DRMS locations were denoted by the mobility of the plaster – low, medium, or high
– as determined by previous Scanning Laser Doppler Vibrometry and mechanical sounding.
The following report summarizes test locations, methods, and results relating to nondestructive and
minimally invasive testing of the ceiling in the President’s Room.

2.
INITIAL INVESTIGATIONS (LABORATORY)
Prior to the first site visit, ANA fabricated a mockup in their lab consisting of a cast slab, approximately
21.5 in. by 13 in. by 1 in. thick, made of a low-strength mortar and topped with a thin (0.12 in.) gypsum
topping. The slab was then partially bonded to a two wythe, fired clay brick wall constructed for a
previous project (Figure 1). The bonded (low mobility) area was the perimeter of the slab and a partial
center rib, which provided two areas of high mobility that were unbonded to the brick wall substrate.

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations

& Associates

Pg. 2
Page 180 of 383

9/12/2024

Figure 1. Back of mortar slab (left) with an uncured gypsum compound just prior to bonding with
the clay masonry wall. At right, mortar slab with resulting “ski goggle” void behind it.

Mechanical sounding of the mortar slab, with a spherical metal impactor, identified distinct areas with
hollow sounds in the central portion of the slab. Acoustic recordings of the sounding impacts were made
using a miniDSP USB microphone and a laptop PC with the recording software Audacity. The recordings
were subsequently analyzed for frequency content using the fast Fourier transform (FFT) function in
MATLAB. Preliminary results indicated that this method could be viable in characterizing, analytically,
the differences in mechanical sounding that are perceptible to the human ear (Figure 2).

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations

& Associates

Pg. 3
Page 181 of 383

9/12/2024

Figure 2. Graphs of recorded sound files that have been processed to indicate frequency content: (a & b) impact on
low mobility areas of slab, and (c & d), impact on a known high mobility areas of the slab. There is lower frequency
energy (500 to 2500 Hz) in the recordings of sounding in high mobility areas that are not present in low mobility
(bonded) areas.

Active infrared (IR) imaging was also used on the mockup to trial the technique. The concept of active IR
imaging is that when an object with thermal discontinuities is either actively heated or cooled using an
external source, the thermal discontinuities may become apparent as temperature differences
measurable at the surface. In the case of active heating, the unattached portions of the mortar slab are
expected to heat more readily than portions of the slab attached to the brick wall due to the additional
thermal mass (heat sink) of the wall. A quartz-halogen light was modified by removing the protective
guards that would cast shadows on the slab and providing an intermediate acrylic lens to filter out
ultraviolet light and scatter the visible and infrared wavelengths more uniformly across the slab.
Based on active IR imaging trials, in a laboratory setting (Figure 3), the method seemed worth
investigating on site at the President's Room to determine if attached and detached sections of plaster
could be identified.

Figure 3. Thermal image of heated mortar slab (left) showing heating in two lobes near the center of the slab

3.

INVESTIGATIVE TESTING (PRESIDENT’S ROOM)

3.1. Microwave Radar Scanning
Surface penetrating radar (SPR) was used to assess the cross-section of the masonry vault and mortar
layers. The SPR method involves the transmission of high frequency electromagnetic radio (radar) pulses
into the object of interest and measuring the time elapsed between transmission, reflection from
subsurface features, and reception back at the surface radar antenna. A pulse of radar energy is
generated on a dipole-transmitting antenna that is placed on the surface of the object of interest.
Reflections of the radar energy occur at discontinuities due to changes in the dielectric properties of the
underlying material. Void spaces, metal presence, and any distinct change in material dielectric will
generate radar reflections due to a change in radar wave velocity at the interface. Scanning was
conducted with a Proceq GP880o unit with stepped frequencies from 400 to 6000 MHz. Scans were
conducted in normal direction (scan direction perpendicular to the dipole antenna axes) and crosspolarized (scan direction parallel to the dipole antenna axes).
The average relative dielectric permittivity of the masonry assemblage was determined by matching the
curvature of a hyperbolic reflection in the SPR data using the manufacturer’s software installed on an
Apple iPad. Determining the relative dielectric permittivity allows for the determination of the radar
velocity in the substrate and the correct interpretation of the depth of features in the radar transects.
Figure 4, below, provides an example SPR trace recorded from the underside of the ceiling. Annotations
indicate the likely features and their associated depths in the cross-section.

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations

& Associates

Pg. 5
Page 183 of 383

9/12/2024

Figure 4. SPR transects recorded from the underside of the ceiling in the central octagonal medallion. The image
shows four scans (numbered at top) in several directions and indicates the general depth (d) of features in the cross
section above the ceiling surface. The horizontal axis is in feet and inches, the vertical axis is in inches.

Additional scans were conducted from the underside of the ceiling to determine if known low mobility
areas could be differentiated from areas with higher mobility (as determined by mechanical sounding).
Although the plaster/brick masonry interface was present in all scans, there was no discernable
difference in the recorded signals under these two conditions. There would need to be a larger air gap
(min. 2-3 mm) between the plaster and substrate to be detectable at the frequencies used by this SPR
device. In theory, higher frequency SPR devices would be able to detect these shallow delaminations,
however we are not aware of any commercially available SPR system with a frequency higher than 6
GHz.
During the ceiling scanning, we noted a metallic anomaly in radial transects from the central octagon
towards the lower portions of the vault. A series of radial transects were done in each of the cardinal
directions and at intermediate radial directions. Each transect showed a metallic anomaly near the spring
line of the vault and a consistent elevation around the perimeter of the vault as shown in Figure 5 and
Figure 6. The initial interpretation was that this could be a tension ring for the dome. The placement near
the spring line and uniformity of location seemed logical for resisting the hoop stresses present in the
lower part of a dome. We later were informed that there are numerous utilities buried in the fill above the
dome. It is also possible that the metal object is a utility conduit. Water or steam pipes in the fill above
the dome could have a potential impact on the plaster ceiling should they leak or form condensates. If a
tension ring is present, we view this as a positive feature.

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations

& Associates

Page 184 of 383

9/12/2024

of the vault. Regardless of the radial scan direction, there is a reflection from a metal object near the spring line.

Figure 7. Cross section sketch of the likely configuration of the ceiling and floor above the President’s Room, based
on SPR scans and information from the 1854 Thomas Walter drawings.

Several of the capitol drawings by Thomas Walter show details regarding the construction of the walls
and masonry vault at the President’s Room. One large section through the North Wing of the Senate
portion of the Capitol indicates the general layout of the masonry vaults from the roof to the
foundations. Another drawing shows a second-floor plan view and section of the “Vice President’s Room”
and indicates the vault was originally intended to be coffered. Radar transects did not indicate any
offsets in the masonry vault and our findings suggest the as-built vault does not have coffers.
3.2. Ultrasonic Pulse Echo (UPE)
A Proceq PD8000 ultrasonic pulse echo array was used to image the cross-section of the vault including
the plaster layers. The device has eight channels which, in turn, each become the transmitter while the
return signals are received by the remaining seven channels (Figure 8). The resulting 28 scans are
combined to provide a reconstruction of the cross section showing where reflections are present.

Figure 8. Schematic representation of the ultrasonic ray paths between channels/sensors
(image courtesy of Proceq SA).

The UPE device was not expected to detect discontinuities within the individual plaster layers or between
the plaster assemblage and the masonry vault – these would be too near the surface to detect. Rather,
the intent was to determine if reflections from the back face of the vault could be detected, which would

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations

& Associates

Pg. 8
Page 186 of 383

9/12/2024

indirectly indicate minimal discontinuities in the cross-section. Conversely, if the back of vault reflection
was not visible, it would indicate the presence of a discontinuity across the section. From mechanical
sounding, we knew that the central octagon plaster has low mobility and appears to be well bonded to
the masonry vault, and from SPR data we understood the back of vault was at a depth of approximately
11 to 12 inches. Using the UPE device at the central octagon and other areas of plaster, determined by
sounding to be low mobility (bonded), we were unable to obtain a reflection from the back face of the
masonry. We suspect that significant scattering and attenuation of the ultrasonic signal was due to the
number of material interfaces – both parallel and normal to the ceiling surface. These include the
plaster/masonry interface as well as the head, bed, and collar joints between fired clay brick units in the
masonry vault.
3.3. Active Infrared Thermography
A FLIR T420 infrared (IR) camera was used to image the ceiling using ambient temperature conditions in
the room. No anomalies were noted as all areas of the ceiling were at a uniform temperature. We then
tried active IR thermography in three locations in the ceiling using the infrared energy emitted from a
standard quartz halogen work light to heat the plaster ceiling. Quartz halogen light sources emit
significant (near) infrared energy in wavelengths from 700 to 2100 nanometers (nm) [1].
The lighting from the work light was filtered and diffused by a 1/8 in. thick sheet of Plaskolite Optix UVX
acrylic placed between the light source and the ceiling. This specific acrylic material absorbs ultraviolet
light below 380 nm, generated by the light source, which could be potentially damaging to the painted
surfaces [2]. The acrylic sheet was abraded on both surfaces with coarse sandpaper to diffuse visible and
IR wavelengths to prevent localized heating. The IR camera used has a thermal sensitivity of less than
0.08 °F, so small temperature changes could be identified as the ceiling was gradually heated. Infrared
thermography detects small changes in surface temperatures, which may be a result of surface or
subsurface anomalies.

Figure 9. Thermal image (left) prior to heating indicates uniform temperatures at the plaster surface in the
Legislation painting. Visible light image (right) indicting areas identified by sounding as having a distinct hollow
sound (high mobility, red hatch) and an area that has low mobility (blue hatch).

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations

& Associates

Pg. 9
Page 187 of 383

9/12/2024

Figure 10. Thermal image (left) after heating for 14 minutes, indicates warming primarily at darker colors
due to higher absorption of infrared energy.

Our interpretation of the results from the active IR imaging was that the technique is possible on an
object with uniform absorption characteristics. The technique showed promise in initial laboratory trials
due to the uniform light-colored gypsum surface of the mockup that evenly absorbed the infrared
energy. However, the variation in absorption of infrared energy between darker and lighter colors in the
ceiling images masks the temperature variations that may be due to thermal breaks or discontinuities
within or behind the plaster layers. In order to overcome this limitation, active IR imaging should be
conducted when uniformly raising or lowering the ambient temperature in the President’s room. This
would eliminate the preferential heating that occurred during heating with an infrared heat source. We
believe this method would be valuable to corroborate or refute the findings from other techniques such
as sounding and SLDV.
3.4. Sounding
ANA mechanically sounded the entire ceiling to identify areas of plaster deemed to be:
• Low mobility (bonded to the substrate),
• Medium mobility (partially bonded to the substrate), and
• High mobility (likely unbonded or having internal delaminations between plaster layers).
Several spherical wood impactors were tried to see which impactor produced sounds that could be
differentiated between areas judged to be low mobility and those with some level of mobility (medium or
high) with the substrate. Wood spheres of 0.5, 0.75, and 1-inch diameter were used on a wood dowel
handle to lightly strike the surface of the plaster. We compared these to impacts with those from
knuckles, which previous investigators had used for sounding. Ultimately, the larger 1-inch impactor was
better at producing sounds that could be easily interpreted. To produce a more compliant (knuckle-like)
impactor, we wrapped two layers of painter’s tape around the sphere.

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations

& Associates

Pg. 10
Page 188 of 383

9/12/2024

Three distinct sounds were identified corresponding to the categories described above. These areas are
noted on a reflected ceiling plan shown below and in more detail in Appendix A.

Figure 11. Reflected ceiling plan with the mechanical sounding results from 2023 (left). Low mobility areas are
shaded blue, and areas of high mobility are shaded red. The remaining unshaded areas we interpret as medium
mobility with respect to the substrate. The image at right is the mechanical sounding results from the CunninghamAdams report in 1995 [3]. Areas of “apparent mortar instability” are shaded red and areas of “no apparent instability”
are shaded green. ANA was not aware of the 1995 sounding results at the time of our investigations.

3.5. Sound Recording and Signal Processing
Mechanical soundings were also recorded to document the acoustic signals associated with low mobility
areas of plaster and two types of sounds where the plaster was interpreted to have medium or high
mobility. The sounds inferring medium or high mobility could be differentiated by the presence of
additional reverberations in the second sound type. Recording was done using the audio recording and
editing software Audacity operating on a tablet computer, with a sampling frequency of 44,100 samples
per second (Hz). The microphone used was a miniDSP UMA-8 USB in monaural mode. For each painting
group, a grid was established at approximately 6-8 inches on center for the image. Recordings were
made at each grid point for further signal analysis and for comparison to future mechanical soundings to
help document changes in the mobility associated with the degree of attachment of the plaster to the
substrate.
The perspective in the paintings provided a geometric challenge as the documented images of the
frescoes were fully rectified, thereby eliminating the differences that exist in width between the upper
and lower portions of the image. For example, on the four rectangular portraits (Columbus, Brewster,
Vespucius, and Franklin), the width of the frame was nearly 12 inches wider at the top versus the bottom
of the painting so that these portraits would appear rectangular as viewed from the floor of the
President’s Room. ANA opted to space the mechanical soundings and recordings for each image in

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations

& Associates

Pg. 11
Page 189 of 383

9/12/2024

proportion to the width at each grid line. Figure 12 below shows a grid layout for recordings made at the
painting of Christopher Columbus with the mechanical sounding results and gridlines overlaid. The
spacing of the soundings and recordings was adjusted to achieve seven data points evenly spaced at each
gridline A through H. The grids for each area of the ceiling are shown in Appendix B. The digital sound
recordings associated with the mechanical soundings made at each area will be included as Waveform
Audio File Format (*.wav) files. The sound recordings are provided in a compressed folder accompanying
this report. The naming convention of each sound file is provided in Appendix C.

Figure 12. Painting of Christopher Columbus with the results of the mechanical soundings and a rectangular grid
overlaid. At each grid point (A1 through H7), mechanical soundings were made, and the results were recorded for
documentation and further analysis.

To categorize each signal, a set of reference values were recorded at the ceiling. A total of 88 reference
signals were recorded comprised of 45 low mobility, 21 medium mobility, and 22 high mobility readings.
Categorization of the reference sounding was determined by ear while on-site. Audio recordings from
the reference files were used to determine a standardized method for analyzing all the audio signals.
The power spectrum of each individual sounding file was processed and analyzed in MATLAB. Plotting
the power spectrum against the normalized frequency, a clear distinction between low mobility and
medium or high mobility soundings emerged at frequencies below 4,410 Hz. To quantify this difference,
the magnitude of the power spectrum was averaged between 0 and 4,410 Hz. The values as listed in
Table 1 are based on a statistical analysis of the reference files and were used to categorize each
sounding condition. Each grid was processed using the values in Table 1 to color code each of the impacts
presented in Appendix B.

3.6. Drill Resistance Measurement System (DRMS)

The DRMS provides a quantitative measurement of the resistance of the material being drilled over the
depth of the hole. This information is useful for detecting differing densities in the material that may
indicate varied materials in the cross section or the presence of voids or poorly consolidated material. A
typical DRMS data set is shown in Figure 15.
Drilling resistance (force) is proportional to the depth of cut for each revolution of the drill bit. This can be
manipulated by changing the penetration rate (higher penetration rates yield higher drilling resistance)
or changing the revolutions per minute (rpm) of the drill motor (a higher rpm reduces the drilling
resistance). For all DRMS readings in the President’s Room, the penetration rate and drill rotational
speed were held constant at 30 millimeters/minute and 250 rpm, respectively. As a result, the DRMS
results from hole to hole are comparable.
When the drilling resistance versus the depth is plotted, visual interpretation of areas of denser/harder or
less dense/softer materials is possible. Another comparison between DRMS data sets can be done by
evaluating the average force used to drill each hole while the bit is in the plaster.
For each data set, a start and stop depth was determined visually and numerically from the data. Start
depth was determined by the first significant rise in force immediately after zero depth. Similarly, the
stop depth was determined by the rise in force when the drill bit entered the brick vault at the back of the
plaster depth (Figure 15). Appendix D contains graphs from all the DRMS holes and the associated
videoscope images. Table 2 summarizes the average drilling resistance, within the plaster, for each hole.

Figure 15. Graph of DRMS data at location Low Mobility 1 (LM1). The graph shows the variation in drilling
resistance from the surface at 0 mm to the fired brick of the vault at 17.8 mm depth. The data shown in red
has been filtered to remove “noise” (false artifacts in the data) that is primarily due to the rotational speed of
the drill (250 rpm). A Butterworth low-pass filter is commonly used to filter out frequencies above 4 Hertz
(Hz).

Figure 17. Videoscope image from HM1 viewed from the surface of the plaster to the plaster/brick interface. The image

CONCLUSIONS
Summary
Surface penetrating radar was unable to identify the shallow delamination depths in areas with medium
or high mobility plaster in the ceiling. Both normal and cross-polarized antenna orientations were used.
SPR was able to detect the plaster/clay brick masonry interface, the back of the masonry vault, and
possibly the overlying floor structure in the room above the President’s Room. We noted one consistent
metallic anomaly around the perimeter of the ceiling at a consistent elevation in the room.
Ultrasonic pulse echo reconstructions were noisy and unable to image delaminations between the plaster
and clay brick masonry vault or the back of the ceiling assembly. We believe this is due to significant
scattering and attenuation of the ultrasonic signal, related to the number of material interfaces – both
parallel and normal to the ceiling surface. These include the plaster/masonry interface as well as the
brick/mortar interfaces at head, bed, and collar joints between the fired clay brick units in the masonry
vault.
Active IR thermography, using an infrared heat source, was not successful at identifying known areas of
well-bonded and unbonded areas. Rather, the increase in surface temperatures measured during heating
with an IR heat source seemed to be a function of the different absorption characteristics of the painted
surfaces. Darker colors tended to absorb heat masking any temperature change due to higher mobility
areas. To overcome this limitation, active IR imaging should be conducted when uniformly raising or
lowering the ambient temperature in the President’s room. This would eliminate the preferential heating
that occurred during heating with an infrared heat source.

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations

& Associates

Pg. 16
Page 194 of 383

9/12/2024

Sounding, using small wood impactors, was used to detect areas with distinct sounds that were inferred
to indicate areas of low mobility and medium or high mobility. We were able to discern two types of
increased mobility, medium and high, associated with sounds that contained lower frequencies than low
mobility areas. As such, three distinct sounds were discernible by ear and analysis of audio recordings.
Annotated rectified images show the three conditions as determined by ANA.
For each subject area of the ceiling, ANA gridded the area and conducted mechanical soundings on the
grids. These soundings were digitally recorded for documentation purposes and to conduct further signal
analysis of the recorded audio recordings. Preliminary findings indicate that we can differentiate
between low mobility and medium and high mobility using standard signal analysis techniques available
in the analysis software MATLAB. Additional signal processing indicated that the three different
conditions – low mobility, medium mobility and high mobility – can usually be discerned by averaging the
power spectrum between o and 4,410 Hz and comparing the relative energies in this frequency range.
The Catholic University of America conducted additional signal analysis on a subset of the recorded
mechanical soundings and found that they could discriminate between the three plaster conditions –
low, medium and high mobility. Their report on the acoustic signal analysis is appended to the main
report of the Laser Doppler Vibrometry results from the President’s Room ceiling [4].
The drilling resistance conducted at the President’s Room provided valuable information about the
conditions within the plaster that had been previously identified using nondestructive methods such as
SLDV and mechanical sounding. Prior to the DRMS measurements, we could only speculate what
internal plaster conditions existed that were causing the measurable differences observed using NDE.
Resistance drilling using the DRMS provided physical data of the integrity of the plaster applied to the
masonry vault. As mentioned, previous assessment methods measured plaster mobility via the SLDV
technique or acoustic differences detectable from mechanical soundings. These nondestructive
evaluation (NDE) methods inferred that there was a physical condition in the plaster assembly that
created differing results from area to area, but the investigative team could only speculate what the
underlying causes could be. The DRMS method provided an insight into the physical conditions that
might be creating these differing results.
In the three DRMS drillings where the nondestructive techniques, SLVD and mechanical sounding,
indicated low mobility there were no discernable voids or poorly consolidated areas in the DRMS data.
Videoscope images provided visual confirmation. However, in the areas deemed to have medium or high
mobility, as determined by nondestructive methods, five of the six DRMS data sets indicated the
presence of low-resistance plaster or a void. Voids could be confirmed by videoscope observations. None
of the DRMS drillings indicated detachment between plaster layers.
Extrapolating from the DRMS findings implies that a significant portion of the ceiling has medium to high
mobility relative to the brick vault.

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations

& Associates

Pg. 17
Page 195 of 383

9/12/2024

Figure 18. ANA sounding map (left) and The Catholic University of America’s SLDV composite mobility map
(right) both indicate large portions of the ceiling have medium or high mobility. Any area in ANA’s reflected
ceiling plan that is not blue falls into these categories. Any area in red/orange in CUA’s mobility map is in
these categories.
Given the hypothesis that large portions of the ceiling have medium or high mobility with the substrate,
why have we not seen failures? We believe that the low mobility areas of the plaster are anchoring
medium and high mobility plaster and that the plaster ceiling is acting as a thin shell structure within the
clay brick masonry vault. Forces, due to the self-weight of the plaster, are being resisted by shear in the
plaster and transferred to low mobility (bonded) areas. As the vault surface becomes more vertical near
the spring line, these self-weight forces become more in-plane (stabilizing) forces. This may be a
contributing factor to the observation that plaster near the spring line is typically low mobility (blue areas
in ANA’s sounding map and purple areas in CUA’s mobility map). Further, the only known failure of the
plaster was at the horizontal portion (octagon) of the vault where self-weight would most stress the bond
to the substrate (Figure 19).

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations

& Associates

Pg. 18
Page 196 of 383

9/12/2024

Figure 19. The direction of gravity loads in the plaster becomes more in-plane lower in the vault reducing
the tensile stress on the plaster to substrate bond.
Although the nondestructive and invasive testing that was carried out in this study was useful, there are
still many questions. We believe that further investigation is necessary to verify or refine the current
assessment of the severity of the detachment of the plaster ceiling and to understand the causal factors.
However, prior to or in parallel with further investigations, we believe it is important to gather the
collective information we have and decide on near-term conservation actions. Table 3 outlines three
possible scenarios based on conservative, moderate and do-nothing conservation approaches. We
recommend a conservative approach.

The following are preliminary recommendations to obtain the information outlined above.

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room Ceiling Investigations

Pg. 20
Page 198 of 383

9/12/2024

Recommendations for Next Steps
• Active IR thermography should be possible if the room were temporarily heated or cooled
uniformly and then monitored during the return to a more typical room temperature. Patterns
may emerge between low mobility (attached) and medium or high mobility plaster sections due
to the differential cooling/heating. Time lapse IR images can be used to determine rates of
temperature change which may be indicative of debonds/delaminations in the composite
construction. This information would back up or dispute the existing knowledge of the mobility
distribution of the plaster ceiling.
• Vibrations are transmitted into the masonry vault from the occupied floor above the President’s
Room and from adjacent rooms in the building. We recommend vibration monitoring of the
ceiling for a minimum of 6 months to identify typical and maximum peak particle velocities. The
crown of the vault could be easily monitored as there is 120V AC power available at the
chandelier for a wireless monitoring device.
• We also recommend environmental monitoring of the vault and interstitial space above the
vault. Eight to ten temperature and relative humidity probes should be placed around the vault
to evaluate the temperature and humidity swings the vault may be experiencing on a daily,
weekly, and annual basis. It would also be important to help understand the variation in
temperature and humidity around the vault (e.g. at interior vs. exterior walls) to know if there is
a potential for condensation in any location and time of the year.
• We recommend an engineering analysis of the vault and plaster system using finite element
analysis (FEA) or a similar method. The model would be used to understand the structural
response or the vault and plaster assemblage to varying environmental conditions and to
understand how the partially bonded ceiling is functioning structurally with the masonry vault.
The environmental and vibration monitoring information could be used to model the effects of
temperature and humidity variation and vibrations in the assemblage. A calibrated computer
model may be able predict bond strengths, that when present over a larger area of the ceiling,
could produce a local failure. Developing an accurate model requires reasonably accurate
material properties. As a result, there will need to be materials testing of the brick and plaster to
obtain typical material properties – plaster tensile, compressive and shear strength, plaster
modulus, brick modulus, and brick and plaster hygric and thermal expansion coefficients.
• The stepped frequencies used in the Proceq GP8800 antenna are a maximum of 6 GHz which
was not able to detect the minimal detachment between the plaster and the substrate.
Considerably higher frequencies may be able to detect this anomaly as commercially available
radar units with this capability become available in the future.

Abstract
This report describes the results of a survey conducted in the summer of 2023 examining the
structural status of plaster in Room S216, the President’s Room of the US Capitol, an update to
the data collected from 2003 in the United States Capitol Building President’s Room (S216).
This analysis identifies areas of concern on the plaster that adorns the ceiling. The focus of this
report is to summarize the preliminary results of 20 scans of the room’s ceiling. These scans
reveal areas where the plaster displays high mobility. Additional text provides some information
on the methodology used for collecting data and making determinations of the plaster’s status.
The document provides plots of local surface mobility alongside photographs which have grids
of measurement locations annotated with areas of concern, accompanied by brief texts describing
the results.

1. Introduction
This report represents a summary of preliminary analysis of data collected in August 2023 in the
President’s Room (S216). Approximately 18 individual scans were performed and analyzed. The
results have been compiled and are presented in the results section. The current analysis is
sufficient to identify many anomalies and to verify that some areas are intact. The results have
been compiled and are presented in the results section. The current analysis is sufficient to
identify many areas of higher mobility and to verify that some areas are intact. It is important to
notice that the more generic phrase “area of higher mobility” will be used in place of the word
“delamination” to be technically correct in their description. Delamination of a multi-layered
material occurs when one layer detaches from another. However, “areas of higher mobility” do
not exclude other types of anomalies that may have caused similar motion detection, like voids,
degradation, or fractures within a fresco layer. Thermal maps of some of the areas under
investigation have been added for validation purposes.
The objective of this study was to determine the structural integrity of plaster in S216 of the
United States Capitol Building through structural vibration analysis. Structural vibration analysis
is a method for examining a surface for underlying irregularities without compromising the
existing integrity of the material. The plaster was excited with sound, causing small-scale
vibration. The resulting vibrations were measured using a laser vibrometer and then used to
reveal the location and size of unseen irregularities in the structure. This procedure was
performed on each of the frescoes, and on the plaster in general, adorning the ceiling of a room
in the Capitol Building to discover areas where the painted plaster has lost adherence to the
underlying structure brick.

1.1 Laser Doppler Vibrometry
Laser Doppler Vibrometry (LDV) is a method of measuring the time-dependent displacement of
vibration of a surface. One advantage of using LDV is that it is a non-destructive method of
metrology. LDV is an optical interferometry approach that collects laser light that has been
backscattered from a moving surface and mixes that light with other light from the same laser
source on the surface of a photodetector. This mixing of light produces an electrical signal
related to the difference in path lengths the two beams have traveled. The electrical signal is
related to the motion of the target surface since one of the beams was projected onto that surface.
4
Page 241 of 383

The Catholic University of America, School of Engineering

Because this method relies on the surface reflecting the laser, there will be some error or noise
produced by imperfect surfaces. If the surface is rough with respect to the wavelength of the
laser, most of the light will be scattered non-specturally, or in all directions. The resulting optical
field is referred to as laser speckle which can limit the accuracy of the measurements. If there is
no higher surface mobility, this error can be mistaken for a local anomaly, though this can be
ruled out by a skilled observer. In addition there are analysis steps to eliminate the effect of a
curved surface on the data. Recent SLDV systems, like the one used in this experiment, are built
to account for curved surfaces or surfaces that are not perfectly level. The application of this
technique to the diagnostic of frescoes is described in Castellini et al. (2000), Vignola et al.
(2005), Adams et al. (2005)., Bucaro et al. (2009).

5
Page 242 of 383

The Catholic University of America, School of Engineering

2. Materials and Methods
For this measurement campaign a Polytec scanning laser Doppler Vibrometer (LDV) was
mounted on a rolling tripod along with a microphone. The LDV was aimed at a section of fresco
to be measured. A set of two speakers on another tripod were also aimed at the section under
measurement. A PYLE Pro PPA 200 amplifier was connected from the acquisition box to the
speakers. A measuring tape was used to document the exact position of the LDV and speakers in
relation to the fresco. This setup can be seen in Figure 2.1.

Figure 2.1: Schematic of the experimental setup.
Two dimensional alignment was performed using high contrast display and automatic alignment.
This was done to establish a flat surface for the LDV to reference. Three-dimensional alignment
was used to define the origin and positive x and y axes. This was done to ensure that various
three-dimensional structures and height changes are accounted for. A grid of scan points was
created over the area of the fresco to be measured. Density was adjusted to ensure adequate
sampling of the fresco. A variable density from 40x40 scan points to 60x60 scan points was used
on the different scans. The density was adjusted for each scan ±1 data point to ensure complete
sampling over the area. Adequate sampling of the surface was determined to be a resolution of
about 5cm x 5cm. This resolution was chosen in order to minimize scan time while still
maintaining coverage of the surface. Faults less than 5 cm x 5 cm are considered to be minor and
were not studied in this survey or the one done in 2004. A geometry scan is then conducted to
establish a laser position for each point in the mesh, defining the shape of the fresco surface. A
final automatic focus was performed prior to beginning the scan. Another technique that was
used for validation and comparison purposes was thermography. Thermographic images of some
of the areas were obtained using thermal imaging camera FLIRT-A70. A few thermal images are
reported in the documents to show the correspondence of results with the vibrational acoustic
analysis.
6
Page 243 of 383

The Catholic University of America, School of Engineering

2.1 Data Collection
To excite the fresco, audio speakers were used to produce a chirp. These speakers were
controlled by the Polytec console and amplified using the PYLE Pro PPA 200 amplifier. The
excitation signal has a duration of 2.5 seconds and a duty cycle of 30% of the duration. The
frequency range is from 100 Hz to 1,000 Hz and an amplitude of 80 dB, re 20 μPa, though due to
some signal drift the frequency response extends outside of that range. The LDV was set to take
four measurements and average the results to ensure accuracy. After the completion of a scan,
the data was exported as a universal file. This file contains the input vibration from the LDV, and
the output voltage to the amplifier. Data sheets were made to supplement the automatically
created info file and together, list all measurement parameters for each scan. The data sheets
contain information about exact start time and speaker location not available in the info file. The
info file contained information about the scan and excitation signal. Finally, two images of the
section of fresco were saved, one showing the fresco with the scan grid overlaid and one without
the grid. These were saved to ensure that the data could be overlaid in the correct positioning in
post processing.

2.2 Data Analysis
The LDV data was then processed using MATLAB to find the velocity and displacement
response of the fresco to the chirp excitation. This analysis was done using a Fast Fourier
Transform (FFT) on the velocities returned by the LDV. All data was then normalized to the
incident acoustic pressure and converted into decibels with 1 m/s Pa as the reference.
These results were then plotted in both magnitude and phase in order to isolate the direction of
movement from the total magnitude of movement. Once a defect had been identified at multiple
different frequencies, a representative frequency or range of frequencies was chosen, and that
plot overlaid with an image of the fresco that was measured. All images used in overlay are in
magnitude for ease of reading.

7
Page 244 of 383

The Catholic University of America, School of Engineering

3. Results Interpretation
3.1 Data Analysis Technique
The measurements resolution for all scans was chosen to be 5 cm x 5 cm. Faults less than 5 cm x
5 cm are considered to be minor and were not studied in this survey or the one done in 2004. In
order to classify an anomaly as legitimate or not it is necessary to consider several things:
● The characteristics of the surface being scanned: gold gilding or three-dimensional
surfaces are more difficult to interpret. In this case scans were repeated more than once
and only when the results appeared reliable were taken into consideration.
● The relative size of the anomaly. If an anomaly is quite small, only one or two data
points, it is likely an error and was disregarded. If there is a large anomaly that is
primarily visible at high frequencies, it can be regarded as an area of interest.
● If the anomaly is only seen at high frequencies, generally any frequency above 200 Hz, it
is likely a small anomaly and well attached if not simply an error. While these smaller
anomalies are important, they are not reliably found and classified by LDV
measurements and not as critical to identify.
The measurement is performed by pointing the camera at a portion of the ceiling and defining a
mesh of measurement points on the surface as shown in the following figures.

Figure 3.1.1: Mesh of measurement points overlaid over the fresco image.
Each scan is then analyzed at every frequency from 50 Hz to 500 Hz and the related images
shown in Figure 3.1.2 are combined and overlaid onto the scan photo like the one shown in
Figure 3.1.3.

8
Page 245 of 383

The Catholic University of America, School of Engineering

Figure 3.1.2: Vibrational maps at different frequencies
Figure 3.1.3 is an example of a result where the red areas represent higher mobility zones that
identify the presence of an anomaly.

Figure 3.1.3: Example of result - vibrational map of Franklin

9
Page 246 of 383

The Catholic University of America, School of Engineering

5. Detailed Results Analysis
The vibrational maps shown in the following paragraphs report the velocity of each scanned
point acquired by the vibrometer and averaged over the range of frequencies of interest in order
to increase the signal to noise ratio and to better define the geometry and location of the areas of
elevated mobility. Several anomalies were found at different frequencies depending on the
characteristics of discontinuity revealed by the vibrometer: depth of the anomaly, presence of
inclusions/different materials due to previous restoration. The main areas of higher mobility were
found in the range 80-120 Hz. In some cases the anomaly was well defined as a single frequency
and there was no need for averaging the data. The results shown here are generally within the
90-119 Hz frequency band. This is because the primary and largest areas of elevated surface
mobility are most visible at these lower frequencies due to their first mode being around this
range. In areas with highly elevated surface mobility, this was the dominant frequency band for
the response. In other scans where there is predominantly baseline surface mobility the
frequencies reported are in the same band so as to facilitate comparisons scan to scan. This
would be much more difficult to do accurately if the reported frequencies are not close together.
There can be some variation in the frequencies chosen due to the noise response from scan to
scan resulting in some variability in the best frequencies to choose.

Page 253 of 383

The Catholic University of America, School of Engineering

7.2 Comparison to 2004 Study
This section will compare the results presented in the previous sections to those from our prior
study in 2004. There are several differences between the results and some are simply reflective
of 20 years of improvements to the LDV technology and our ability to process the data. Some
differences are likely due to the deterioration of the fresco. Since 2004 it is unknown whether or
where conservation took place and there has been an earthquake that damaged several historic
buildings in the district. The same post processing techniques were done to both datasets in 2023
though the analysis of the 2023 data was more in depth and thorough. Despite the same post
processing being done on both datasets, the magnitude of surface motion shown is not
comparable between the two datasets. This is due to differences in how the data was taken and
referenced in the two studies. The locations of elevated surface mobility are able to be compared.
The two datasets are shown below at similar frequency bands, with the 2004 dataset being at a
slightly higher band due to differences in equipment used in the experiment. In addition, the
difference in available technology does not invalidate any data from either study. The primary
difference between the two systems used in the two studies is that the newer system allows for
scans covering larger areas of fresco to be taken. This is due to increases in optical sensitivity of
the receiver allowing for lower amplitude excitation and an increase in focal range. It can be seen
that there are many more gaps and small scans from the 2004 study, this is due to the technical
limitations of the system that have since been resolved.
Figures 7.2.1 and 7.2.2 show Liberty and its surroundings both in 2023 and 2004 respectively.
Overall these two datasets show a high degree of similarity both in the areas that show elevated
surface mobility and those that are closer to baseline. The area in the lower left area of Liberty
that shows baseline mobility in 2023 was also baseline in 2004. Overall this area, while having
elevated surface mobility, seems to be fairly stable. The only area that may not be is in the upper
right side of Liberty’s frame.

Figure 7.2.1: 2023 data of Liberty and
surroundings

Figure 7.2.2: 2004 data of Liberty and
surroundings
54
Page 291 of 383

The Catholic University of America, School of Engineering

Figures 7.2.3 and 7.2.4 show the area of Executive and its surroundings in both 2023 and 2004
respectively. The two datasets show some similarities in the locations of elevated surface
mobility however there are some differences. One area that seems to have changed between the
two studies is the left triangle below Executive. In 2004 it did not seem to have an elevated
mobility than its surroundings but in 2023 it does seem to be more mobile than its surroundings.
In addition the area in the upper right of Executive and its frame that showed as baseline with
respect to its surroundings now shows as much smaller in size and may be more unstable than its
surroundings.

Figure 7.2.3: 2023 data of Executive and
surroundings

Figure 7.2.4: 2004 data of Executive and
surroundings

The Catholic University of America, School of Engineering

Figures 7.2.5 and 7.2.6 show Legislation and its surroundings in 2023 and 2004 respectively.
There is a high degree of similarity between these two datasets and the whole area seems largely
stable. This includes the area of higher surface mobility in the right triangle between Legislation
