8. Comparison Between Thermal Camera and Laser Vibrometer Results
Another technique that was used for validation and comparison purposes was the thermography.
Thermographic images of some of the areas were obtained using thermal imaging camera
FLIRT-A70. The camera accurately measure temperatures with up to 640 × 480 (307,200 pixels)
thermal resolution and ±2°C accuracy and is able to reveal thermal detail with low-noise imagery
and data
The following thermal images obtained are compared with the vibrational maps in order to show
the correspondence of results with the vibrational acoustic analysis. While not the primary focus
of this study, This comparison provides insight into the technique. In this study the thermography
was used to preliminarily image the fresco. This data was then used as another comparison and
validation of some of the more significant scanned areas. The two diagnostic methods can be
used together to improve the reliability of results. Moreover thermal imaging allows a quick
survey of the area and gives information on where to focus the acquisition by laser vibrometer.

8.1 Liberty
The comparison between the two images show a good correspondence of the areas of higher
mobility. In this particular case it is evident that the vibrometer is able to give a better definition
of the geometry of the defect. This is due to the fact that the thermal images were acquired
without providing any form of thermal source and this resulted in a low signal to noise ratio. The
quality of results could be much improved if thermal sources are used to heat the surface before
the acquisition.

Figure 8.2: Liberty Thermal map

9. Conclusions
From the results obtained it is possible to conclude that the entire ceiling presents a network of
areas displaying high mobility, and that the ceiling’s structural integrity may have worsened from
the last measurement campaign of 2004.
The main findings and their location are synthesized below:
● Liberty: area of high mobility over almost the entire area with particular concern in
correspondence with the area of the Liberty head and above both on the left and right
side.
● Executive: large area of high mobility spread over almost the entire area.
● Legislation: main areas of high mobility are found in the upper left side extending to the
central left side and in the central upper side.
● Religion: large area with significant areas of high mobility that covers more than half the
scanned surface and extend from the right and cover the majority of the portrait but fade
away after crossing the border.
● Franklin: areas of high mobility in the area above Franklin’s head and in the upper left
corner.
● Franklin lower cherub: minor area of high mobility above the eagle head consistent with
other scans of the area.
● Columbus: extended areas of high mobility spread over almost the entire upper part of the
portrait.
● Columbus upper cherub: large areas of high mobility spread over the entire surface
analyzed.
● Columbus lower cherub: this portion of the ceiling does not outline any areas of high
mobility.
● Vespucius: two main areas of concern at the top left and right side of the scanned surface.
● Vespucius upper cherub: the main areas of concern are in the upper part of the scanned
surface and at the bottom right side where large areas of high mobility are located.
● Vespucius lower cherub: no major anomalies were found.
● Vespucius Legislation triangle: large area of high mobility in the top and center of the
scan consistent with data from neighboring scans.
● Brewster: significant area of high mobility on the upper right corner and in the higher
part of the left side; another area of concern is above Brewster’s head on the left side.
● Brewster lower cherub: no major anomalies were found.
● Brewster Religion triangle: There is an anomaly which spans the top portion of this scan
and is consistent with data from neighboring scans.
● Center Medallion and Franklin Upper Cherub: areas of high mobility have been revealed
in correspondence of the entire eagle, and in the ornament between the two cherubs in the
bottom center and in the frames of Liberty and Legislation.
● Center Medallion and Brewster Upper Cherub: significant areas of high mobility were
found in the bodies of the cherubs and above them and in the frame of Executive.

The CUA team was provided with an audio recording of sounding strikes
on the president’s room frescoes by the ANA team. Below are plots of the
provided audio signal in both the time and frequency domains are in Figure 1a. The signal includes audio of 9 strikes on fresco, the first three on an
area suspected to be well bonded to the substrate, the second group of three
are on an area suspected to be semi-bonded to the substrate, and the third
group of three on an area suspected to be debonded with the substrate. The
strikes are shown and numbered in Figure 1b. In addition the full frequency
response of the entire signal is shown. It can be seen that the majority of the
frequency content occurs below 8000Hz and will be the focus of this report.

(a) Full time history and frequency do- (b) Full time history with strikes highmains.
lighted and numbered.

Figure 1: Plots of the audio signal in time and frequency domains.
Each of the nine total strikes can be isolated and analyzed on its own.
Time windows of a constant length were used to isolate the strikes so as to
1

Page 304 of 383

keep the frequency resolution constant. Figure 2 shows the first three strikes
plotted on top of each other along with their frequency composition. These
three strikes are from the area that is likely well bonded with the substrate.
It can be seen that both the time history and the frequency compositions for
the three strikes are relatively similar which is good.

Figure 2: Plots of y2 in time and frequency domains
One method to analyze audio signals is to analyze in different frequency
bands. This is the same basis for the method that the CUA team uses on the
LDV data. Two groups of bands were measured, the first group were with a
bandwidth of 200Hz and below 1000Hz were 0-200 Hz, 200-400 Hz, 400-600
Hz, 600-800 Hz, and 800-1000 Hz. The second group of bands were with a
bandwidth of 1000 Hz and were under 10,000 Hz were 0-1000 Hz, 1000-2000
Hz... ect. Figure 3 shows the time history of one signal from each type, and
the frequency bands of the response shown in two plots. The first column
has data from strike 2 from the well bonded area, the second column has
data from the strike 5 from the semi-bonded area, and the last column has
data from strike 9 from the debonded area. For each of these columns, the
top plot has the time history of the strike, the middle plot has the frequency
bands less than 1000Hz, and the lower plot has the data from the frequency
bands over 1000Hz.

2

Page 305 of 383

Figure 3: Comparison of bonding areas with frequency bands shown
Data from the bonded area is shown in the first column of plots in Figure 3. In the middle plot, the response over five bands less than 1000 Hz can
be seen. These all have roughly the same peak amplitude, 10−4 dB, however
there is a trend of higher frequency bands having slightly higher maxima
with the exception of the first band. This is also seen in the lower plot for
this group with shows the 1000 Hz bands up to 8000. There is a trend of
the higher bands having slightly increasing maxima but still not deviating
all that much from the lowest band. Data from the semi-bonded area is
shown in the middle column of the plots in Figure 3. The middle plot shows
the bands under 1000 Hz. Like in the bonded section, there is a trend of
higher frequency bands having higher maximum amplitudes after the first
band. Unlike the bonded area, the higher bands are higher than their predecessors. Notably the highest two bands both have peak amplitudes over
10−4 dB. The lower plot shows that the maximum amplitudes of the larger
bands is fairly consistent across the whole frequency range. The last column
has the data from the debonded area. The center plot shows an increase in
frequency content across the 0-1000 Hz spectrum, particularly in the second
two bands. In addition, in the lower plot, the first band of 0-1000 Hz has a
higher maximum than the rest of the bands which supports this finding.

3

Page 306 of 383

Figure 4: Comparison of band level trends
The maximum band levels are plotted in Figure 4. The top plot of Figure 4 shows the results from the first group of bands and the bottom plot
shows the results from the second group of bands. The top plot shows that
the bonded and semi-bonded areas have very similar band levels up until
700Hz where the semi-bonded area diverges. All three bands had similar
initial band levels but the debonded area levels quickly increase and peak at
500Hz. Aside from the initial band, the debonded area levels do not share
any trends with the other two areas below 1000Hz. In the lower plot, all
three areas do follow the same general trend after 1500Hz. After 1500Hz
all three areas levels stay relatively consistent until dropping after 6000Hz.
It should be noted that while the semi-bonded areas and the bonded areas
have similar trends, it does seem that they have a higher band level under
2000Hz than the bonded area. This is likely the cause of the difference in
sound noted by the ANA team.

4

Page 307 of 383

Figure 5: Comparison of frequency spectra
In addition to looking at large frequency bands for comparison, it is also
possible to directly compare frequency spectra of different strikes. Figure 5
shows the smoothed frequency spectra of the strikes in Figure 3. The top left
plot shows all three spectra, the top right plot shows only the bonded and
semi-bonded spectra, the bottom left shows only the bonded and debonded
spectra, and the bottom right shows only the semi-bonded and debonded
spectra. There are several trends to observe in these plots. First is the
increase in debonded frequency magnitude bellow 1000Hz. This area has
much more frequency content for the debonded case than the bonded or
semi-bonded cases. This is due to the debonded area being able to freely
vibrate rather than vibrate with the substrate. After approximately 1500Hz,
the debonded case decreases to match the bonded case. Between approximately 700 and 2000Hz the semi-bonded case has elevated frequency content
with respect to the bonded case. The magnitude of the semi-bonded case
approaches the magnitude of the debonded case under 1000Hz, as if shifted
higher in frequency. Between 2000 and 5000Hz all signals are roughly even in
terms of magnitude with some fluctuations. After 6000Hz, the debonded case
begins to fall off while the other two cases begin falling closer to 7000Hz. This
is likely due to the bonded and semi-bonded areas adherence to the substrate
which adds stiffness to the area and increases the higher frequency response.
Based on the data shown above, areas where debonding is suspected tend
5

Page 308 of 383

to have a different frequency response than properly bonded areas. In general,
debonded areas will have more frequency content between 200 and 1500 Hz.
This is consistent with the methods and techniques that the CUA team have
used in both the 2004 and 2023 studies.

1. Executive Summary
This report presents a laboratory analysis on three plaster samples taken from the President's Room at the United States
Capitol. A full compositional analysis was performed on one of the samples using both petrography and quantitative
chemical analysis. Qualitative petrography alone was performed on the other two samples.
All three samples consist of three-coat plaster applications with scratch coat, brown coat, and a finely sanded finish coat.
The finish coats are all quite thin at only about 1 millimeter thickness. None of the layers contain any type of fiber
reinforcement. The base coats have a soft and permeable paste. The finish coats all have a soft paste but these exhibit a
hydrophobicity that may be related to a more modern treatment.
The base coat aggregates are fine and somewhat narrowly-graded quartz sands with a low content of fines. The finish coat
sand is a clean quartz aggregate that was screened and probably washed. The nominal top size of the finish coat sand is at
the No. 50 sieve. The binder in all three layers is a nonhydraulic intermediate lime. No gypsum-based binders or cements
are identified. For Sample LM, the lime to sand ratios are estimated at approximately 1 : 3.7 for the base coats and 1 : 1.6 for
the finish coat. The materials and proportioning in the other two samples are qualitatively similar.
Certain material features are less than ideal including the absence of fiber reinforcement, the use of intermediate lime, high
sand contents, and a non-ideal aggregate gradation. That aside, there are no obvious placement deficiencies. The plasters are
all relatively compact with no incipient debonds. There is a fair amount of microscopic shrinkage cracking in the base coats
but this is typical of lime-rich plaster mixtures. All layers are fully carbonated and cured.
There are no qualitative features of the examined plasters that clearly correspond to differences in mobility measured through
laser Doppler vibrometry.

Integrated Conservation Resources, Inc.
President's Room, United States Capitol

Page 314 of 383

Report #: SL1934-01
Page 3 of 43

2. Introduction
On April 29, 2024, Highbridge received three plaster samples from Ms. Assya Plavskina of Integrated Conservation
Resources, Inc. The samples are reported to have been taken from a ca. 1850s painted ceiling in the President's Room at the
United States Capitol. The samples are identified as PR-LM-S1, PR-MM-S1, and PR-HM-S1 and represent examples of
plaster that are characterized through laser Doppler vibrometry as low mobility, medium mobility, and high mobility,
respectively. For simplicity, the laboratory has shortened the sample identifications to LM, MM, and HM and will use these
abbreviated identifications throughout the report.
The client has requested a full compositional analysis on the scratch, brown, and finish coats of Sample LM. The testing
includes a petrographic examination and chemical analysis to identify constituents, estimate proportions, and assess overall
condition. Acid digestions to extract sand samples for description and gradation are also included. No analysis is performed
on the decorative finishes. Petrographic analysis is also requested for all three coats of Samples MM and HM. No
quantitative analysis is requested for these two samples. For the set of samples, the client has requested an emphasis on
whether differences in mobility measured nondestructively correspond to differences in material composition or quality.

3. Methods of Examination
Petrographic examination is conducted in accordance with the standard practices contained within ASTM C1324-20a. Data
collection is performed or supervised by a degreed geologist who by nature of their education is qualified to operate the
analytical equipment employed. Analysis and interpretation are performed or directed by a supervising petrographer who
satisfies the qualifications as specified in Section 4 of ASTM C856/C856M-20.
Chemical analysis is performed in general accordance with the procedures outlined in ASTM C1324-20a. Water, carbon
dioxide, and aggregate weight percentages are determined gravimetrically. Oxide weight percentages are determined by
inductively coupled plasma - optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES). While ASTM classifies C1324 as a test method, it is
intended to serve as a guideline for qualified practitioners with ample experience in the various materials under consideration.
Section 10.2 indicates the need for discretion on the part of the laboratory to ensure that methods are tailored to specific
mortar compositions. As such, Highbridge chooses specific digestion methods, supplementary tests, instrumentation
protocols, and mathematical models to best characterize each individual mortar under consideration. Many of these are
proprietary methods that have been researched internally.

4. Notes on Sample Preparation
For the petrographic examination, each sample was sawn by hand using a fine-toothed jeweler's saw. The cutting was done
to produce a cross section perpendicular to the plaster surface. The cross sections were placed under vacuum to remove as
much ambient moisture as possible without using heat. Following this treatment, the cross sections were each impregnated
with a low-viscosity, blue-dyed epoxy. These stabilized billets were then used to produce petrographic thin sections that
preserved the in situ relationships of all represented plaster layers.
Sample LM was used for additional testing including chemical analysis and sand gradation. These tests required each layer
to be carefully separated for individual analysis. The finish coat was sampled first. The sides of the plaster were coated with
a high-viscosity, non-penetrating epoxy to prevent contamination with the base coat material. Once the epoxy cured, a minichisel was used to carefully carve out the finish without penetrating the outermost surface of the base coat. The collected
powder was set aside.
The epoxy border was unwrapped to expose the base coats. The petrographic thin section was laid over the plaster cross
section so that the lift boundaries could be traced onto the plaster itself with a felt-tipped marker. A putty knife was used to
clean off the base of the scratch coat and ensure that no substrate materials would contaminate the subsample. The jeweler's
saw was used to cut away the scratch coat without crossing into the brown coat. The sampled scratch coat was set aside.
The putty knife was used to scrape away the rest of the scratch coat so that it did not contaminate the brown coat. Once
cleaned, the jeweler's saw was used to cut away the first of the two brown coat layers in this sample. The cutting was done
fully within the first brown coat to avoid contamination with the overlying coat. The sampled brown coat was set aside.
The sampled layers were divided for chemical analysis and sand extraction. More intact pieces were favored for the
chemical analysis to ensure that the binder to sand ratio was properly represented. The remainder of each sample was
reserved for the sand extraction. Ultimately, the highly similar results for scratch coat and brown coat suggest that the
subsampling was accurate at least for the base coats.

Integrated Conservation Resources, Inc.
President's Room, United States Capitol

Page 316 of 383

Report #: SL1934-01
Page 5 of 43

5. Petrographic Findings and Discussion
5.1 - General Appearance
The three plaster samples provided for examination are all similar in appearance and quality. Total thicknesses are generally
about 20 to 25 millimeters though Sample HM is a bit thinner. The majority of each sample consists of base coat material.
Thicknesses of scratch coats and brown coats are reported in Table 5.1. The base coats are sandy, dull, and nearly white in
color (Munsell color code approximately 1Y 8.5/0.75). The binder paste is soft and the matrix is highly permeable. Though
the base coats are cohesive, small pieces are easily disaggregated with mild finger pressure. Finish coats are only about 1
millimeter in thickness. These are brighter white in color (Munsell code approximately N9.5). Though the paste is soft, the
finish coat material is highly hydrophobic. The client has indicated that there is a record of paint consolidation. It is possible
that the hydrophobicity derives from this treatment.
Table 5.1: Plaster Layer Thicknesses
Sample

5.2 - Materials
All base coat layers contain a quartz-rich natural sand. With the exception of some trace clay, the aggregate is clean and
contains no coatings or friable materials that would cause it to be unsuitable for plaster use. Samples of the scratch coat and
brown coat sand were chemically extracted from Sample LM. These are similar in appearance with an average pale brown
color (Munsell color code approximately 10YR 6.5/2). There is some mild color variegation produced by lesser feldspar and
trace ironstone.
The base coat sands are sharp-textured. The sands extracted from Sample LM are identically graded. Petrographic
observations suggest that the base coat sands in Samples MM and HM have approximately similar particle size distributions.
For the aggregates in Sample LM, the nominal top size is found at the No. 8 sieve and the peak abundance is between the No.
30 and No. 50 sieves. The gradation is somewhat narrow with approximately 60% retained within the peak interval.
Furthermore, there is only about 15% passing the No. 50 sieve. Details of the gradation profiles are presented in Section 6.
The finish coat aggregates are very fine, light-colored sands consisting almost entirely of quartz. The quality suggests that
they were manufactured, screened, and washed. A sample extracted from Sample LM is semi-translucent and uniform in
appearance with a buff color (Munsell color code approximately 10YR 7.75/0.75). The finish coat sand is sharp-textured. A
gradation analysis was made for the one extracted sample. This has a nominal top size at the No. 50 sieve and about 90%
retained between the No. 50 and No. 200 sieves.
The binder in all plaster layers is a nonhydraulic lime. Though rare contaminants of gypsum-lime paste and natural cement
are detected petrographically, these are clearly contaminants from other construction activities. The lime chemistry was
determined for Sample LM (Table 7.2). All three layers of this sample contain an intermediate lime with roughly 10%
magnesia on a free lime basis. There is also a fair amount of soluble silica. In contrast with a high-calcium fat lime, this lime
would have been relatively slow to slake and would have produced less volume of putty for the same weight of raw lime.

Integrated Conservation Resources, Inc.
President's Room, United States Capitol

Page 317 of 383

Report #: SL1934-01
Page 6 of 43

There is scant petrographic evidence indicating the lime source. There are two small marble fragments identified in Samples
MM and HM that might represent the source rock for the lime. Otherwise, only traces of fired quartz and undifferentiated
clinker particles are detected in the lime paste. The intermediate composition is certainly more typical of marble lime than
other varieties. Plausible contemporary sources of marble lime with this intermediate chemistry include the Texas and
Beaver Dam Quarries of the Cockeysville, MD region and the Thomaston lime from Rockland, ME.
5.3 - Component Proportions
Chemical analysis was used to determine the mix proportions of the plaster layers in Sample LM. Details are presented in
Section 7. For the base coat layers, the lime putty to sand ratio is estimated at 1 : 3.7 and 1 : 3.6 for the scratch coat and
brown coat respectively. The finish coat is richer with a putty to sand ratio of approximately 1 : 1.6. Considering that the
plaster would have been proportioned by hand, the similarity in proportioning of the two base coats suggest that the layers
may have used the same batch of plaster. Large batches would likely have been prepared in advance and banked for later
use.
Chemical analysis was not performed for the other two samples. Qualitatively speaking, the distribution of materials appears
similar between all samples when viewed petrographically. However, when cementitious mixtures are heavily sanded, it can
be difficult to distinguish differences in sand content of several tens of percent.
5.4 - Placement and Workmanship
Based on the petrographic observations, all of the aggregates appear to have been relatively well washed. Silt and clay
represents less than 1% of the sand in the base coats as measured in Sample LM. The finish coat sand is even cleaner. The
lime was very well prepared in the base coats. Undispersed lumps are relatively uncommon suggesting that lime was either
ground before slaking or screened after slaking. In either case, the lime was thoroughly slaked. There is no evidence for
subsequent slaking after hardening despite the higher magnesium content of the lime. Lime particles with diffuse grain
boundaries are more common in all three finish coats. Still, the material is thoroughly slaked. The lime and sand is observed
to have been well blended in all of the layers observed petrographically. There are no significant sand or lime streaks.
Photographs supplied by the client indicate that thin residues of the scratch coat remain adhered to the clay brick substrate
after sampling. This suggests that there was some degree of suction that allowed for initial adhesion of the scratch coats.
Technically, the base of each layer is a cohesive break within the scratch coat. Each subsequent layer was well compacted.
Lift boundaries show good adhesion and there are no incipient debonds observed petrographically. Internally, the
consolidation of the base coats was adequate though a moderate percentage of fine consolidation voids are observed in the
interstitial space between closely-packed sand particles. This is a function of the relatively high sand contents. Though the
mix designs were not quantified for Samples MM and HM, there are no obvious differences in the density and distribution of
the plaster constituents. Finish coats are more thoroughly consolidated in all samples. With minor exception, total air
contents in these layers never exceeds a few percent.
The base coats in all layers exhibit a moderately high concentration of discontinuous microscopic shrinkage cracks. These
are highly typical of lime-rich mortars. While indicative of the weaker quality of lime-based mixes, these are not necessarily
a deficiency. Much of the cracking is randomly oriented. However, the microscopic cracking in Samples MM and HM tend
to be more perpendicular to the ceiling surface as the finish coat is approached. This is a typical response to the presence of a
free surface where principal stresses must rotate to lie within that surface. Though the orientation of microcracks is similar in
the three finish coats, the cracking is much less prevalent.

Integrated Conservation Resources, Inc.
President's Room, United States Capitol

Page 318 of 383

Report #: SL1934-01
Page 7 of 43

5.5 - Condition and Service Performance
All of the lime paste is fully carbonated and this is a normal and desirable consequence of curing. There is no evidence for
any deleterious service effects. There are no secondary mineralizations aside from the carbonation and no chemical
degradation of the binder. All microscopic cracking observed is attributed to early drying shrinkage of the lime paste. These
cracks are considered dormant.
5.6 - Commentary
All three samples have characteristics that are fairly typical of nineteenth century lime plasters. With that said, there are
several features that may be considered to have impacted the overall quality of the cured products:
•
•
•
•

The base coats contain no hair reinforcement. Natural fibers serve to arrest any fine cracks that form in plaster and
help the material remain cohesive.
The intermediate lime identified in Sample LM is not necessarily deficient. However, it offers neither the richness
and plasticity of a fat lime nor the strength gain of a purely dolomitic lime.
The sand contents are somewhat high in the base coats of Sample LM particularly for the intermediate lime. All else
being equal, cohesive strength has an inverse correlation with sand content.
The sand is somewhat narrowly graded and a bit deficient in fine particles passing the No. 50 sieve. A better
gradation profile may have improved the cohesive strength and reduced early shrinkage.

The client has distinguished the three samples based on the degree of mobility measured using laser Doppler vibrometry.
Presumably, higher mobility would be an indicator of lower material density, internal damage, or delamination from the
substrate. One goal of the study was to evaluate whether the differences in mobility correlate with any qualitative or
quantitative material differences. Since a chemical analysis and sand gradation were not performed for all three samples, it is
possible that there are quantitative differences in mix design that are not distinguished. However, as previously described,
the petrographic examination reveals no obvious qualitative differences in the original materials or design.
It should be stressed that there are some aspects of a plaster installation and service history that cannot be observed through
laboratory testing. These can include poor initial suction, poor keying, deformations of the structure, and long-term
embrittlement of the matrix. Any of these can have caused what were once identical mixtures to have aged differently.
The client has suggested that the degree of disaggregation experienced in handling may correlate with the degree of mobility
represented by the three samples (i.e., the high mobility sample is more friable than the low mobility sample). The author
does not perceive such an obvious difference in matrix friability. However, if it is stipulated that there is a mechanical
difference in the three samples such that the high mobility sample is more friable, consideration should be given to the
possibility that the environment rather than the plaster designs are responsible. For example, greater structural movements or
thermal cycling may have been experienced where high mobility is now measured. These can have caused progressive
weakening of the plasters in the high mobility areas even where these were materially identical to the low mobility plasters.
While this type of interpretation is conjectural, it is certainly not ruled out by the petrographic evidence.

Integrated Conservation Resources, Inc.
President's Room, United States Capitol

Page 319 of 383

Report #: SL1934-01
Page 8 of 43

6. Aggregate Sieve Analysis
Aggregates were extracted from Sample LM by digesting each layer in a dilute hydrochloric acid solution. Any fines
remaining in suspension were decanted off separately and are assumed to represent fine silt and clay. The sand that settled at
the bottom of the solution was cleaned, dried, and graded through a standard sieve stack. The "Pan" reported in the following
tables contains coarse silt that is finer than a No. 200 sieve but settled from suspension after a few seconds. The suspended
material that was decanted off represents finer silt and clay. These are reported as "Fines" in the following tables. For the
purposes of reporting a gradation, the fines are considered to "pass" the pan.
While every attempt was made to subsample the finish coat cleanly as described in Section 4, the dried aggregate from the
finish coat clearly contained some more strongly-colored sand that must have derived from the underlying brown coat. A
No. 40 sieve was used to screen out these coarser grains. These are excluded from the gradation analysis below. There may
still be some particles retained on the No. 50 sieve that are contaminants from the brown coat. However, this error is
considered quite minor.

7. Chemical Analysis
Chemical analysis was performed on Sample LM only. For each of the plaster layers in Sample LM, the digestion procedure
was modified from that in ASTM C1324 to account for the lime binder. The method includes a room temperature acid
digestion alone, and the base digestion is excluded. The insoluble residue was determined directly from the initial digestion
rather than through the digestion of a separate aliquot.

Table 7.2: Estimated Lime Chemistry
The binder in each of the three layers of Sample LM is interpreted to consist of a nonhydraulic lime with no other additives.
As such, the lime chemistry is estimated from the total chemical analysis presented in Table 7.1. The five major oxides in the
binder are normalized to a 99% weight yield. This normalizes the lime to a dry weight basis and is equivalent to the preslaked condition. The residual 1% is assumed to represent trace unmeasured constituents. Important chemical indices are
calculated as follows:

The following features are worth noting:
•

•

•

The CaO content is high but less than 90% in each layer. The CaO/MgO ratios are calculated between 6.7 and 8.9. The
moderate magnesium content of the lime is typical of so-called intermediate limes where the lime is not dolomitic but
there is still a notable magnesium content.
Minor siliceous impurities are noted petrographically in association with the lime. However, the low hydraulicity and
cementation indices indicate that the lime is entirely nonhydraulic. A lime would need to have a cementation index of at
least 0.40 to result in even a weak hydraulicity (Eckel, 171).
The lime chemistries are nearly identical between the scratch and brown coats. In fact, the three chemistries are quite
similar in all three layers. This suggests that all of the layers are contemporaneous and that the binders represent the
same material source.

Notes:
1. Volumetric ratios are calculated assuming bulk densities for nonhydraulic lime and damp, loose sand of 40 lbs./ft.3 and 80 lbs./ft.3, respectively. Another
calculation is provided assuming the lime in putty form. This assumes a unit of dry lime hydrate will lose approximately 20% of its volume when mixed to the
consistency of a stiff paste.

The lime is calculated from the five measured oxides (Table 7.1) assuming that the CaO and MgO were present as hydrates,
and the other phases as pure oxides. The sand is taken directly from the acid-insoluble residue.
Two separate volume estimates are given in Table 7.3. One is given with the lime in the form of a dry hydrate despite the
fact that the lime mortar examined for this report predates the commercial availability of prepackaged hydrated lime.
Calculating the lime as a dry hydrate is convenient because it does not have to take into account the mix water used in a lime
putty. It also allows for a more direct assessment of possible repair formulations using modern bagged masonry lime.
Nonetheless, it is generally assumed that a volume of dry hydrate will lose approximately 20% of its volume when water is
added to produce a putty of stiff consistency. This assumption is the basis for the volume estimate reported for lime putty.
This ratio is derived from the dry hydrate calculation.
It should also be noted that where volume proportions are given, these are based on estimated original bulk densities of the
materials. However, limes are subject to great variation in volume due to factors such as settling or “fluffing” in dry powders
and mix water content in putties. Table 7.3 also presents the weight percentages of the dry ingredients (dry hydrated lime
and sand). These are more accurate as they represent direct measurements of material mass and are not based on assumptions
of bulk density.
Of course, all of these discussions may be academic since it is usually desirable to modify the original design if modern
materials will be used to replace their historic counterparts. The findings of a plaster analysis are best used to constrain
possible repair designs rather than as a prescription for a specific formulation.

References
Eckel, Edwin C. Cements, Limes, and Plasters. Their Materials, Manufacture, and Properties. New York: Robert Drummond, 1905.

The sample consists of one full-depth plaster panel with areal dimensions of 7.5 cm x 6.9 cm. The thickness
ranges from 2.3 to 2.7 cm. Based on the hand sample view, the plaster consists of the following layers:
•
Scratch coat of approximately 6 to 10 millimeters thickness.
•
Two brown coats. The first is approximately 11 millimeters thick and the second is approximately 5
millimeters thick.
•
Finish coat of approximately 1 millimeter thickness.
The inner surface is roughly planar. A few mortar residues from the substrate are adhered to the base of the
plaster.
All material layers are intimately bonded along apparently planar surfaces.
The outer surface is a planar troweled surface covered in decorative paint.
All base coat layers are similar in appearance and quality. Freshly exposed surfaces have a dull luster and a
nearly white color (Munsell color code approximately 1Y 8.5/0.75). Millimeter-sized white binder inclusions
are occasionally observed. No fiber addition is noted. The paste is soft. Though the mortar is cohesive, the
matrix is easily disaggregated with mild finger pressure. The matrix is very rapidly water-absorptive.
Freshly exposed surfaces of the finish coat are dull-lustered and white (Munsell color code approximately N9.5).
It is difficult to probe the layer due to its thinness. The paste is clearly soft and appears hydrophobic.
No macroscopic cracking is visible in hand sample.

Placement features
Constituent distribution
Mix water
Base coat placement

Finish coat placement

The plaster constituents were well dispersed in all layers.
There is no variation in paste density that would suggest inconsistent distribution of mix water within any of the
plaster lifts.
Lift planes between base coat layers are planar and exhibit no sign of incipient debonding. All base coats are
compact and well consolidated. Still, air contents are moderately high in all layers. The volumes are difficult to
estimate due to the presence of shrinkage cracking though most appear somewhat greater than 10% by volume.
Finer voids tend to be spherical to subspherical. Void shapes are more amoeboid where coarser in size. Voids
are generally less than 1 millimeter in diameter.
The finish coat is compact against the brown coat and the contact surface is tight. The finish coat plaster is well
consolidated. Air voids are sporadically distributed. Most areas contain less than 2% air. There are minor
regions where the air content is locally higher and as much as 10%.

•
Scratch coat of up to approximately 9 millimeters thickness.
•
Brown coat between 16 and 18 millimeters thickness.
•
Finish coat of approximately 1 millimeter thickness.
The inner surface is irregular in shape.
All material layers are intimately bonded along apparently planar surfaces.
The outer surface is a planar troweled surface covered in decorative paint.
All base coat layers are similar in appearance and quality. Freshly exposed surfaces have a dull luster and a
nearly white color (Munsell color code approximately 1Y 8.5/0.75). Millimeter-sized white binder inclusions
are rare. No fiber addition is noted. The paste is soft. Though the mortar is cohesive, the matrix is easily
disaggregated with mild finger pressure. The matrix is very rapidly water-absorptive.
Freshly exposed surfaces of the finish coat are dull-lustered and white (Munsell color code approximately N9.5).
It is difficult to probe the layer due to its thinness. The paste is clearly soft and appears hydrophobic.
No macroscopic cracking is visible in hand sample.

The plaster constituents were well dispersed in all layers.
There is no variation in paste density that would suggest inconsistent distribution of mix water within any of the
plaster lifts.
Lift planes between base coat layers are planar and exhibit no sign of incipient debonding. Both base coats are
compact and well consolidated. Still, air contents are moderately high in all layers. The volumes are difficult to
estimate due to the presence of shrinkage cracking though most appear somewhat greater than 10% by volume.
Finer voids tend to be spherical to subspherical. Void shapes are more amoeboid where coarser in size. Voids
are generally less than 1 millimeter in diameter.
The finish coat is compact against the brown coat and the contact surface is tight. The finish coat plaster is well
consolidated. Fine air-voids are estimated at less than 3% by volume.

Appendix I: Photographs and Photomicrographs
Microscopic examination is performed on an Olympus BX-51 polarized/reflected light microscope and an Olympus SZ40
stereoscopic microscope. The polarized light microscope is fitted with a Tucsen MIchrome 5 Pro 5MP digital camera. The
stereoscopic microscope is used for simple magnification. Sample types examined under this microscope include fractured
surfaces, fine constituents extracted through chemical or physical means, or honed or polished cross sections. The polarized
light microscope (PLM) magnifies but also employs principles of optical crystallography. The most common sample
preparation for the PLM is the petrographic thin section. For this preparation, cross-sectioned samples are mounted to glass
slides and are milled to a thickness sufficient to allow light to be transmitted through the material. These are usually prepared
without water and with minimal heat to avoid altering minerals that are water or temperature-sensitive. In many cases, the
samples are impregnated with a low-viscosity, blue-dyed epoxy. When so treated, blue areas represent some type of void
space (e.g., air-voids, capillary pores, cracks, etc.). The polarized light photomicrographs are taken using a variety of optical
settings chosen to best demonstrate the feature(s) of interest. These are distinguished as follows:
Plane polarized light (abbreviated as PPL)
This method uses the refractive power of different constituents to produce an artificial sense of surface relief. Otherwise, the
method is the closest to a simple magnification of the material. The setting is often used to demonstrate granular
relationships or microstructure. Pore spaces and cracks are observable with this setting if the blue-dyed epoxy is used.
Conoscopic polarized light (abbreviated as CPL)
In this setting, the transmitted light is condensed just before passing through the thin section. The method tends to bring
colors or finer particulates into higher contrast at the expense of image sharpness. The setting is often used to image grain
boundary failures in dimension stone, pigment particulates in binders, or gel phases in the micropores of cement pastes.
Cross polarized light (abbreviated as XPL)
The setting places the thin section between two pieces of polarizing film oriented at 90° to one another. In isotropic materials
(e.g., glasses, simple salts), all light is absorbed and the materials appear black. In anisotropic crystals, two light rays
traveling at different speeds are produced within the thin section and these offset waves interfere at the upper polarizing film.
The interference produces a color that can be used to calculate properties of the crystal structure and aid in identification of
mineral species. In essence, the colors are artificial. It should be noted that color is a function of orientation and color
differences do not necessarily indicate material differences.
Compensator plates
When in XPL mode, full-wave or quarter-wave compensator plates may be inserted into the light path to add or subtract
interference. Technically, these methods are used to calculate properties of the crystal structure. However, they can also be
used to alter the image appearance to help improve contrast between different constituents. They can also reveal preferred
orientations in some materials (e.g., oriented residual crystallinity in fired ceramics).

Heading Definitions:
Total Plaster Depth: Drill distance (mm) from decorated plaster surface to brisk
Finish Coat Depth: Drill distance (mm) of finish coat identified by initial spike in force
Base Coat Depth: Drill distance (mm) of base coat identified by continuous low but variable force
Air Gap: Drill distance (mm) where force was constant and near zero

########## J.5 2024-08-27 Memo of Advisory Panel Comments on ICR Final Report.pdf ##########

UNCLASSIFIED // For Official Use Only

MEMORANDUM
Date: August 27, 2024
To: Ece Kilic, AOC Project Manager

Work Order Number: 90305.018

From: Lonnie Hovey, AIA, FAPT

Contract Number: AOC19C3007

Subject: Comments on ICR Final Report

Project: President’s Rm. Advisory Panel

CC: M. Oehrlein, B. Ramsay, T. Williams, File
Members of the President’s Room Advisory Panel reviewed the PHASE 1-3 DRAFT REPORT on the PRESIDENT’S
ROOM CEILING ANALYSIS submitted August 2, 2024 by Integrated Conservation Resources, Inc. (ICR), and
provided the following overall and specific comments and suggestions to improve the report for the project record.
Overall comments:
•

The ICR report needs more explanation to develop a compelling case for the recommendation to shore the
ceiling. The slides shown in the August 16 presentation will bolster this explanation, but more is likely
needed.

•

The ICR report needs an Executive Summary to concisely describe the reasons for testing, the testing done,
findings, and recommendations.

•

Similarly, the goals of paint analysis should be described in the final ICR report to focus the information
presented. Slides in the GPS presentation outlined their goals and findings very clearly.

•

The report should answer the question: Which NDT methods are reliable predictors of voids behind the
plaster, and which are not?

•

The entire ICR report needs copy editing to eliminate spelling errors and other typographical errors.

•

The recommendation is reiterated that CUA’s report be updated to provide greater clarity in what they are
measuring (mobility) vs. what they are inferring (delamination). The report mischaracterizes their findings in
the absence of physical data (which they did not have at the time of their testing). If this is not updated, ICR’s
report needs to make a clear statement prior to CUA’s report explaining this difference.

•

The report needs a prioritized and clear list of recommendations, including clarification of what constitutes
“monitoring,” recommended frequency of “regular monitoring,” and by whom. Clear, actionable steps for AOC
must be provided.

•

Put bookmarks in the final report pdf, in order to navigate 300+ pages.

Specific comments:
•

Page 3: Brumidi worked in the Capitol primarily between 1855-60 and intermittently after.

•

Page 4, para. 1: “The ceiling is assumed to be a brick vault 12” deep” – did ICR receive original drawings,
and can we update to state what drawings show?

•

Page 4, para. 2 and Fig. 3: can we update based on recent testing?

•

Page 7: what is the difference between “deflected” and “detached” plaster as defined here?

•

Page 7: SLDV definition – add that it uses sound to create the displacement.

UNCLASSIFIED // For Official Use Only
Document2

UNCLASSIFIED // For Official Use Only

August 27, 2024

Page 2

90305.018

•

Page 9: Not certain why you have reached this conclusion re: alterations of the interstitial space. Re:
systems in the ceiling of the room. MO indicates that there are none except electrical and old gas piping.
There is no plumbing in the space above, the roof drains are vertical and located in exterior wall chases.
Those that have been rerouted to the interior are still vertical, not concealed in the floors.

•

Page 11: 1921: At some undocumented point in time after the failure of the plaster at the center of the ceiling
a painted canvas was installed to cover the new plaster. The painting was apparently not well executed and
with the permission of the prior curator, Barbara Wolanin, the canvas was removed during the 1995
conservation work. It was left in the curator’s office, current disposition unknown.

•

Page 11: 1920-28 The fresco repairs by Comins would have required removal of the damaged plaster. Text
in GPS report indicates “repainting.”

•

Page 13: “Any offsets in the cracks were smaller than the density of the laser scan” – what was the density?
Please provide the density of the laser scanning. There is no description anywhere in the document.

•

Page 14: somewhere please indicate that all photographs taken during the investigation have been
submitted to the AOC. We assume, but it is not stated.

•

Page 14, Fig. 7: Use an arrow to clarify area of retouching (or clarify in caption)

•

Page 15, Fig. 8: Use arrows to identify areas of efflorescence (professionals know, but others don’t) as it
looks quite similar to the figure’s veil.

•

Page 15, Figs 9-10: Need arrows (maybe multiple colors for Fig. 10 if multiple conditions shown)

•

Fig. 11: Please document the locations of the reveals in an overall plan.

•

Page 18, Fig. 12: Fix caption to be more precise about red and orange areas. Fig. 12 is a map of sounds, not
a map of attachment/detachment.

•

Page 19, 20, Fig. 13 & 14: Is this the 2003 data or the most recent data? Please add date and clarify that
the mapping represents only vibration differentials not areas of detached plaster.

•

Page 19, 20, Fig. 13 & 14: Need units on scale. I don’t see any colors in the range besides blue and red. No
purple. Even the light red in the image isn’t represented on the scale. Clarify.

•

Page 20, Fig. 14 – remove “detachment” from caption, as this hasn’t been shown. This is the same image as
in Fig 13, showing mobility/vibration – not detachment.

•

Page 20: Discussion of 2003 (page 19) or 04 (?) and 2023 comparison again characterizes the scans as
detachment. Please correct.

•

Page 21, para. 1 – plaster ceiling is 1-1.5” thick.

•

Page 21, Crack Mapping, Methodology: what about cracks between 0.14-0.016” thick? (typo?)
o

How do you differentiate between “cracks”, “crazing”, and “fissures”?

•

Page 21, Findings: “suspected” correlation between sounding and giornata is unfounded per Figure 14,
which shows giornata in blue and red areas. The giornata are in both low and high areas and cross over both
areas.

•

Page 22 and 25: “signs of water infiltration” these have not been previously described or discussed. Where
are these locations and what are the indicators of moister infiltration?

UNCLASSIFIED // For Official Use Only
Document2

UNCLASSIFIED // For Official Use Only

August 27, 2024

Page 3

90305.018

•

Page 26-27: Reference Figs 21 and 22 in the text. Clarify that “LM” and “MM” are the low and medium
mobility samples. Please include the photos of the high mobility sample removal. Please use the sample
numbers in the text and figures and provide a full ceiling image to show the sample locations.

•

Page 26 Findings: mortar remained attached to the back of the plaster sample, but the base coat did not
appear keyed into the mortar joints. How can this be?

•

Page 28: Does the petrography help us understand the causes of observed cracking?

•

Page 29: Isn’t high mobility also correlated to plaster thickness?

•

Page 29, para. 2: Water entering plaster from the brick? New information – what is the basis? Need to
explain earlier in the report. The signs of water infiltration have not been previously described. The posit that
moisture from the brick has weakened the plaster is not substantiated.

•

Page 29: Fig. 25 seems out of place and comes from nowhere. Figure 25 is unexplained, what surface
conditions? Should be back in Observations/Findings, not analysis. What is the basis for this? What “surface
conditions” lead to the inference of moisture infiltration? “substantial portions of the ceiling are detached” is
not substantiated.

•

Page 29, para. 3: “though substantial portions of the ceiling are detached” – this is not supported by the data.
Rewrite.

•

Page 29: detachment was between brick and base coat. Not consistent w/photos, which show thin layer of
base coat on brick.

•

Page 30, Fig. 26: last column – find other words for “attached”, “damaged”, and “borderline” – these are NDT
results and need to describe vibration, not inferred attachment.

•

Page 30, Fig. 26: Samples 10, 12, and 13 do not have good correlation. 3 out of 13.

•

Page 30, Fig. 26: Add plaster thickness as a column.

•

Page 30, Fig. 26: Sample 13: what does “(AP)” mean? Include all samples in the chart.

•

Page 30: Fix caption of Fig. 27 – does not indicate detachment; indicates sound and vibration, respectively.
Include locations of drills on both images in Fig 27.

•

Page 31, Fig. 28: remove “attached” from caption. Does not indicate attachment; indicates sound.

•

Page 30-32, Figs. 27 - 29: correct descriptions. The mapping does not indicate detachment or compromised
plaster. Detachment is being surmised by interpretation of all data.

########## J.6 ICR - Presidents Room - GPR Findings Memo.pdf ##########

MEMO
To:

From:

Desmond Scott, Contracting Officer
Architect of the Capitol
Desmond.Scott@aoc.gov

President’s Room Ceiling Analysis

RE:
Presidents Room Ceiling – Surface Penetrating Radar Assessment
Integrated Conservation Resources, Inc. (ICR) is providing this memo at the request of the
Architect of the Capitol. The purpose of this memo is to outline the findings of Surface
Penetrating Radar performed at the Press Room, above the Presidents Room, in relation to
plaster conditions identified during the Presidents Room Ceiling Phase 1-4 assessment, and as
discussed in detail with AOC over several meeting and email exchanges in August-October
2024.
As part of ICR’s Presidents Room Plaster Ceiling Phase 1-4 Report, a phased plan of
recommendations was provided that would allow the team to better understand both the root
cause of detachment and compromised plaster, as well as if any of the causes of the plaster
conditions are ongoing. AOC approved performing Surface Penetrating Radar at the Press
Room above the Presidents Room, one of the steps in the first phase of recommendations.
On October 30th, 2024, ICR and Atkinson-Noland and Associates (ANA) performed a site visit
to the US Capitol during which Surface Penetrating Radar was performed in the spaces above
the Presidents Room, in order to better understand the cross section of the brick vault above
the plaster ceiling and identify any anomalies within the ceiling structure. The attached report
from ANA outlines the methodology and findings of this assessment.
The Surface Penetrating Radar allowed ANA to identify the location of utility lines as well as
map the cross section of the vault. Additionally, several anomalies within the vault were
identified, including a steel ring around the perimeter of the room as well as isolated locations
of potential internal voiding at the collar joint in the brick vault. As noted in the
recommendations of the Phase 1-4 report, the information gained from this assessment is not
sufficient to advise on root cause or ongoing issues, However, the information found in this
assessment allows the team to make informed decisions on locations and placements of
probes and environmental and vibration monitoring systems.

1.
INTRODUCTION
On October 30th, 2024, Gary Ogden of Atkinson-Noland and Associates Inc. (ANA) was onsite at the US
Capitol to conduct nondestructive testing of the ceiling in The President’s Room (Senate Room S-216).
All investigations were performed from above The President’s Room, on the floor of the Press Room (S316). Michelle Leach of ICR facilitated site access to the Capitol and assisted ANA during the
investigations. This investigation follows previous phases of investigation to further determine the asbuilt construction of The President’s Room’s vaulted ceiling.
Testing performed by ANA, during this site visit, included surface penetrating radar (SPR). The following
report summarizes test locations, methods, and results relating to nondestructive testing of the floor in
the Press Room. Figure 1 provides the extent of ANA’s investigation. An image from a previous
renovation within the Capitol was used as a reference to help further understand the construction of the
vaulted floors.

Figure 1. Plan view of the Press Room (S-316) outlining the approximate extent of ANA’s investigations. Background
by others.

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room: Press Room SPR

& Associates

Pg. 2

11/21/2024

Figure 2. Image of an exposed floor during a previous renovation (not at the Press Gallery). Visible in the image are
embedded pipes, the fill material, and the brick vault system. Image provided by ICR.

2.
INVESTIGATIVE TESTING (PRESS ROOM)
2.1. Microwave Radar Scanning
Surface penetrating radar (SPR) was used to assess the cross-section of the masonry vault of the
President’s Room as well as portions of the adjacent rooms, by scanning from above in the Press Room.
The SPR method involves the transmission of high frequency electromagnetic radio (radar) pulses into
the object of interest and measuring the time elapsed between transmission, reflection from subsurface
features, and reception back at the surface radar antenna. A pulse of radar energy is generated on a
dipole-transmitting antenna that is placed on the surface of the object of interest. Reflections of the
radar energy occur at discontinuities due to changes in the dielectric properties of the underlying
material. Void spaces, metal presence, and any distinct change in material dielectric will generate radar
reflections due to a change in radar wave velocity at the interface. Scanning was conducted with a
Proceq GP800o unit with stepped frequencies from 200 to 4000 MHz.
The average relative dielectric permittivity of the masonry assemblage was determined by matching the
curvature of a hyperbolic reflection in the SPR data using the manufacturer’s software installed on an
Apple iPad as well as matching the shape of the vaulted ceiling in the scan data to Lidar Scanning
previously completed by ICR. Determining the relative dielectric permittivity allows for the
determination of the radar velocity in the substrate and the correct interpretation of the depth of
features in the radar transects. Figure 3, below, provides an example SPR trace recorded from the Press
Room. Annotations indicate the identified features of the floor assembly.

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room: Press Room SPR

& Associates

Pg. 3

11/21/2024

Figure 3. SPR transect recorded from the floor of the Press Room. The image shows a scan taken from east to west
across the floor. Markups highlight the interpretation of the scan including embedded elements within the floor
systems as well as the overall construction. The horizontal axis is in feet and inches, the vertical axis is in inches.

Based on the investigation, ANA was able to further determine and refine previous results for the as-built
construction of the President’s Room ceiling. The floor system was constructed of tiles with an assumed
settling bed on top of a subfloor. Repetitive reflections present at this depth likely indicate a subfloor
system with wooden sleepers. Note that the sleepers appear to be orientated in a different direction over
the President’s Room versus the entryway, which is why they are not visible for the entire scan. The
presence of sleepers in the corridor was inconclusive. The structural system was a two-wythe brick arch
system, with fill material above. The adjacent vaulted ceilings have similar structures based on the
scanning. These conclusions are similar to the results from the SPR scanning completed previously from
below. The major difference was the brick vault thickness was determined to be 8 inches thick (compared
to the 12-inch thickness determined from previous phases). A cross-section based on the findings is
provided in Appendix A.
Scanning from above also identified the metallic element near the spring line of the vault. During
previous phases, the reflection was assumed to be a radial tension ring. Given its depth compared to the
other identified utility lines, it further indicates that this is not an embedded utility line. Reflections
within the brick vault (dashed yellow line in Figure 3) was also consistent with previous results completed
from below. These reflections were typically present on the upper third of the vaulted ceiling and may
indicate voiding within the collar joint of the brick vault.
Several lines of embedded utilities, such as gas, electric, or water lines, were identified within the floor
system. These utilities were typically 4 to 4.5 inches from the top surface of the floor. The position of the

Atkinson-Noland

President’s Room: Press Room SPR

& Associates

Pg. 4

11/21/2024

identified utility lines is provided in Appendix B. Additionally, ANA understands the chandelier in the
President's Room was historically gas and is currently hung from the ceiling using the abandoned gas
line. The scanning confirmed that an embedded element was present directly over the chandelier,
indicating that some of the embedded utility lines are likely historic gas lines.

########## J.7 Capitol Building HazMat Requirements July 2019 - FINAL 1.pdf ##########

Requirements for Conservators Regarding the Handling of
Hazardous Chemicals, Hazardous Waste, and PCB Wastes
at the Capitol Building and CVC
In addition to AOC requirements related to hazardous waste and polychlorinated biphenyl
(PCB) wastes, the following specific requirements shall be incorporated into all contracts with
contracts with conservation work performing work in the Capitol Building (CB) and Capitol
Visitor Center (CVC) that produce hazardous waste:
RESPONSIBILITES:


If the work will produce individual hazardous waste in a quantity less than 55 gallons (or
one drum), the CB jurisdiction will provide a location for storage of the hazardous waste
in the CB Hazardous Waste Accumulation Site (located outside of the Senate East
Carriage Entrance) and will arrange for disposal. The contractor shall be responsible for
(1) determining if wastes are hazardous (e.g., lead-containing) or regulated as PCB
wastes, and (2) the collection of the waste(s) and placement of the collected waste(s) into
the CB Hazardous Waste Accumulation Site. If the work area contains material(s) that
have not yet been tested for PCB, the contractor is responsible for the testing for the
presence of PCBs.



If the work includes disturbing paint containing lead greater than two square feet in total
area, the contractor shall submit a lead work plan that complies with attached document,
“Lead Work Requirements for Conservation and Restoration in the U.S. Capitol” and
meet with CB safety staff before beginning work.



All hazardous waste and PCB wastes shall be placed in UN approved containers and be
properly placed in the CB Hazardous Waste Accumulation Area through coordination
with the CB Hazardous Waste Accumulation Site Manager. Containers of hazardous
wastes must be (1) labeled with the appropriate preprinted labels for wastes which can be
found in the CB Hazardous Waste Accumulation Site, (2) marked with an accumulation
start date, and (3) labeled with words that identify the hazard of the waste. For example, if
waste consists only of lead-containing paint, it shall be labeled “waste lead paint”. If, in
addition to the lead hazard, the waste contains any solvent(s), each solvent must be listed
on the label. Rags containing ignitable solvents are considered hazardous waste and must
be handled as such. Different types of waste shall not be mixed. Hazardous waste can
only be accumulated on site for 90 days from the first day the container is put into use.
Containers holding PCB wastes shall be labeled with the appropriate label referenced in
Chapter 3 of the AOC Environmental Manual and shall be marked with an accumulation
start date.



Non-hazardous waste shall be collected and disposed of separately each day utilizing the
trash dumpster at the CVC loading dock.



Materials used that are known to be hazardous or flammable according to the OSHA
“List of Hazardous Chemicals, Toxics and Reactives” (e-CFR 1910.119 App A, 1910
Subpart H) may be stored in the Capitol, but shall be removed from the building at the
end of the shift. Storage of these materials can be arranged in coordination with the CB
Hazardous Waste Site Accumulation Manager, Contracting Officer’s Representative
(COR), or the CB Safety Manager. Any hazardous chemicals not in the original
container (only daily usage quantities) must be clearly labeled in accordance with the
OSHA Hazard Communication Standard – 29CFR 1910-1200.



No materials or waste shall be deposited in interior or exterior drains.



At the start of each work week, the contractor shall provide a list of any specific
hazardous chemicals that will be used that week, along with the associated SDS for each
item. The contractor must follow the SDS requirements for the handling, storage, and
disposal of these materials. In addition, a copy of each SDS must be organized in a
clearly marked binder and must be available at the work site. Work schedules and lists of
hazardous chemicals and their SDS should be sent by e-mail to Cori Wright (Capitol
Building Hazardous Waste Accumulation Site Manager) cwright@aoc.gov, Brent
Dittman (CB Safety Manager) brent.dittman@aoc.gov, Gerardo Figueroa (CB Safety
Manager) gfigueroa@aoc.gov, Michele Cohen (AOC Curator) mcohen@aoc.gov, and
Jennifer Blancato (AOC Museum Curator) jblancato@aoc.gov.



The contractor shall follow all federal, state, and local environmental and safety
regulations. (See 29 CFR 1910.120, 1910 Subpart Z, 1910.1200, 40 CFR 260-299, 40
CFR 761, and 20 DCMR, Chapters 40-54.) The COR is responsible for enforcement of
the contract requirements.



########## J.8 Lead Work Requirements for Conservation and Restoration in the U.S. Capitol - June 2019.pdf ##########

Lead Work Requirements for Conservation and Restoration
in the U.S. Capitol
The following safety requirements shall be incorporated into all projects involving disturbance or
removal of Lead or Lead-Based Paint.
The Conservator Shall:
Submit a lead work plan for approval that is in accordance with 29 CFR 1926.62 and
that explains in detail:
a.
How the work will be performed showing how the Conservator will comply
with laws, ordinances, rules and regulations of federal, state, and local
authorities regarding removing, handling and storing of lead paint and lead
waste materials.
b. A schedule for testing and monitoring for lead. Testing shall include pre and
post-wipe sampling. Clean up of lead dust in work area shall be to the pre-test
level. Sweeping or use of compressed air is not allowed for cleaning lead dust.
c.
How building occupants in the area surrounding the Conservation and
Restoration project will be protected from lead contamination.
d. How the conservator will collect and store all potential lead-contaminated
waste, including but not limited to, removed paint chips, architectural materials,
scrap, debris, bags, rags, containers, equipment and lead-contaminated clothing.
e.
How staff use Personal Protective Equipment (PPE) to keep lead contamination
within the work area.
Provide training certificates and certify that all workers have been trained to remove
lead based paint or lead containing material. Lead training must meet OSHA
standards and EPA requirements on the use of lead-safe practices (see
http://www.epa.gov/fedrgstr/EPA-TOX/2008/April/Day-22/t8141.htm).
Provide the name, address, and telephone number of the designated Competent
Person assigned to supervise the project. The Competent Person assigned to this
project shall be required to onsite and supervising any and all work being performed
inside the Lead Control Area.
Submit to the Superintendent Safety Office all Safety Data Sheets (SDS’s) for all
products being used on the project. Use the least toxic product suitable for the job
and acceptable to the AOC.
Fifteen (15) business days prior to the start of the Conservation and Restoration
project, notify all of the following:
- Capitol Building Point of Contact, Mr. Benjamin Roberts, telephone (202) 226.6477
- Capitol Building Safety Office, telephone (202) 226-5938 or 5939.
- Capitol Building Insulation Office, telephone (202) 593-1896 or 1897.
Comply with the District of Columbia’s lead law 11-221, “Lead-Based Paint
Abatement and Control Act of 1996, as amended.”

Date and Initial
when Complete
